POEMS: By Michaell Draiton Esquire.

N L



LONDON, Printed for N. Ling. 1605.

The Arguments.
THe Barrons wars. Englands Heroical Epistles. Idea. The Legend of Robert
Duke of Normandy. The Legend of Matilda. The Legend of Pierce Gaueston.
To Sir Walter Aston, Knight of the honorable order of the Bath, and my most
worthy Patron.
I Will not strive m'invention to enforce,
With needless words your eyes to entertain,
T'obserue the formal ordinary course
That every one so vulgarly does feign:
Our interchanged and deliberate choice,
Is with more firm and true election sorted,
Then stands in censure of the common voice,
That with light humor fondly is transported:
Nor take I pattern of an others praise,
Then what my pen may constantly avow,
Nor walk more public nor obscurer ways
Then virtue bids, and judgment will allow;
So shall my love, and best endeavors serve you,
And still shall study, still so to deserve you.
Michaell Drayton.

To the Reader.
THat at first I made choice of this argument, I have not as yet repented me
for if the Muse have not much abvsed me, it was most worthy to have found a
more worthy Pen then mine own; for the Barrons wars, (omitting the quality
of those Arms, whereof I have not here to speak) were surely as well for
their length in continuance, as for their manifold bloodshed, and multitude of
horrid accidents, meet matter for trumpet or tragedy. Therefore, as at first
the dignity of the thing was the motive of the doing, so the cause of this my
second greater labor was the insufficient handkung of the first, which though
it were more then boldness to venture on so noble an argument without leisure,
and study co^petent, either of which travel hardly affoords; yet the
importunity of friends made me, contrary to mine own judgment, take,
vndertakem and publish it, so as the world has seen; but herein I intend not
to be too exact, as if either it needed too much excuse (knowing, that even as
it was it ought to have passed for better then some would suffer, who can
hardly think any thing has savor but their own, though never so unsavory)
or as if I should seem now to have excelled my self, and failing in my hopes,
be kept without excuse. Grammaticasters have quarreled at the title of
Mortimeriados, as if it had been a sin against Syntaxis to have subscribed
it in the second case, but not their idle reproof has made me now abstain
from fronting it by the name of Mortimer at all, but the same better advise
which has caused me to alter the whole; and where before the stanza was of
seven lines, wherein there are two couplets, as in this figure appears. the
often harmony thereof softened the verse more then the majesty of the subject
would permit, unless they had all bin Geminels, or couplets. Therefore, (but
not without fashioning the whole frame) I chose Ariostos stanza, of all other
the most complete and best proportioned, consisting of eight, six interwouen,
and a couplet in base.



The Quadrin does never double, or to v^e a word of He raldrie, never brings
forth Gemells. The Quiazain too soon. The Sostin has Twins in the base, but
they detain not the Music nor the Cloze (as Musicians term it) long enough
for an Epicke Poem; The stanza of seven is touched before; This of eight both
holds the tune clean through to the base of the column, (which is the
couplet, the foot or bottom) and closes not but with a full satisfaction to
the care, for so long detention.

Briefly, this sort of stanza has in it, majesty, perfection and soliditie,
resembling the pillar which in Architecture is called the Tuscan, whose shaft
is of six diameters, and bases of two. The other reasons this place will not
bear, but generally all stanzas are in my opinion but tyrants and torturers,
when they make invention they their number, which sometime would otherwise
scantle it self. A fault that great Masters in this Art strive to avoid.
Concerning the division which I use in this Poem, I am not ignorant that
antiquity has used to distinguish works into Books, and every one to bear
the number of their order, Homers ^liads, and Vlysiads indeed are
distinguished by several letters of the Greek Alphabet, as all the world
kn^wes, and not by the numerall letters only, which to lot^ are digit, and
afterward compound; the Alpha being our unite, for the Greeks had no figures
nor ciphers in their Arithmeticke. Virgils Aeneis, Statius Theba^s, Silius
work of the Carthaginian war, Illyricus Argonauticks, Vidas Christeis, are
all divided into books. The Italians use Cantos, and so our first late great
Reformer Master Spenser; that I assume another name for the sections in this
volume, cannot be disgratious nor vnauowable.

Lastly, if I have not already exceeded the length of an Epistle, I am to
entreats, that he who will (as any man may that will) make himself a party to
this of ours, would be pleased to remember that Spartan Prince, who being found
by certain Ambassadors playing among his children, requested them to forbear
to censure, till also they had some of their own. To such I give as ample
power and privilege as ever Ius lib^rorum coulain Rome, craving back again
at their hands by a regrant, the like of that which I impart; for great reason
there is, that they should undergo the license which themselves challenge, and
suffer that in their fames which they would wrongly put upon others, according
to the most indifferent law of the Talio. Fare you well.

To M. Michaell Drayton.
WHat ornament might I devise to fit
The Aspiring height of thy admired spirit?
Or what fair Garland worthy is to sit
On thy blessed brows, that compass in all merit?
Thou shall not crowned be with common Bays,
Because for thee it is a crown too low,
Apolloes tree can yield thee simple praise,
It is too dull a vesture for thy brow;
But with a wreathe of stars shall thou be crowned,
Which when thy working temples do sustain,
Will like the Spheres be ever moving round,
After the royal music of thy brain:
Thy skill does equal Phoebus, not thy birth,
He to heaven gives music, thou to earth.
Thomas Green.

To M. Michaell Drayton.
THose painful wits, which natures depth admire,
And view the causes of unconstant strife,
Do tremble least the Universe expire,
Through lasting iarres, the enemies of life,
On earthly signs let not such Sages look,
Nor on the clear aspects of hopeful stars;
But learn the worlds continuance from thy book,
which frames past natures force eternal wars;
wherein the Mases showing perfect glory,
Adorn it so with graceful harmony,
That all the acts of this lamented story,
Seem not performed for peoples liberty:
Nor through the awe of an imperious King,
But that thy verses their deep wounds might sing.
John Beaumont.

THE FIRST BOOK of the Barrons wars.
The Argument.
The grievous plagues and the prodigious signs
That this great war and slaughter do foreshow,
Th'especiall cause the Baronage combines,
The Queens strong grief, whence many troubles grow,
The time by course unto our fallinclines,
And how each country does to battle go;
What cause to yield, the Mortimers pretend,
And their commitment perfecting the end.
1
THe bloodiefactions, and rebellious pride
Of a strong nation, whose vnmanag'd might
Them from their natural Sovereign did divide,
Their due subjection, and his lawful right,
Whom their light error loosely does misguide,
Urged by lewd Minions tyrannous despite;
Me from soft lays, and tender loves does bring,
Of dreadful fights, and horred wars to sing.
2
What hellish fury poysned your hie blood,
Or should bewitch you with accursed charms,
That by pretending of the general good,
Rashly extrudes you to tumultuous arms,
And from the safety wherein late you stood,
Re^t of all taste, and feeling of your harms,
That France and Belgia with affrighted eyes,
Were sad beholders of your miseries.
3
T(...)ueterate ranckor in their bosoms bred,
Who for their charter waged a former war,
Or through your veins, this raging venom spread,
Whose next-succeeding Nephews now you are,
Or that hot gore your bows in conquest shed,
Having enlarged your Countries bounds so far,
Ensign to ensign furiously oppose,
With blades of Bilbo dealing English blows.
4
O thou the great director of my Muse,
On whose free bounty all my powers depend,
Into my breast a sacred fire infuse,
Ravish my spirit this great work to attend;
Let the still night my labored lines peruse,
That when my Poems gain their wished end,
They whose sad eyes shall read this tragique story,
In my weak hand shall see thy might and glory.
5
What Care would plot, Dissentions quickly cross,
Which like an earthquake rends the tottering state,
By which abroad we bear a public loss,
Betrayed at home by means of private hate;
Whilst us those strange calamities do toss,
(The daily nurse of mutinous debate)
Confusion still our countries peace confounds,
No help at hand, and mortal all our wounds.
6
Thou Church then swelling in thy mightiness,
Tending the care and safety of the soul;
O nurse not factions flowing in excess,
That with thy members should their grief condole,
In thee rests power this outrage to repress,
Which might thy zeal and sanctity enrole,
Come thou in pureness meekely with the word,
Lay not thy hand to the unhallowed sword.
7
Blood-thirsting war arising first from hell,
And in progression seizing on this I Will,
Where it before near forty years did dwell,
And with pollution horribly defile,
By which so many a worthy English fell,
By our first Edward banished awhile,
Transferd by Fortune to the Scottish meare,
To ransacke that, as it had rauinde here.
8
Where hovering still with inauspicious wings
About the verge of these distempered climes,
Returning now, new error hither brings,
To stir us up to these disastrous crimes,
Weakeneth our power by often diminishings;
And taking hold on these unsettled times,
Forcing our frailty sensually at length,
Cracked the stiff nerves that knit our ancient strength.
9
Whose frightfull vision, at the first approach,
With violent madness struck that desperate age,
So many sundry miseries abroach,
Giving full speed to their unbridled rage
That did our ancient liberty encroach,
And in these strong conspiracies engage,
The worthiest blood the subjects loss to bring,
By innaturall wrongs unto their natural king.
10
When in the North whilst horror yet was young,
These dangerous seasons swiftly coming on,
Whilst over our heads portentious meteors hung,
And in the skies stern Comets brightly shone,
Prodigious births often intermixed among,
Such as before to times had been unknown,
In bloody issues forth the earth does break,
Weeping for us, whose woes it could not speak.
11
When by the rankenes of contagious air,
A mortal plague inuadeth man and beast,
Which soon dispersed, and raging every where,
In doubt the same too quickly should have ceast,
More to confirm the certainty of fear
By cruel famine haplesly enereast;
As though the heavens in their remisfull doom,
Took those best loved from wor^er days to come.
12
The level course that we propose to go
Now to the intent you may more plainly see,
And that we every circumstance may show
The state of things, and truly what they be,
And with what skill, or project we bestow,
As our accurrents happen in degree.
From these portents we now divert our view,
To bring to birth the horrors that ensue.
13
The calling back of banished Gaueston,
Against which the Barrons were to Longshanks sworn,
That insolent lascivious Minion,
A Sovereigns blemish, and a countries scorn,
The signiories, and great promotion,
Him in his lawless courses to suborne,
Stirs up that hateful and outrageous strife,
That cost ere long so many an English life,
14
O worthy La^y, had thou sparde that breath
Which shortly after Nature thee denide,
To Lancaster delivered at thy death,
To whom thy only daughter was affide,
That this stern war too quickly publishes,
To aid the Barrons against that Minions pride,
Thy Earledomes, lands, and titles of renown,
Had not so soon returned unto the Crown.
15
The Lordships Bruise unto the Spensers past,
Crossing the Barrons vehement desire,
As from Jove's hand that fearful lightning cast,
When fifty towns lay spent in envious fire,
Alas too vain and prodigal a waste;
The strong effect of their conceived ire,
Urging the weak King with a violent hand,
T'abiure those false Lords from the troubled land.
16
When the fair Queen that progressing in Kent,
Lastly denide her entrance into Leedes,
Whom Badlesmere unkindly does prevent;
Who against his Sovereign in this course proceeds,
As adding further to this discontent,
One of the springs which this great mischief feeds,
Heaping on rage and horror more and more,
To thrust on that which went too fast before.
17
Which more and more, a kingly rage increast,
Moved with the wrongs of Gaueston disgraded,
Which had so long been settled in his breast,
That all his powers it wholly had invaded,
Giving the Spensers an assured rest,
By whom his reasons chiefly are persuaded,
By whose lewd counsels he is only led,
To leave his true Queen, and his lawful bed,
18
That now herself who while she stood in grace,
Applied her powers these discords to appease
When yet confusion had not fully place,
Nor former times so dangerous as these,
A party now in their afflicted case,
A willing hand to his destruction lays,
That time whose soft palm heals the wound of war,
May cure the soar, but never close the scar.
19
In all this heat his greatness first began,
The serious subject of our sadder vain,
Brave Mortimer, that euer-matchlesse man,
Of the old Heroes great and God-like strain,
For whom invention doing best it can,
His weight of honor hardly can sustain,
Bearing his name immortalized and hie,
When he in earth unnumbered times shall lie.
20
That uncle now (whose name this Nephew bare,
The only comfort of the woeful Queen)
Who from his cradle held him as his care,
In whom the hope of that great name was seen,
For this young Lord now wisely does prepare,
Whilst yet this deep hart-goaring wound is green,
And on this fair advantage firmly wrought,
To place him highly in her princely thought.
21
At whose deliberate and unusual birth,
The heavens were said to counsel to retire,
And in aspects of happiness and mirth,
Breathed him a spirit insatiatly t'aspire,
That took no mixture of the ponderous earth,
But all comprest of clear ascending fire,
So well made up, that such an one as he,
Jove in a man like Mortimer would be.
22
The temper of that nobler moving part,
With such rare pureness rectified his blood,
Raising the powers of his resolved heart,
Too proud to be locked up within a flood,
That no misfortune possibly could thwart;
Which from the native greatness where it stood,
Even by the virtue of a piercing eye,
Showed that his pitch was boundless as the sky.
23
Worthy the grandchild of so great a Lord,
Who whilst first Edward fortunately reigned,
Reedified great Arthurs ancient board,
The seat at goodly Kennelworth ordained,
The order of old Knighthood there restored,
To which a hundredth duly appertained
With all the grace, and beauties of a Court,
As best became that brave and martial sport.
24
The hart-swolne Lords with fury set on fire,
Whom Edwards wrongs to vengeance still provoke,
With Lancaster and Hartfoord now conspire
No more to bear the Spensers servile yoke.
And thus whilst all a mutual change desire,
The ancient bonds of their allegiance broke,
Resolved with blood their liberty to buy,
And in this quarrel vowed to live and dye.
25
What privilege has our free birth? (say they)
Or in our blood what virtue does remain,
To each lascivious Minion made a pray,
That us, and our nobility disdain,
Whilst they tryumphing boast of our decay?
Either those spirits we do not now retain
That were our fathers, or by fate we fall
Both from their greatness, liberty and all.
26
Honor dejected from that sovereign state,
From whence at first it challenged a being,
Now prostitute to infamy and hate,
As with it self in all things disagreeing,
So out of order, disproportionate,
From her fair course preposterously flying,
Whilst others as themselves, and only we
Are not held those we would but seem to be.
27
Then to what end has our great conquest served,
Those acts achiued by the Norman sword,
Our Charters, patents, or our deeds reserved,
Our offices, and tytles to record,
The crests that on our monuments are carved,
If they to us no greater good afford?
Thus do they murmr every one apart,
With many a vexed soul, many a grieved heart.
28
Whilst this sad Queen to depth of sorrow thrown,
Wherein she wastes her flower of youth away,
Beyond belief to all but heaven unknown,
This quickening spark, where yet it buried lay,
By the sharp breath of desperate faction blown,
Conuerts her long night to the wished day,
The woeful winter of misfortune cheering,
As the dark world at the bright suns appearing.
29
Yet ill perplexed amid these hard extremes,
All mean^s deprest her safety to prefer,
Deprived of those late comfortable beams,
Whose want might make her the more easly err,
Her hopes relinquisht like deceiptfull dreams,
Which in her breast such sundry passions stir,
Where struggling which each other should control
Work strange confusion in her troubled soul.
30
That now disabled of all sovereign state,
That to her graces rightly did belong,
To be rejected, and repudiate,
So true a Lady, goodly, fair and young,
Which with more feruor still does intimate
Her too-deepe-setled and inveterate wrong,
What wisdom would, a woman's will denies,
With arguments of her indignities.
31
When to effect the angry Fates pursue
In heavens high Court that long time did depend,
When these full mischiefs to a ripeness grew,
And now the harvest hastening in the end,
And all these lines into one center drew,
Which way soever they seemingly extend;
All these together in proportion laid,
Each breath of hope, a gale of certain aid.
32
Now is the time when Mortimer does enter,
Of great employment in this tragic act,
His youth and courage boldly bid him venture,
And tell him still how strongly he was backed;
And at this instant in due season sent her,
When the straight course to her desire is tract,
(And but upon more certainty does stay)
By a direct, what though a dangerous way?
33
This dreadful Commet drew her wondering eye,
Which now began his golden head to rear,
Whose glorious fixure in so fair a sky,
Strikes the beholder with a chilly fear,
And in a Region eleuate and hie,
And by the form wherein it did appear,
As the most skillful seriously divine,
Foreshew'd a kingdom shortly to decline.
34
Yet still recoyling at the Spencers power,
As often checked with their intemperate pride,
Th'vnconstant Barrons wavering every hour,
The fierce encounter of this boisterous tide,
That easily might their liuelihoode devour,
Had she not those that skilfully could guide,
She from suspicion craftily retires,
Careless in show, of what she most desires.
35
Dissembling grief, as one that knew not ill;
So can she rule the greatness of her mind,
As a most perfect Rectoresse of her will,
Above the usual weakness of her kind;
For all this storm immooueable and still,
Her secret drift the wisest miss to find;
Nor will she know what (yet) these factions meant,
With a pleased eye to sooth sad Discontent.
36
The least suspicion cunningly to heal,
Still in her looks humility she bears,
The safest way with mightiness to deal,
So Policy Religions habit wears;
It Is now no time her greeuance to reveal,
He Is mad which takes a Lion by the ears,
This knew the Queen, exampled by the wise,
This must they learn that rightly temporize.
37
The learned Bishop Torleton in the land,
Upon a text of Politickes to preach,
Which he long studying, well did understand,
And by a method could as aptly teach,
That was a Prelate of a potent hand,
Wise were the man could go beyond his reach:
This subtle Tutor Isabell had taught,
In nicer points than ever Edward sought.
38
Rage which no limits longer can contain,
Lastly breaks forth into a public flame,
Then slipped occasion better to regaine,
When to their purpose things sofitly frame,
And now discerned visibly and plain,
When treason boldly dare it self proclaim,
Casting aside all secular disguise,
Leading proud legions furiously to rise.
39
As Severne lately in her ebbes that sanke,
Vast and forsaken leaves th'vncouered sands,
Fetching full tides, luxurious, hie, and rank,
Seems in her pride t'inuade the neighboring lands,
Breaking her limits, covering all the banks,
Threatening the proud hills with her watery hands,
As though she meant her Empery to have,
Where even but lately she beheld her grave.
40
Through all the land, from places far and near,
Led to the field as Fortune lots their side,
(With th'auntient weapons used in war to bear)
As those directed whom they chose their guide,
Or else perhaps as they affected were,
Or as by friendship, or by duty tide,
Swayde by the strength and motion of their blood,
No cause examined, be it bad or good.
41
From Norfolk, and the countries of the East,
That with the long pyke best could manage fight,
The men of Kent unconquered of the rest,
That to this day maintain their ancient right,
And for their strength that we account the best,
The Cornishmen, most active, bold, and light,
Those near the plain that gleaue and polax weeld,
And claim for due vaward of the field.
42
The noble Britton sprung of Illyon race,
From Lancashiere most famous for their bows,
With those of Cheshiere, chiefest for their place,
Men of such bone, as only made for blows,
That for their faith are had in special grace,
And as the guard unto the Sovereign goes;
Those of the North in feuds so deadly fell,
That for their spear and horsemanship excel.
43
For every use experience could espy
Such as in Fens and Marsh-lands use to trade,
The doubtful foards and passages to try,
With stilts and loapstaues that do aptliest wade,
And fit'st for scowts and Currers to discry,
Those from the Mines with pickaxe, and with spade,
For Pyoners best, that for intrenching are,
Men chiefly needful in the use of war.
44
O noble Nation furnished with Arms,
So full of spirit, so eminent alone,
Had heaven but blessed thee to foresee these harms,
And as thy valiant Nephews to have gone,
Paris, ^oan, Orleance shaking with alarmes,
As the bright Sun thy glory then had shone;
To other Realms thou had transferd this chance,
Nor had your sons been first that conquered France.
45
And thus on all hands making for their rest,
And now set forward for this mighty day,
Where every one prepares to do his best,
When in success their lives and fortunes lay;
No cross event their purposes to wrest,
Where now they stand in so direct a way;
And whilst they play this strange and doubtful game,
The Queen stands by, and only gives the aim.
46
When this brave Lord his foot had scarcely set
Into the road where Fortune had to deal,
But she disposd his forward course to let,
Her lewd condition quickly does reveal,
Glory to her vain deity to get,
By him, whose birth did bear her omenous seal,
Winning occasion from this very hour,
In him to prove and manifest her power.
47
As when we see the early rising Sun,
With his fair beams to emulate our sight,
And when his course but newly is begun,
The humorous fogges deprive his wished light,
Till through the moist clouds his clear forehead run,
Climing the noonsted in his gorgeous height:
His bright beginning fortune hinders thus,
To make the rest more rich, more glorious.
48
The King discreetely that considered,
The space of earth whereon the Barrons stand,
What were their powers to them contributed,
Now being himself but partner of his land,
And of the strength and army that heeded,
Against them that do so great a power command,
In which it was well he did so wisely look,
The task was great that now he undertook.
49
And warned by danger to mis-doubt the worst,
In equal scales whilst eithers fortune hung,
Must now perform the utmost that he durst,
Or undergo the burden of his wrong;
As good to stir, as after be inforst
To stop the head whence many evils sprung:
Now with the marchers thinks it best begin,
Which first must loose, ere he could hope to win.
50
The Mortimers being men of greatest might,
Whose name was dreadful, and commanded far,
Sturdie to manage, of a haughty spright,
Strongly aly'd much followed, popular,
On whom if thus he happily could light,
He hopes more easly to conclude this war;
Which he intends speedily to try,
To quit that first, which most stood in his eye.
51
For which he expeditiously provided
That part of land into his power to get,
Which if made good, might keep them still divided,
Their combination cunningly to let,
Who being couioyn'd, would be too strongly sided,
Two, so great strengths together safely met,
The face of war would look so stern and great,
As well might threat to heave him from his seat.
52
Wherefore from London strongly setting forth,
With a fair Army furnished of the best,
Accompanied with friends of greatest worth,
With whom there is many a gallant spirit is pressed;
Great Lancaster, the Lord of all the North,
The Mortimers are Masters of the West,
He toward mid England makes, the way twixt either,
Which they must cross, ere they could com together.
53
And thus inueagled with delightful hope,
Stowtly to front and shoulder with debate,
Knowing to meet with a resolved troop
That came prepared with courage, and with hate,
Whose stubborn Crests if he enforced to stoop,
He now must tempt some great and powrefull fate;
And through stern guards of swords and i^efull flame,
Make way to peace, and propagate his name.
54
When now the Marchers well upon their way,
(Expecting such should promised succor bring
Which all this while abused them by delay)
Are suddenly encountered by a King,
And now perceive their dilatory stay
To be the causer of their ruining,
How near their bosoms black destruction stood,
With open jaws prepared for their blood.
55
And by the shifting of inconstant wind,
Seeing what weather they were like to meet,
Which (even) at first so aukwardly they find
Ere they could yet give sea-roomth to their fleet,
Clean from their course, and cast so far behind,
And yet in perrill every hour to split,
Some unknown harbor suddenly must sound,
Or run their fortunes desprately on ground.
56
The elder peer, grave, politic, and wise,
Which had all dangers absolutely scand,
Finding high time his Nephew to advise,
Since now their state stood on this desperate hand,
And from this mischief many more to rise,
With long experience learned to understand:
Nephew (says he) it is longer vain to strive,
Counsel best serves our safety to contriue.
57
The downright peril present in our eye
Not to be shund, what certain end t'assures;
The next, the weight that on our fall does lie,
And what our life to our design procures,
Each hope, and doubt that does arise thereby,
Proving with judgment how the same endures:
For who observes strict policies true laws,
Shifts his proceeding to the varying cause.
58
To hazard fight with the Imperial powers,
May our small troops undoubtedly appall,
A desperate end us willingly deuours;
Yielding our selves, by this we loose not all,
We leave our friends this litttle force of ours,
Reserved for them, though haplesly we fall;
That show of weakness has a glorious hand,
That falls it self, to make the cause to stand.
59
Twixt inexpected and so dangerous ills,
That Is best wherein we smallest perrill see,
A course that reason necessary wills,
And that does most with policy agree,
The idle vulgar breath it nothing skils,
It Is sound discretion must our Pylot be,
He that does still the fairest mean prefer,
Answers opinion how so ere he err.
60
And to the worl^ ^s eye seeming yet so strong,
By our discending willingly from thence,
May urge the show of our opposed wrong
Rather b'inforcement then forethought pretense,
Leaving the advantage does to us belong,
May qualify the nature of the offense,
Men are not always incident to loss,
When Fortune seems their forward cause to cross.
61
Nor give we envy absolute access,
To lay our fall upon thy forward mind,
There Is nearer means this mischief to redress,
And make succesfull what is yet behind,
Nor of our hope us wholly dispossess,
Fortune is ever variously inclind,
And a small vantage to the course of Kings,
Guides a slight means to compass mighty things.
62
Which speech so caught his Nephews pliant youth,
Fastening upon a dutiful respect,
Which he with such celeritie pursu'th,
(Well could he counsel, well could he direct)
Proceeding from integrity and truth,
And working with such prosperous effect,
Shows wisemens counsels, by a powerful fate,
(Seeming from reason) yet prove fortunate.
63
To which they awful Majesty inuit
By the most due and ceremonious way,
With circumstance, and each conditi'nall rite
Might win respect unto this new essay,
Or might opinion any way excite,
To which the King does willingly obey;
Who as themselves in finding danger near,
Rather accepts doubt, then a certain fear.
64
Which he receaues in presage of his good,
To his success auspiciously applied,
which cooles the heat of his distemperd blood,
Before their force in doubtful Arms was tried,
In his protection when they only stood,
At his disposing wholly to abide,
vvhereon in safety he dismissed their power,
Sends them away as prisoners to the Tower.
65
O all-preparing providence Divine,
In thy large Book what secrets are enrold?
What sundry helps does thy great power assign,
To stay the course thou steadfastly do hold?
What mortal sense is able to define
Thy mysteries, thy counsels manifold;
By these digressions strangely that extends
Thy obscure proceedings to aparent ends?
66
This was the mean, by which the Fates dispose
More threatened plagues upon that age to bring,
Utter confusion on the heads of those
That were before the Barrons ruining,
With the subversion of so many foes,
The murder of the miserable King;
And that which came as Epilogue to all,
Lastly, his fearful, and so violent fall.
67
Which to their hope gives time for further breath,
As the first pawse in this their great affair,
That yet awhile deferred this threatening death,
Trusting this breach by leisure to repair,
And here a while this fury limetteth:
Whilst in this manner things so strangely fare,
Horror beyond the wonted bounds does swell,
As the next Canto dreadfully shall tell.
The end of the first Canto.
The second Book of the Barrons wars.
The Argument.
At Burton-bridge the puissant armies me^,
The form and order of the doubtful fight,
Whereas the King the victory does get,
And the proud Barrons lastly forcde to flight;
How they again toward Borough forward set,
Where then the Lords are vanquished outright,
Lastly, the laws do execute their power,
On those the sword before did not devour.
1
THis chance of war, that dreadfully had swept
So large a share from their full-reckned might,
Which their proud hopes so carefully had kept
Whilst yet their state stood equally upright,
That could at first so closely intercept,
That should have served them for a glorious fight,
Musters supplies of footmen, and of horse,
To give a new strength to their ruinde force.
2
Th'inueterate grief so deep and firmly rooted,
Yet slightly curde by this short strengthelesse peace;
To assay t'remoue, since it but vainly booted,
That did with each distemprature increase,
And being by every offered cause promooted,
The Effect too firmly settled to surcease:
When each evasion sunday passions brought,
Strange forms of fear in every troubled thought.
3
And put in action for this public cause,
Whilst every one a party firmly stood,
Taxt by the letter of the censuring laws,
In the sharp tainder of his honored blood;
And he that's free'st, entangled by some clause,
Which to this mischief gives continual food;
For where confusion gets so strongly hold,
Till all consumde, can hardly be controlde.
4
Where now by night, even when pale leaden sleep,
Upon their eie-lids heavily did dwell,
And, step by step, on every sense did creep,
Mischief (that black inhabitant of hell)
Which never fails continual watch to keep,
Fearful to think, a horred thing to tell,
Entered the place where now these warlike Lords
Lay maild in armor, girt with irefull swords.
5
Mischief with sharp sight, and a meager look,
And always prying where she may do ill,
In which the fiend continual pleasure took,
Her starued body Plenty could not fill,
Searching in every corner, every nooke,
With winged feet, too swift to work her will;
Hung full of deadly instruments she went,
Of every sort to hurt where ere she meant,
6
And with a vial filled with banefull wrath,
Brought from Cocytus by this cursed spright,
Which in her black hand readily she has,
And drops the poison upon every wight;
For to each one she knew the ready path:
Now in the midst and dead-time of the night,
Whose envious force inuadeth every Peer,
Striking with fury, and impulsive fear.
7
The weeping morning breaking in the East,
When with a troubled and affrighted mind,
Each whom this venom lately did infest,
The strong effect soon inwardly do find;
And lately troubled by unquiet rest,
To sad destruction every one inclind;
Rumors of spoil through every ear does fly,
And fury sits in every threatening eye.
8
This done, in haste unto King Edward hies,
Which now grown proud upon his fair success,
The time in feasts and wantonness implies
With crowned cups his sorrows to redress,
That on his fortune wholly now relies,
And in the bosom of his Courtly press
Vaunting the glory of this late won day,
Whilst the sick Land with sorrow pines away.
9
Thither she comes, and in a Minions shape
She creeps near the person of the King,
Warmed with the verdure of the swelling grape,
In which, she poison secretly does wring,
Not the least drop vntaynted does escape,
To which intent she all her store did bring,
Whose rich commixtu^e making it more strong,
Fills his hot veins with arrogance and wrong.
10
And having both such courage, and such might
As to so great a business did belong,
Never considering their pretended right
Should be inducement to a trebled wrong,
When misty error so deludes their sight,
Which still betwixt them, and clear reason hong;
By which opinion falsely was abusde,
As left all out of order, all confusde.
11
Now our Minerua tells of dreadful Arms,
Enforced to sing of worse then civil wars,
Of Ambuscades, stratagems, alarmes,
Unkind discentions, fearful massacars,
Of gloomy magiques, and benumming charms,
Fresh-bleeding wounds, and neuer-healed skarrs,
And for the sock wherein she used to tread,
Marching in greaues, a helmet on her head.
12
Whilst hate, and grief, their weakened sense delude,
The Barrons draw their forces to a head,
(Whom Edward spurred with vengeance still pursude)
By Lancaster, and noble Herford led,
This long proceeding lastly to conclude;
Whilst now to meet, both Armies freshly sped
To Burton, both incamping for the day,
With expectation for a glorious pray.
13
Upon the East, from Needwoods bushy side,
There rises up an easy clyming hill,
At whose fair foot the silver Trent does glide,
With a deep murmr permanent and still,
With liberal stor^ of many Brooks supplide,
Th'^n^atiate Meades continually do fill,
Upon whose stream, a bridge of wondrous strength
Does stretch it self in forty arches length.
14
Upon this Mount the Kings pauillion fixed,
And in the town, the foe entrenched in sight,
When now the flood is risen so betwixt,
That yet a while prolonged th'unturall fight,
With tributarie waters intermixed,
To stay the fury doing all it might,
Things which presage both good and ill there be,
Which heaven fore-shewes, but mortals cannot see.
15
The heaven even mourning over our heads does sit,
As grieved to see the time so out of course,
Looking on them who never look at it,
And in mere pity melting with remorce,
Longer from tears that cannot stay a whit,
Whose confluence on every lower sourse,
From the swollen fluxure of the clouds does shake
A rank Impostume upon every lake.
16
O warlike Nation! hold thy conquering hand,
Even senseless things admonish thee to pawse,
That Mother soil on whom thou yet do stand,
That would restrain thee by all natural laws.
Can thou (unkind) inuiolate that band,
When even the earth is angry with the cause?
Yet stay thy foot in mischiefs ugly gate,
Ill comes too soon, repentance still too late.
17
And can the clouds weep over thy decay,
And not one drop fall from thy droughtie eyes?
See thou the snare, and wilt not shun the way,
Nor yet be warned by passed miseries?
It Is yet but early in this fatal day;
Let late experience learn thee to be wise,
Mischief foreseen, may easly be prevented,
But hap'd, vnhelp'd, though nere enough lamented.
18
Cannot the Scot of your late slaughter boast?
And are you yet scarce healed of the sore?
Is It not enough you have already lost,
But your own madness needsly make it more?
Will you seek safety in a foreign Coast?
Your wives and children pitied you before;
But when your own blood your own swords imbrue,
Who pitties them which once did pity you?
19
The neighboring groves dispoiled of their trees,
For boats, and timber to assay this flood,
Where men are laboring as the Summer be,
Some hollowing truncks, some binding heaps of wood,
Some on their breasts, some working on their knees,
To win the bancke whereon the Barrons stood,
Which over this current they by strength must tew,
To shed that blood that many ages rue.
20
Some sharpen swords, some on their Murrians set
The Greaues, and pouldrons others riuet fast,
The archers now their bearded arrows whet,
Whilst every where the clamerous Drums are bra'st,
Some taking view where surest ground to get,
And every one advantage does forecast;
In ranks and fyles each plain and meadow swarms,
As though the land were clad in angry Arms.
21
The crests and honors of the English name,
Against their own opposed rudely stand,
As angry with th'atchieuements whence they came,
That to their virtues gave the generous brand;
O you unworthy of your ancient fame,
Against your selves to lift your conquering hand!
Since foreign swords your height could not abate,
By your own power your selves to ruinate.
22
Upon his surcoate valiant Neuell bore
A silver saltoyre graced on martial red;
A Ladies sleeue hie-spirited Hastings wore,
Ferrer his Taberd with rich verry spread,
Well known in many a warlike match before;
A Raven sat on Corbets warlike head,
Cou'ring his Helmet; Culpepper inrayld,
On maiden Arms, a bloody bend engrayld,
23
The noble Percy in this furious day,
With a bright Cressant in his guide-home came,
In his fair Cornet Verdoon does display
A Geuly fret, prized in this mortal game
That had been taskt in many a doubtful fray,
His lances pennons stained with the same;
The angry horse chafde with the stubborn bit,
The ruinous earth with rage and horror smit.
24
I could the sum of Staffords arming show,
What colors Courtney, Rosse and Warren hold,
Each sundry blazon I could let you know,
And all the glorious circumstance have told,
What all the Ensigns standing in arow,
But wailing in a Muse, (ah me) thou art controlde,
When in remembrance of this horred deed,
My pen for ink even drops of blood does sheede.
25
The Imperial standard in this place is pitched,
With all the hatchments of the English crown,
Great Lancaster with all his power enricht,
Sets the same Leopards in his Colors down;
O if with fury you be not bewitched,
Have but remembrance, on your self you frown,
A little note, or difference is in all,
How can the same stand, when the same does fall?
26
Behold the Eagles, Lions, Talbots, Bears,
The badges of your famous ancestries,
And shall they now by their inglorious heirs,
Stand thus opposed against their families?
More honored marks no Christian nation wears,
Relics unworthy of their progenies;
Those beasts you bear, do in their kinds agree.
O that than beasts more savage men should be!
27
But whilst the king no course concluded yet,
In his directions variably does houer,
See how misfortunes still her time can fit,
Such as were sent the Country to discover;
As up and down, from place to place they flit,
Had found a foord to land their forces over:
Ill news has wings, and with the wind does go,
Comfort's a Cripple, and comes ever slow.
28
When Edward fearing Lancasters supplies,
Proud Richmond, Surry and great Penbrooke sent,
On whose success his chiefest hope relies;
Under whose conduct half his Army went,
The nearest way, conducted by thespies;
And he himself, and Edmond Earl of Kent,
Upon the hill, in sight of Burton lay,
Watching to take advantage of the day.
29
Stay, Surry stay, thou mai'st too soon be gone,
Pawse till this rage be somewhat over past,
Why runn'st thou thus to thy destruction?
Richmond and Penbrooke, whither do you haste?
You labor still to bring more horror on;
Never seek sorrow, for it comes too fast:
Why do you strive to pass this fatal flood,
To fetch new wounds, and shed your native blood?
30
Great Lancaster, sheath up thy angry sword,
On Edwards arms whose edge thou should not whet,
Thy natural kinsman, and thy sovereign Lord;
Are you not one, both true Plantaginet?
Call but to mind thy once-engaged word,
Can thou thy oath to Longshankes thus forget?
Consider well, before all other things
Our vows be kept we make to Gods and Kings.
31
The winds are hushed no little breath does blow,
Which seems so still as though it listening stood,
With trampling crowds the very earth does bow,
And through the smoke the sun appears like blood;
What with the shout, and with the dreadful show,
The heirds and flocks run bellowing to the wood.
When drums and trumpets give the fearful sound,
As they would shake the clouds unto the ground.
32
The Earls then charging with their power of horse,
Taking a signal when they should begin,
Being in view of the imperial force,
Which at the time assayed the bridge to win;
That now the Barrons change th'intended course,
T'auoide the danger they were lately in;
Which on the sudden had they not forecast,
Of their black day this hour had been the last.
33
When from the hill the Kings maine power comes down,
Which had Aquarius to their valiant guide,
Brave Lancaster and Herford from the town
Now issue forth upon the other side,
Peer against peer, the crown against the crown,
The one assailes, the other munifide,
Englands red cross upon both sides does fly,
Saint George the king, S. George the Barrons cry.
34
Like as an exhalation hot and dry,
Amongst the ayre-bred moistie vapors thrown,
Spetteth his lightning forth outragiously,
Rending the gross clouds with the thunder-stone,
Whose fiery splinters through the thin air fly,
That with the terror heaven and earth does groan;
With the like clamor and confused woe,
To the dread shocke these desperate Armies go.
35
Now might you see the famous English Bows
So fortunate in times we did subdue,
Shoot their sharp arrows in the face of those
which many a time victoriously them drew,
Shunning their aim, as troubled in the loose;
The winged weapons mourning as they slew.
Cleave to the string, (now in potent and slack)
As to the Archers they would feign turn back.
36
Behold theremnant of Troyes ancient stock,
Laying on blows, as Smiths on Anuils strike,
Grapling together in this fearful shocke,
whereas the like incountreth with the like,
As firm and ruthlesse as th'obdurate Rock,
Deadly opposed at the push of pike;
Still as the wings, or battles brought together,
when Fortune yet gives vantage unto neither.
37
From battred caskes with every envious blow,
The scattred plumes fly loosly here and there,
which in the air does seem as drifts of snow,
which every light breath on his wings does bear,
As they had sense and feeling of our woe;
And thus affrighted with the present fear,
Now back, now forward such strange windings make,
As though uncertain which way they should take.
38
Slaughter runs wildely through the afflicted host
Whilst yet the battle strongly does abide,
That in this strange distemprature is lost,
Where hellish fury sensibly does guide,
Never suffisde, where tyrannizing most,
That now their wounds (with mouths even opend wide,)
Lastly inforcde to call for present death,
That wants but tongues, your swords do give them breath.
39
Here lies a heap half slain, and halfly drowned,
Gasping for breath among the watery segs,
And there a sort fallen in a deadly swownd,
Troade with the press into the muddy dregs,
Other lie bleeding on the firmer ground,
Hurt in the bodies, maimde of arms and legs,
One kills a foe, his brain another cuts;
Ones feet entangled in another guts.
40
One his assayling enemy beguiles,
As from the bridge he happily does fall,
Crushed with his weight upon the forced piles,
Some in their gore upon the pauement spraule,
That every place so loathsomely defiles,
The careasses lie heaped like a wall,
Such hideous shreekes yet still the soldiers breathe,
As though the spirits had howled from beneathe.
41
The faction still defying Edwards might,
Edmond of Woodstocke with the men of Kent,
Charging afresh, reuiues the doubtful fight,
Upon the Barrons languishing and spent,
New preparation for a tragic fight;
when they again supplies immediate sent
A second battle prowdly to begin;
The noblest spirits but newly entered in.
42
As at Troyes sack, fair Thetis godlike sun,
Courageous Talbot with his shield him bare,
Clifford and Mowbray bravely following on,
Awdley and Gifford thronging for a share;
These seconding, the former being gone,
Elmsbridge and Baldsmere in the thickest are,
Pell mell together fly this furious power,
when they perceive that death will all devour.
43
Mountfort and Teis, your worth I feign would speak,
But that your valu^e does so ill deserve,
And Denuile here from thee perforce must break,
And from thy praises Willington must swerue,
Your deeds permit not I your wrongs should wreake,
Proud Damory, here must thy glory starve:
Concealing many most deserving blame,
Because your actions quench my sacred flame.
44
O had you fashioned your great deeds by them,
Who sommond Acon with an English drum,
Or marched before that fair Jerusalem,
With the united powers of Christendom,
Eternal then had been your Diadem,
And with Christ's warriors slept about his toombe:
Then ages had immortalizde your name,
Where now my song can be but of your shame.
45
O age inglorious, arms untimely borne,
When now this proved and victorious shield
Must in this civil massacre be torn,
Which bare the marks of many a bloody field;
And lastly, in their overthrown forlorn,
When now to flight the Barrons basely yield,
That since that time the stones for very dreed,
Against foul storms small drops of moisture sheed.
46
When now those wretched and unsteadfast friends,
Which all this while stood doubtfully to pawse,
When they perceive what desteny intends,
And his success does justify his cause,
Their faintness now more comfort apprehends,
For victory both fear and friendship draws,
T'an open smile, convert a covered frown,
All lend their hands to hew the conquered down.
47
That part of power the imperial seemde to lack,
whilst yet the adverse bare an upright face,
when now constraind to give a recreant back,
Quickly returns to prosecute the chase,
where now the Barrons wholly go to wrack,
In the just trial of so near a case;
Inforede to prove the fortune of the coast,
When they perceine the glorious goal is lost.
48
And to the fortunes of the conquering King,
Which well confirmde his long and tendered hope,
His fair success still more encouraging,
which now had got so large and ample scope,
The Earl of Carlell happily does bring
His light-armde bands the valiant Northern troop,
Armed too lately, and with too much speed,
To do most harm, even when we least had need.
49
When now the Barrons making out their way,
Through parts for safety, and advantage known,
Keeping their force still bodied as they may,
Into the depth of this misfortune thrown;
And in pursuit, devising day by day
TO offend the assailant, and defend their own,
In their last hope the utmost to endure,
To defer the effect, although the end were sure.
50
And whilst their fortune suting sadly thus,
To Burrough-bridge conducted by their fate,
Bridges to Barrons ever ominous,
And to this place their fall preordinate,
That ministering such cause of grief to us,
By the remembrance of their passed state;
The very soil by deep impression yet,
Even to this day does still remember it.
51
New courage now, new fights, new battles rangde,
New breath (but what might make destruction new)
They change the ground, but yet their fate vnchangde,
Which too directly does their course pursue,
Nor from their former misery estrangde,
Their strength decays, their dangers daily grew,
To shorten that which whilst it did depend,
Gave a long breathing to a fearful end.
52
Like to a heird of weary heartlesse dear,
whom hote-sporrde huntsmen seriously do chase,
In brakes and bushes falling here and there,
Proouing each covert, every secret place,
Yet by the hounds recovered every where,
with eager yearning in the sented trace;
Hemmed on each side with horns rechating blast,
Headlong themselves into the toils do cast.
53
Ensign beards ensign, sword genst sword does shake,
Wing against wing, and rank does rank oppose,
In, on each other furiously they brake,
And death in earnest to his business goes,
A general havoc as disposde to make,
And with destruction does them all enclose,
Dealing it self impartially to all,
Friend by his friend, and foe by foe does fall.
54
This part of life which yet they did respire,
In spite of Fortune, as they stood preparde,
With courage chargde, with comeliness retire,
Make good their ground, and then relieve their guarde,
Withstand the entrer, then pursue the flier,
New form their battle, shifting every ward,
As your hie courage; but were your quarrel good,
O noble spirits, how dear had bin your blood?
55
The Northern bands the ambitious Herckley led
On the weak Barrons mangled so before,
That now toward Borough make a puissant head,
Encouraging the imperial power the more;
O day so fatal, and so full of dread,
When ere shall time thy ruinous waste restore,
Which to amend although thou shall persevere,
Thou still may promise, but perform it never.
56
Pale death beyond all wonted bounds does swell,
Caruing proud flesh in cantells now at large,
As leaves in Autumn, so the bodies fell
Under rough steel at every boystrous charge,
O what sad pen can the destruction tell,
Where scalps lay beaten as the battred targe;
And every one he claims as his right,
That not prouides to escape away by flight.
57
Those ensigns erst, that in the glittring field
With their curld foreheads threat the ambitious foe,
Like wetherd foul the drowping pinions yield,
Stouping their proud heads to the dust below,
There sits a helmet, and there lies a shield,
O ill did fate these noble Arms bestow,
Which as a quarry on the soilde earth lay,
Seizde on by conquest as a glorious pray.
58
Here noble Bohune that brave, issued peer,
Herford so hie in every gracious heart,
Unto his country so receiude, and dear,
Wounded by treason in the lower part.
(As over the bridge his men returning were)
Through those ill-ioynd planckes by an envious dart:
But Lancaster, whose lot not yet to die,
Taken, reserved to greater infamy.
59
O subject for some sadder Muse to sing,
Of five great Earledomes happily possessed,
Of the direct line of the English king,
with favors friends, and earthly honors blessed,
If so that all these happiness could bring,
Or could endow assurednes of rest;
But what estate stands free from fortunes power?
The Fates have guidance of our time and hour.
60
Some few themselves in sanctuaries hide,
In mercy of that privileged place,
Yet are their bodies so vnsanctifide,
As scarce their souls can ever hope for grace;
Whereas they still in want and fear abide,
A poor dead life this draws out a space,
Hate stands without, and horror sits within,
Prolonging shame, but pard'ning not their sin.
61
Here is not death contented with the dead,
As though of some thing carelessly denide,
Till which might firmly be accomplished
His utmost fully were not specifide,
That all exactly might be perfected
A further torment vengeanec does provide,
That dead men should in misery remain,
To make the living die with greater pain.
62
You sovereign Cities of the afflicted I Will,
In Cipresse wreathes, and widowed attire,
Prepare ye now to build the funeral pile;
Lay your pale hands unto this latest fire,
All mirth and comfort from your streets exile,
Till you be purgde of this infectious ite,
The noblest blood yet living to be shed,
That ever dropped from your rebellious dead.
63
When this brave Lord great Lancaster, who late
This pu^ssant force had now thus long retainde,
As the first Agent in this strange debate
At fatal Pomfret for those facts arraignde;
(...) whom of all things they articulate,
To whom these factions chiefly appertainde:
Whose proofs apparent so directly sped,
As from his body reft a reverent head.
64
Yet Lancaster, it is not thy dear breath
Can ransom back the safety of the Crown,
Nor make a league of so great power with death,
To warrant what is rightfully our own,
But they must pay the forfait of their faith,
Which sondly broke with their ambition,
when now revenge unto the utmost rackt,
The Agents justly suffer with the act.
65
Even in that place where he had lately led,
As this dark path unto the rest to show,
It was not long ere many followed,
In the same steps that he before did go;
London, thy freedom is prohibited,
The first in place (O would the first in woe)
Others in blood did not excel thee far,
That now devour the remnant of this war.
66
O parents ruthful, and hart-renting sight,
To see that son thy tender bosom fed,
A mothers joy, a fathers sole delight,
That with much cost, yet with more care was bred;
A spectacle even able to affright
The most senseless thing, and terrify the dead;
His blood so dear upon the cold earth powered,
His quartered coarse of birds and beasts devoured.
67
But it is not you that here complain alone,
Or to your selves this fearful portion share,
Here Is choice, and strange variety of moan,
Poor childrens tears with widows mixed are;
Many a friends sigh, many a maidens groan,
So innocent, so simply, pure, and rare;
As though even Nature that long silent kept,
Burst out in plaints, and bitterly had wept.
68
O wretched age, had not these things been done,
I had not now in these more calmer times
Into the search of former troubles run;
Nor had my virgin impolluted rimes,
Altered the course wherein they first begun,
To sing these bloody and unnatural crimes,
My lays had still been to Ideas bower,
Of my dear Ankor, or her loved Stoure.
69
Or for our subject your fair worth to choose,
Your birth, your virtue, and your hie respects,
That gently daine to patronize our Muse,
Who our free soul ingeniously elects
To publish your deserts, and all your dues
Maugre the Momists, and Satyricke sects,
Whilst my great verse eternally is sung,
You still may live with me in spite of wrong.
70
But greater things reserved are in store,
Unto this task my armed Muse to keep,
Still offering me occasion as before,
Matter whereof my tragic verse may weep;
And as a vessel being near the shore,
By adverse winds enforced to the deep,
Am driven back from whence I came of late,
Unto the bus'nes of a troubled state.
The end of the second Canto.
The third Book of the Barrons wars.
The Argument.
By asleepie potion that the Queen ordaines,
Lord Mortimen escapes out of the Tower,
And by false slights, and many subtle trains,
She gets to France to raise aforraigne power;
The French King leaves his sister; need constraines
The Queen to Henault in a happy hour:
Edward her son to Philip is affide,
And for invasion presently provide.
1
SCarce had these passed miseries their ends,
When other troubles instantly begun,
As (still) new matter mischief apprehends,
By things that inconsid'rately were done;
And further yet this insolence extends,
Whilst all not yielded that the sword had won;
For some there were that secretly did lie,
That to this bus'nes had a watchful eye.
2
Whenas the King (whilst things thus fairly went)
Who by this happy victory grew strong,
Sommons at York a present Parliament,
To plant his right, and help the Spensers wrong,
By which he thinks t'establish his intent,
Whence (more and more) his Minions greatness sprung,
Whose counsels still in all proceedings crossde
The Enraged Queen, whom all misfortunes tossde.
3
When now the eldst, a man extremely hated,
Whom yet the King not aptly could prefer,
The edge of their sharp insolence abated,
This Parliament makes Earl of Winchester,
Where Herckley Earl of Carlell is created,
And Baldocke likewise is made Chancellor,
On whom the king had for his purpose wrought,
A man as subtle, so corrupt, and naught.
4
When now mishaps that seldom come alone,
Thick in the necks of one an other fell,
The Scot pretends a new invasion,
And France does thence our useful power expel,
Treasons suspected to attend his throne,
The grieved Commons every day rebel,
Mischief on mischief, curse does follow curse,
One ill scarce past, when after comes a worse.
5
For Mortimer this wind yet fitly blew,
Troubling their eyes which else perhaps might see,
Whilst the wise Queen, who all advantage knew,
Is closly plotting his delivery,
(Which now she does with all her powers pursue)
Aptly continued by her deep policy,
Against opinion, and the course of might,
To work her will, even through the jaws of spite.
6
A sleepy drink she secretly has made,
Whose operation had such wondrous power,
As with cold numnesse could the sense invade,
And mortify the patient by an hour,
The lifelesse coarse in such a slumber laid,
As though pale death did wholly it devour:
Nor for two days take benefit of eyes,
By all means Art or Physic could devise.
7
For which she Plantane and cold Lettice had,
The water Lilly from the marrish ground,
with the wanne Poppy, and the Nightshade sad,
And the short moss that on the trees is found,
The poysning Henbane, and the Mandrake drad,
With Cypress flowers that with the rest are pownd;
The brain of Cranes like purposely she takes,
Mixed with the blood of Dormise, and of Snakes.
8
Thus sits the great Enchauntresse in her Cell,
Strongly engi^t with ceremonious charms,
Her cleansed body sensde with halowing smell,
With vestall fire her potent liquor warmes,
Having full heat, unto her busnes fell,
When her with Magic instruments she arms:
And from the herbs the powrefull verdure wrong,
To make the medcine forcible and strong.
9
The sundry doubts that incident arise,
Might be supposde her trembling hand to stay,
If she considred of the enterprise,
To think what peril in th'attempting lay,
The secret lurking of deceitful spies,
That on her steps continually do pray:
But when they leave off virtue to esteem,
Those greatly err which take them as they seem.
10
Their plighted faith for liberty they leave,
Their love is cold, their lust hot, hot their hate,
With smiles and tears they serpent-like deceive,
In their desires they be insatiate,
There Is no restraint their purpose can bereave,
Their will no bound, nor their revenge no date,
All fear exempt where they at ruin aim,
Covering their sin with their discovered shame.
11
The elder of the Mortimers this space,
(That many sundry miseries had past,)
So long restrainde within that healthlesse place,
Redeemde by death, yet happily at last
That much auailes the other in this case,
And from this Lord that imposition cast:
Which the dear safety of his uncles breath
within the tower so strictly limiteth.
12
Put there was more did on his death depend,
Than heaven was pleased the foolish world should know,
And why the Fates thus hasted on his end,
Thereby intending greater things to show;
Brave Lord, in vain thy breath thou did not spend,
From thy corruption further matters grow,
And some beginning fruitfully to spring,
New forms of fear upon the time to bring.
13
All things preparde in readiness, and fit,
The Queen attends her potions power to prove,
Their steadfast friends, their best assisting it,
Their seruan^s seal their secrets up in love;
And he express his valor and his wit,
Whom of the rest it chiefly does behoue,
Places resolved where guide and horses lay,
And where the ship him safely to convey.
14
As his large bounties liberally were heap't
To all deserving, or to those that heed,
His solemn birth-dayes festival was kept
At his free charge, all in the Tower to feed,
which may suspicion clearly intercept,
A strong assistant in so great a need,
When amidst their cates, their furious thirst to quench,
Mixing their wine with this approved drench.
15
Which soon each sense, and every power does seize,
when he that knew the strength of every ward,
And to the purpose sorting all his keys,
His corded ladders readily preparde;
And lurking forth by the most secret ways
Not now to learn his Compass by the Card,
To win the walls courageously does go,
which look as scorning to be maistied so.
16
They soundly sleep whilst his quick spirits awake,
Opposde to peril, and the stern'st extremes,
Alcydes labors new to undertake,
Of walls, of gates, of watches, and of streams,
Through which his passage he is now to make,
And let them tell king Edward of their dreams:
For ere they rose out of the brainsicke france,
He hopes to tell this noble jest in France.
17
The sullen night has her black curtains spread,
Lowering the day had tarried up so long,
Whose fair eyes closing softly steals to bed,
When all the heavens with duskie clouds are hung,
And Cynthia now pluckes in her horned head,
And to the West incontinently flung;
As she had longed to certify the sun,
What in his absence in her Court was done.
18
The glimmering lights, like Sentinels in war,
Behind the clouds stand craftily to pry,
And through false loope-holes looking from afar,
To see him skirmish with his desteny;
Not any fixed, nor any wandering star,
As they had held a counsel in the sky;
And had before concluded with the night,
It should not look for any cheerful sight.
19
In deadly silence all the shores are hushed,
Only the Skreech-howle sounds to the assault,
And Isis with a troubled murmr rushed,
As if consenting, and would hide the fault;
And as his foot the sand or gravel crushed,
A little whispering moved within the vault,
Made by the treading softly as he went,
Which seemed to say, it furthred his intent.
20
This wondrous Queen whom care yet restless kept,
Now for his speed to heaven holds up her hands,
A thousand strange thoughts in her bosom heap't,
As in her closet listening still she stands,
That many a sigh spent, many a warm tear wept,
And though divided, as in sundry strands,
Most absent, present in desires they be,
Our minds discern where eyes do cease to see.
21
The small clouds issuing from his lips she says,
Laboring so fast, as he the ladder clame,
Should purge the air of pestilence and death;
And as sometime that filch'd Promethian flame,
Even so the power and virtue of his breath,
New creatures in the elements should frame;
And to what part of heaven it happ'd to stray,
There should path out another milkie way.
22
Attainde the top, half spent a while to blow,
Now round about he casts his longing eyes,
The gentle earth salutes him from below,
And covered with the comfortable skies,
Viewing the way that he is now to go,
Cheered with the beams of Isabels fair eyes,
Down from the turret desperately does slide,
Night be successful, fortune be his guide.
23
With his descent, her eye so still descends,
As fear had fixed it to fore-warne his fall,
On whom her hope and fortune now depends,
When sudden fear her senses does appall;
For present aid her god-like hand extends,
Forgets herself, and speedy aid does call;
Silent again, if ought but good should hap,
She begs of heaven his grave may be her lap.
24
Now she intreates the dark distempred air,
Then by strong Magicks she coniures the wind,
Then she inuokes the gloomie night by prayre,
Then with her spells the mortal sense to bind;
And fearing much lest these yet frustrate are,
Now by the burning tapers she diuin'd,
Entreating Thames to give a friendly pass,
The dearest fraught ere on her bosom was.
25
The rushing murmr stills her like a song,
But yet in fear the stream should fall in love,
Suspects the drops that on his tresses hung,
And that the billows for his beauty strove,
To this fair body that so closely clong,
Which when in swimming with his breast he drove;
Palled with grief she turns away her face,
Jealous that he the waters should embrace.
26
This angry Lion having slipped his chain,
As in a fever makes King Edward quake,
Which knew (too well) ere he was caught again,
Dear was the blood must serve his thirst to stake,
Many the labors had been spent in vain,
And he enforced a longer course to take,
Saw further vengeance hanging in the wind,
That knew the pride and greatness of his mind.
27
The faction working in this lingering jarr^,
How for the Scot free passage might be made,
To lay the ground of a succesfull war,
That hope might breed fresh courage to invade;
And whilst our safety stands out so far,
More dangerous proiects every where are laid;
That some in hand home troubles to enure,
Others in France do foreign broiles procure.
28
By these discentions that were lately sown,
Inciting Charles to open Arms again,
Who seazing Guyne, pretended as his own,
That Edward should unlawfully detain,
Proceeds to make a further title known.
To Our Lands in Pontieu, and in Aquitaine,
When wanted homage has desolu'd the truce,
Waking his wrongs by Isabels abuse.
29
This plot concluded that was long in hand,
(Which to this issue prosperously had thriu'd)
The Base whereon a mighty frame must stand,
With mickle Art, yet with more fcare cont^iu'd;
So strongly builded by this factious band,
As from the same their safety is derived,
Till their full-rooted and inueterate hate,
Getting more strength might deeply penetrate.
30
When choice of such to sway this French affair,
Which as a sharpelesse and unwieldy mass,
Might well employ the strength of all their care;
So hard and perlous to be brought to pass,
Which it behooues them quickly to prepare,
That being now so settled as it was,
Craves a grave spirit, whose eminence and power,
Might like a stiff gale check this threatening shower.
31
This must a Session seriously debate,
That depth of judgment craved to be discust,
That so concerns the safety of the state,
And in a case so plausible and just,
As might have quenched all sparckes of former hate,
(...) (...)ight be thought even policy might trust,
Could envy master her distracted will,
Or apprehend sacietie in ill.
32
Tarleton, whose tongue men's ears in chains could tie,
And as a fearful thunderbolt could pierce,
In which there more authority did lie,
Then in the Sybils sage propheticke verse,
Whose sentence was so absolute and hie,
As had the power a judgment to reuerse;
On the Queens part with all his might does stand,
To lay this charge on her well-guiding hand.
33
What helps her presence to the cause might bring,
Being a wife, a sister, and a mother,
And in so great and pertinent a thing,
To right her lonne, her husband, and her brother,
Her gracious help to all distributing,
To take of her what they should hold of other;
Which color serves to effect in these extremes,
That which (God knows) King Edward never dreams.
34
Torleten, is this thy spiritual pretense?
Would God thy thoughts were more spiritual,
Or less perswasiue were thy eloquence.
But O! thy actions are too temporal;
Opinion lends too great pre-eminence,
Thy reasons subtle, and sophistical;
Would all were true thy supposition says,
Thy arguments less force, or thou more faith.
35
These sudden broiles that were begun of late,
Still kept in motion by their secret slight,
By false suggestions so interminate;
That as a ballast of some solid weight,
Betwixt these adverse currents of debate,
Kept their proceeding in a course so straight,
As lends the Queen an ampler color still,
By general means to work a general ill.
36
She which thus fitly found both wind and tide,
And sees her leisure serve, the hour so near,
All her endeavors mutually applied,
Whilst for her purpose things so fitly were,
And thus advantage quickly had espied,
As one whose fortunes taught the worst to fear,
Seeing the times so variously inclinde,
And every toy soon altring Edwards mind.
37
Her followers such as friendlesse else had stood,
Suncke, and dejected by the Spensers pride,
Who bare the brands of treason in their blood,
Which but with blood there was no way to hide;
Whose mean was weak, whose will was but too good,
Which to effect did but the hour abide,
And knew all means that mischief could invent,
That any way might further her intent.
38
Whilst Mortimer which now so long has lain
From our just course, by fortune lately crosst,
In France now struggling how he might regaine
That which before he had in England lost,
All present means does gladly entertain,
No jote dismaide in all these tempests tosst:
Nor his great mind can thus be overthrown,
All men his friends, all countries are his own.
39
And Muse, transported by thy former zeal,
Led in our progress where his fortune lies,
To thy fair aid I seriously appeal,
To sing this great man his magnanimous guise,
The ancient Heroes unto me reveal,
whose worths may raise our nobler faculties,
That in my verse, transparent, nete, and clear,
His character more lively may appear.
40
Such one he was, of him we boldly say,
In whose rich soul all sovereign powers did suit,
In whom in peace the elements all lay
So mixed, as none could sovereignty impute;
As all did govern, yet all did obey,
His lively temper was so absolute,
That t'seemde when heaven his model first began,
In him it showed perfection in a man.
41
So thoroughly seasond, and so rightly set,
As in the level of clear judgments eye,
Time never tuch't him with deforming fret,
Nor had the power to wrap him once awry,
Whose steadfast course no cross could ever let,
His elevation was so heavenly hie,
Those giddy tempests that the base world prove,
State under where he Planet like did move.
42
Which this fair Queen that had a knowing spirit,
And saw the beauties resting in his mind,
One that had thoroughly looked into his merit,
Above the value of the vulgar kind,
That rightly did his Grandsires deeds inherit,
When now the ages in their course declinde,
when the old world, being weak began to bow,
To th'effeminate baseness that it rests at now.
43
What weighs he wealth, or what his Wigmore left?
Let needless heaps, things momentary stand,
He counts not his that can be rapde by theft,
Man is the sole Lord both of sea and land,
And still is rich of these that is not reft,
Who of all creatures has an upright hand;
And by the stars is only taught to know,
That as they progress heaven, he earth should do.
44
Wherefore wise Nature forcde this face of ground,
And through the deeps showed him the secret way,
That in the floods her judgments might be found,
Where she for safety did her treasure lay;
Whose store, that he might absolutely sound,
She gave him courage for her only kay,
That he alone of all her creatures free,
Her glory, and her wondrous works should see.
45
Let wretched worldlings sweat for mud and earth,
whose groueling bosoms lick the recreant stones,
And pesants carke for plenty, and for dearth,
Fame never looks upon these prostrate drones,
Man is allotted at his princely birth,
To manage Empires, and to sit on thrones,
Frighting coy Fortune when she sternst appears,
Which else scorns sighs, and jeeteth at our tears.
46
When now Report with her fleet murmuring wing,
Tucht the still entrance of his listening ear,
A fleet preparde this royal Queen to bring,
And her arrival still awaited near,
When every sound a note of love does sing,
The joyful thoughts that in his bosom were:
The soul in doubt to make her function less,
Denies the utterance fully to express.
47
Quoth he, Slide billows gently for her sake,
Whose sight can make your aged Nereus young,
For her fair passage even allies make,
On the sleeke waters wast her sails along;
And whilst she glides upon the pleasant lake,
Let the sweet Sirens rock her with a Song:
Though not Loves mother that does pass this way,
Fairer than she that's borne upon the sea.
48
You Sea-bred creatures, gaze upon her eye,
And never after with your kind make war;
O steal the accents from her lip that fly,
Which like the musickes of the Angels are,
And them unto your amorous thoughts apply,
Comparde with which, Aryons did but jarre:
Wrap them in air, and when black tempests rage;
Use them as charms the rough seas to assuage.
49
France, send to fetch her with full sholes of oars,
With which her fleet may every way be plide,
And being landed on thy happy shoares,
As the vast navy does at anckor ride;
For her departure when the wild sea roares,
Ship mount to heaven, there brightly stellifide:
Next Iasons Argo on the burnisht throne,
Assume thee there a constellation.
50
Her person hence conuaide with that delight,
Which best the languish of her iournies easde,
That to her pleasure does it self inuit,
Whereon her mind, and subtle fancy seasde,
And that (most dear) her liking might excite,
Which then this Lord, naught more her presence pleased,
where, when with state she first her time could take,
Thus the fair Queen her Mortimer bespake.
51
O Mortimer, great Mortimer, quoth she,
What angry power did first this mean devise,
To separate Queen Isabell and thee,
whom loves eternal union strongly ties;
But if supposde this fault began by me,
For a just penance to my longing eyes:
(Though guiltless they) this punishment assignde,
To gaze upon thee, till they leave me blind.
52
It Is strange, sweet friend, how thou art altered thus,
Since first in Court thou did our favors wear,
whose shape seemde then not mortal unto us,
when in our eye thy brow was beauties sphere,
In all perfection so harmonious,
A thousand several graces mooving there;
But what then could thou be, not now thou art,
An alien first, last home-borne in my heart.
53
That powerful fate thy safety did enforce,
And from the worst of danger did thee free,
Still regular, and constant in one course,
Wrought me a firm and even path to thee,
Of our affections as it took remorce,
Our birth-fix't stars so happily agree;
Whose revolution seriously directs
Our like proceedings, to the like effects.
54
New form of counsel in the course of things,
To our dissignement finds a nearer way,
That by a clear and perfect managing,
Is that firm prop whereon we only stay;
Which in it self the authority does bring,
That weak opinion has no power to sway;
Confuting such, whose sightlesse judgment sit,
In the thick rank with every vulgar wit.
55
Then since pleased Time our wished content assures,
Embrace the blessings of our mutual rest;
And whilst the day of our good hap endures,
And we as favorites lean on Fortunes breast,
Which does for us this vacancie procure,
In choice make free election of the best;
Never fear the s^orme before thou feel the shower,
My son a King, an Empire is my dower.
56
Of wanton Edward when I first was wooed,
Why came thou not into the Court of France?
Thy self alone then in my grace had stood;
Dear Mortimer, how good had been thy chance?
My love attempted in that youthful mood,
I might have been thine own inheritance;
Where entering now by force, thou hold'st thy might,
And art deseisor of another right.
57
Honor thou Idol women so adore,
How many plagues do thou retain to grieve us,
When still we find there is remaining more,
Then that great word of Majesty can give us;
Which takes more from us then it can restore,
And of that comfort often does deprive us,
That with our own selves sets us at debate,
And make us beggars under our estate.
58
Those pleasing raptures from her graces rise,
Strongly inuading his impressiue breast,
That soon entranced all his faculties,
Of the proud fullness of their joys possessed;
And having thoroughly wrought him in this wise,
Like tempting Sirens sing him to his rest,
When every power is passive of some good,
Felt by the spirits of his high-rauisht blood.
59
Like as a Lute that is touched with curious skill,
In musickes language sweetly speaking plain,
When every string his note with sound does fill,
Taking the tones, and giving them again,
And the ear bath's in harmony at will,
A diapason closing every strain;
So their affections set in keys so like,
Still fall in consort as their humors strike.
60
When now the path to their desire appears,
Of which before they had been long debarred,
By desolution of some threatening fears,
That for destruction seemed to stand prepared,
Which the smooth face of better safety bears,
And now protected by a stronger guard,
Gives the large scope of leisure to forecast
Events to come, by things already past,
61
These great dissignements setting easly out,
By due proportion measuring every pace,
T'auoide the cumbrance of each hindering doubt,
That might distort the comeliness and grace,
Coming with every circumstance about,
Strictly observing person, time and place;
All ornaments in fair discretions laws,
Could give attire to beautify the cause.
62
The Embassy in terms of equal height,
As well their state and dignity might fit,
Apparelling a matter of that weight,
In ceremony well beseeming it,
To carry things so steady, and so right,
Where Wisdom with clear majesty might sit;
All things still seeming strictly to effect,
That Love commands, and Greatness should respect.
63
Whose expedition by this fair success,
That does again this ancient league combine,
when Edward should by covenant release,
And to the Prince the Provinces resign,
With whom king Charles renues the happy peace,
Receiving homage due to him for Guyne:
And lastly now to consumate their speed,
Edwards own person to confirm the deed.
64
Who whilst he stands yet doubtful what to do,
The Spensers chiefly that his counsels guide,
Nor with their Sovereign into France durst go,
Nor in his absence durst at home abide;
Now whilst the weak king stands perplexed so,
His listening ears with such persuasion plide,
As he at last to stay in England's won,
And in his place, to send the Prince his son.
65
Thus is the King encompasst by their skill,
A mean to work what Herford does devise,
To thrust him on, to draw them up the hill,
That by his strength they might get power to rise:
Thus they in all things are before him still,
This perfect steersman of their policies
Has cast to walk whilst Edward bears the light,
And take that aim that must direct his sight.
66
And by th'allowance of his liberal will,
Supposde his safety, furthering their intent,
Stands as a test to justify their ill,
Made sound and current by this late event,
And what yet wanting lastly to fulfill,
Things in their course to fall in true consent,
Gives full assurance of that happy end,
On which they now laboriously attend.
67
Nor finding reason longer to protract,
Or in suspense their home-left friends to hold,
By being now so absolutely backed,
And thereby waxing confident and bold,
By their proceedings publishing their act,
whenas their power was ripened as they would,
Now with an armed and erected hand,
To abet their faction absolutely stand.
68
When now the fearful fainting Exceter,
A man experiencde in their counsels long,
Whether himself thought his way to prefer,
Or moved in conscience with king Eawards wrong,
Or it was his frailty forede him thus to err,
Or other fatal accident among,
The only first that back to England flew,
And knowing all, discovered all he knew.
69
The plot of treason lastly thus disclosde,
And Torletons drift by circumstaunces found,
With what conueyance things had been disposde;
The cunning used in casting of the ground,
The means and apt advantages he chosde,
When better counsel coldely comes to sound,
Awakes the King to see his own estate,
When the prevention comes too vain and late.
70
And whilst the time she daily does adjourne,
Charles as a brother, by persuasions deals,
Edward, with threats, to hasten her return,
And John of Rome with Papal curse assaies,
