Which his proud Leopards for their safety wore,
Raging at Hastings like that ominous Lake,
From whose dread waves our glorious name we take
84
Had I been chargde upon mine armed horse,
As when I came unto the walls of Gaunt,
Before the Belgike and Burgonian force,
There challenging, my Countries Combattant,
Borne from my seat in some robustious course,
That of my spoils the enemy might vaunt,
Or had I fallen under my battered shield,
And lent mine honor to some conquered field.
85
I have not followed Fortun like a slave,
To make her bounty any whit the less,
By my desert her judgment to deprave,
Nor lent me aught I freely not confess,
And have returned with intrest what she gave,
A mind that suited with her mightiness,
He twice offends which sin in flattry bears,
Yet every hour he dies that ever fears.
86
I cannot fear what forces others quake,
The times and I have tuggd together so,
Wonting my way through sword and fire to make,
So often constraind against the stream to row,
To doubt with Death a covenant to make,
When I am grown familiar with my woe:
And nothing can the afflicted conscience grieve,
But he can pardon, that does all forgive.
87
And thus thou most adored in my heart,
Whose thoghts in death my humbled sprite does raise
Lady most fair, most dear, of most desert,
Worthy of more than any mortal praise,
Condemned March, thus lastly does depart
From her, the greatest Empress of her days:
Nor in the dust mine honor I interre,
Thus Caesar did, and thus dies Mortimer.
88
To Nottingham this Letter brought unto her,
Which is subscribde with her Emperious style,
Puts her in mind how once that hand did woo her,
With this short thought to please herself a while,
Thus sorrow can so subtly vndooe her,
That with such flattery does her sense beguile.
To give a sharper feeling to that pain,
Which her grieved heart was shortly to sustain.
89
Putting her fingers to unrip the seal,
Cleaning to keep those sorrows from her eyes,
As it were loath the tidings to reveal,
Whence grief should spring in such varieties;
But strongly urged does to her will appeal,
When the soft wax unto her touch implies,
Sticking unto her fingers bloody red,
To show the bad news quickly followed.
90
Thus by degrees she easly does begin,
As the small fish plays with the baited hook,
Then more and more to swallow sorrow in,
As threatening death at every little look;
Where now she reads th'expences of her sin,
Sadly set down in this black dreadful book,
And those dear sums were like to be desray'd,
Before the same were absolutely paid.
91
An host of woes her suddenly assail,
As every letter wounded like a dart,
As though contending which should most prevail,
Yet every one does pierce her to the heart,
As every word did others case bewail,
And with his neighbor seemde to bear a part,
Reason of grief each sentence is to her,
And every line a true remembrancer.
92
Grief makes her read, yet straitwaies bids her leave,
With which ore-charg'd she neither sees nor hears,
Her senses now their Mistress so deceive,
The words do wound her eyes, the sound her ears,
And every organe of the use bereaues,
When for a fescue she does use her tears;
That when some line she loosely ouer-past,
The drops do tell her where she left the last.
93
O now she sees, was never such a sight,
And seeing, cursed her sorrow-seeing eye,
Yet thinks she is deluded by the light,
Or is abusde by the orthography;
And by some other it is deuisde for spite,
Or pointed false, her schollership to try;
Thus when we fondly sooth our own desires,
Our best conceits often prove the greatest liers.
94
Her trembling hand as in a fever shakes,
Wherewith the paper does a little stir,
Which she imagines at her sorrow shakes,
And pitties it, who she thinks pitties her,
Each small thing somewhat to the greater makes,
And to the humor something does infer;
Which when so soon as she her tongue could free,
O worthy Earl, dear loved Lord quoth she.
95
I will reserve thy ashes in some Vrne,
Which as a relic I will only save,
Mixed with the tears that I for thee shall mourn,
Which in my dear breast shall their burial have,
From whence again they never shall return,
Nor give the honor to another grave,
But in that Temple ever be preserved,
Where thou a Saint religiously art served.
96
When she breaks out to cursing of her son,
But March so much still runs in her mind,
That she abruptly ends what she begun,
Forgets her self, and leaves the rest behind,
From this she to another course does run,
To be reuengde in some notorious kind:
To which she deeply does engage her troth,
Bound by a strong vow and a solemn oath.
97
For pen and incke she calls her maids without,
And the kings dealings will in grief discover,
But soon forgetting what she went about,
She now begins to write unto her lover,
Here she sets down, and there she blotteth out.
Her grief and passion do so strongly move her:
When turning back to read what she had writ,
She tears the paper, and condemns her wit.
98
And thus with contrarieties araised,
As waters chillness wakeneth from a swownd,
Comes to her self, the agony appeased,
When colder blood more sharply feels the wound,
And grief her so incurably has seized,
That for the same no remedy is found,
As the poor refuge to her restless woes,
This of her grief she lastly does dispose.
99
That now unkind King as thou art my son,
Leaving the world, some legacy must give thee,
My hearts true love the dying March has won,
Yet that of all I will not quite bereave thee;
The wrong and mischief to thy mother done,
I thee bequeathe, so bound that they out live thee,
That as my breast it hourly does torment,
Thou may enjoy it by my Testament.
100
Henceforth within this solitary place,
Abandoning for ever general sight,
A private life I willingly embrace,
No more rejoicing in the obuious light,
To consumate the weary lingering space,
Till death enclose me with continual night:
Each small remembrance of delight to fly,
A conuertite and penitently die.
Finis.
To the Reader.
Seeing these Epistles are now to the world made public, it is imagined that I
ought to be accountable of my private meaning, chiefly for mine own
discharge, lest being mistaken, I fall in hazard of a inst and universal
reprehension, for: Hae nugae seria ducent
In mala derisum semel exceptumque sinistre.


Three points are especially therefore to be explained. First, why I entitle
this work Englands Heroical Epistles; then, why I observe not the persons
dignity in the dedication; lastly, why I have annexed notes to every Epistles
end. For the first, the title I hope carries reason in it self, for that the
most and greatest persons here in, were English, or else, that their loves
were obtained in England. And though (heroical) be properly understood of
demigods, as of Hercules and Aeneas, whose parents were said to be, the one
celestall, the other mortal, yet is it also transferred to them, who for the
greatness of mind come near to Gods. For to be borne of a celestial
Incubus, is nothing else but to have a great and mighty spirit, far above
the earthly weakness of men; in which sense, Ovid (whose imitator I partly
profess to be) does also use Heroical. For the second, seeing none to whom I
have dedicated any two Epistles, but have their states ouer-matched by them,
who are made to speak in the Epistles, howeuer the order is in dedication, yet
in respect of their degrees in my devotion, and the cause before recited, I
hope they suffer no disparagement, seeing every one is the first in their
particular interest, having in some sort, sorted the complexion of the Epistles
to the character of their judgments to whom I dedicate them, excepting only
the blamefulnes of the persons passion, in those points wherein the passion is
blamefull. Lastly, such manifest diffrence being betwixt every one of them,
where, or howsoever they be marshalled, how can I be justly appeached of
vnaduisement. For the third, because the work might in truth be judged
brainish, if nothing but amorous humor were handled therein, I have enter-wouen
matters historical, which vnexplaned, might defraude the mind of much
content, as for example, in Queen Margarites Epistle to William de la Poole,
My Daizie flower, which once perfumde the air,


Margarite in French signifies a Daizie, which for the allusion to her name,
this Queen did give for her devise; and this as others more, have seemed to me
not worthy the explaning.

Now, though no doubt I had need to excuse other things beside, yet these
most especially, the rest I ouerpasse to eschew tedious recital, or to speak
as malicious envy may, for that in truth I ouersee them. If they be as
harmelesly taken, as I meant them, it shall suffice to have only touched the
cause of the title of the Dedications, and of the Notes, whereby emboldned to
publish the residue, (these not being accounted in men's opinions relishlesse) I
shall not lastly be afraid to believe and acknowledge thee a gentle Reader.

M. D.

To M. Michaell Drayton.
HOw can he write that broken has his pen;
Has rent his paper, thrown his incke away;
Detests the world, and company of men,
Because they grow more hateful day by day?
Yet with these broken relics, mated mind,
And what a iustly-grieued thought can say:
I give the world to know, I never could find,
A work more like to live a longer day.
Go Verse, an object for the prowdest eye;
Disdain those which disdain to read thee over,
Tell them they know not how they should descry,
The secret passions of a wirty lover.
For they are such, as none but those shall know,
Whom Beauty schools to hold the blind Boys bow.
Once I had vowed, (O who can all vows keep?)
Henceforth to smother my unlucky Muse;
Yet for thy sake she started out of sleep,
Yet now she dies: Then do as kinsfolks use;
Close up the eyes of my new-dying style,
As I have opened thy sweeet babes ere-while.
E. St. Gent.
Duris decus omen:
To M. Michaell Drayton.
LOng have I wished and hopde my weaker Muse,
(In nothing strong but my unhappy love)
Would give me leave my fortune to approve,
And view the world, as named, Poets use;
But still her fruitless bosom does refuse
To bless me with indifferency of praise,
Not daring (like to many) to abuse
That title which true worth should only raise;
Thus bankerout, and despairing of mine own,
I set my wish and hope (kind friend) on thee,
Whose fruit approved, and better fortune known,
Tells me thy Muse my loves sole heir must be,
So barren wombs embrace their neighbors young,
So dumb men speak by them that have a tongue.
Thomas Hassall, Gent.

To M. Michaell Drayton.
NOw I perceive Pithagoras diuinde,
When he that mocked Maxim did maintain,
That spirits once spoilde, reuested were again,
Though changde in shape, remaining one in mind;
These love sick Princes passionate estates;
Who feeling reads, he cannot but allow,
That Ouids soul reuines in Drayton now,
Still learnd in love still rich in rare conceits,
This pregnant spirit affecting further skill,
Often altring form, from vulgar wits retirde,
In diverse Ideoms mightily admirde,
Did prosecute that sacred study still;
While to a full perfection now attainde,
He sings so sweetly that himself is stainde.
William Alexander-Scotus.

To the excellent Lady Lucie Countess of Bedford.
MAdam, after all the admired wits of this excellent age, which have labored
in the sad complaints of fair and unfortunate Rosamond, and by the excellency
of invention, have sounded the depth of her sundry passions; I present to your
Ladyship this Epistle of hers to King Henry, whom I may rather call her lover
than beloved. Here must your Ladyship behold variablenes in resolution; woes
constantly grounded; laments abruptly broken off; much confidence, no
certainty, words begetting tears, tears confounding matter, large complaint^
in little papers; and many deformed cares, in one vniformed Epistle. I strive
not to effect singularity, yet would feign fly imitation, and prostrate mine
own wants to other men's perfections. Your judicial eye must model forth what
my pen has laid together, much would she say to a King, much would I say to a
Countess, but that the method of my Epistle must conclude the modesty of
hers, which I wish may recommend my ever vowed service to your Honor.

Michaell Drayton.

The Epistle of Rosamond to King Henry the second.
The Argument.
Henry the second of that name, King of England, the son of Geffrey
Plantaginet, Earl of Anlow, and Mawd the Empress, having by long suit and
Princely gifts, won (to his unlawful desire) fair Rosamond, the daughter of
the Lord Walter Clyfford and to avoid the danger of Ellinor his jealous
Queen, had caused a Labyrinth to be made within his Palace at Woodstocke, in
the center whereof he had lodged his beauteous paramour. Whilst the king is
absent in his wars in Normandy, this poor distressed Lady, enclosed in this
solitary place, touched with remorce of conscience, writes to the king of her
distress and miserable estate, urging him by all means and persuasions, to
clear himself of this infamy, and her of the grief of mind, by taking away
her wretched life.

IF yet thine eyes (great Henry) may endure
These tainted lines, drawn with a hand impure,
Which fain would blush, but fear keeps blushes back,
And therefore suited in dispairing black,
This in loves name, O that these lips might crave,
But that sweet name (vile I) profaned have,
Punish my fault, or pity mine estate,
Read (...) for love, if not for love, for hate.
If with my shame thine eyes thou feign would feed,
Here let them su^feit, on my shame to reed;
This scribled paper which (...) send to thee,
If noted rightly, does resemble me;
As this pure ground, whereon th^se letters stand,
So pure was I, er^stained by thy hand;
Ere I was blotted with this foul offense,
So clear and spotless was mine innocence,
Now like these marks which taint this hateful scroule,
Such the black sins which spot my l^prous soul.
O Henry, why by loss thus should thou win?
To get by conquest? to enrich with sin?
Why on my name this slander do thou bring,
To make my fault renowned by a King?
Fame never stoopes to things but mean and poor,
The more our greatness, makes our fault the more.
Lights on the ground, themselves do less^n far,
But in the air, each small spark seems a star.
Why on a woman's frailty would thou lay
This subtle plot, mine honor to betray?
Or thy unlawful pleasure should thou buy
With vile expense of kingly maies^ie?
It Was not my mind consented to this ill,
Then had I been transported by my will:
For what my body was inforcde to do,
(Heaven knows) my soul did not consent unto;
For through mine eyes had she her liking seen,
Such as my love, such had my lover been.
True love is simple, like his mother Truth,
Kindly affection, youth to love with youth;
No sharper corsiue to our blooming years,
Then the cold badge of winter-blasted hairs.
Thy kingly power makes to withstand thy foes,
But can not keep back age, with Time it grows,
Though honor our ambitious sex does please,
Yet in that honor, age a foul disease,
Nature has her free course in all, and then,
Age is alike in Kings, and other men,
Which all the world will to my shame impute,
That I my self did basely prostitute;
And say that gold was fuel to the fire,
Gray hairs in youth not kindling green desire.
O no; that wicked woman wrought by thee,
My tempter was to that forbidden tree,
That subtle Serpent, that seducing devil,
Which bade me taste the fruit of good and evil;
That Circe, by whose magic I was charmd,
And to this monstrous shape am thus transformed,
That viperous hag, the foe to her own kind,
That wicked spirit unto the weaker mind;
Our frailtes plague, our natures only curse,
Hels deepst damnation, the worst evils worse.
But Henry, how can thou affect me thus,
T'whom thy remembrance now is odious?
My hapless name, with Henry's name I found,
Cut in the glass with Henry's Diamond,
That glass from thence feign would I take away;
But then I fear the air would me betray;
Then do I strive to wash it out with tears,
But then the same more evident appears.
Then do I cover it with my guilty hand,
Which that names witness does against me stand;
Once did I sin, which memory does cherrish,
Once I offended, but I ever perrish.
What grief can be, but time does make it less?
But infamy time never can suppress.
Sometimes to pass the tedious irksome hours,
I climb the top of Woodstockes mounting towers,
Where in a Turret secretly I lie,
To view from far such as do travel by,
Whether (me thinks) all cast their eyes at me,
As through the stones my shame did make them see,
And with such hate the harmless walls do view,
As unto death their eyes would me pursue.
The married women curse my hateful life,
Which wrong a lawful bed, a Queen, a wife;
The maidens wish I buried quick may die,
The loathsome stain to their virginity.
Well knewst thou what a monster I would be
When thou did build this Labyrinth for me,
Whose strange Meanders turning every way,
Be like the course wherein my youth did stray;
Only a Clue to guide me out and in,
But yet still walk I circular in sin.
As in the Ta^ras here this other day,
My maid and I did pass the time away,
Among many pictures which we passed by,
Theseely gerle at length hapt to epsy
Chaste Lucrece picture, and desires to know,
What she should be herself that murdered so?
Why getle (quoth I) this is that Roman Dame,
Not able then to tell the rest for shame,
My tongue does mine own guiltiness betray;
With that I send the prattling girl away,
Lest when my lisping guilty tongue should hault,
My looks should be the index to my fault.
As that life blood which from the heart is sent,
In beauties field pitching his crimson Tent,
In lonely sanguine suits the Lilly cheek,
Whilst it but for a resting place does seek;
And changing oftentimes with sweet delight,
Conuerts the white to red, the red to white.
The lovely blush the palenesse does distaine;
The palenesse makes the blush more fair again;
Thus in my breast a thousand thoughts I carry,
Which in my passion diversely do vary.
Whenas the Sun hales toward the Western slade,
And the trees shadows three times greater made,
Forth go I to a little current near,
Which like a wanton traile creepes here and there,
Wherewith mine angle casting in my bait,
The little fishes (dreading the deceit)
With fearful nibbling fly th'inticing gin,
By nature taught what danger lies therein.
Things reasonlesse thus warnde by nature be,
Yet I devoured the bait was laid for me;
Thinking thereon, and breaking into groans,
The bubling spring which trips upon the stones,
Chides me away, lest sitting but too nie,
I should pollute that native purity.
Rose of the world, so does import my name,
Shame of the world, my life has made the same.
And to th'vnchaste this name shall given be,
Of Rosamond, derived from sin and me.
The Cliffords take from me that name of theirs,
Famous for virtue many hundred years.
They blot my birth with hateful bastardie,
That I sprang not from their nobility;
They my alliance utterly refuse,
Nor will a strumpet shall their name abuse.
Here in the garden wrought by curious hands,
Naked Diana in the fountain stands,
With all her Nymphs got round about to hide her,
As when Acteon had by chance espide her;
This sacred Image I no sooner viewed,
But as that metamorphosde man pursued
By his own hounds; so by my thoughts am I,
Which chase me still, which way so ere I fly.
Touching the grass, the honny-dropping dew,
Which falls in tears before my limber shue,
Upon my foot consumes in weeping still,
As it would say, why wentst thou unto ill?
Thus to no place in safety can I go.
But every thing does give me cause of woe.
In that fair Casket of such wondrous cost,
Thou sentst the night before mine honor lost
Amimone was wrought, a harmless maid,
By Neptune that adult'rous God betraide;
She prostrate at his feet begging with prayers,
Wringing her hands, her eyes swollen up with tears;
This was not the entrapping bait of men,
But by thy virtue gentle warning then;
To show to me for what intent it came,
Lest I therein should ever keep my shame.
And in this Casket (ill I see it now)
What Jove's love I-o, turnde into a Cow.
Yet was she kept with Argus hundred eyes,
So wakefull stil^ be Iunoes jealousies:
By this I well might have forewarned been,
To Have cleerde my self to thy suspecting Queen,
Who with more hundred eyes at ends me,
Then had poor Argus single eyes to see.
In this thou rightly imitatest Jove,
Into a beast thou have transformde thy love.
Nay, worser far, (degenerate from kind)
A monster, both in body and in mind.
The waxen taper which I burn by night,
With his dull vapory dimnesse mocks my sight,
As though the dampe which hinders his clear flame,
Came from my breath, in that night of my shame,
When it did burn as darkness ugly eye,
When shot the star of my v^ginitie,
And if a star but by the glass appear,
I strait entreat it not to look in here;
I am already hateful to the light,
It is enough betray me not to night.
Then since my shame so much belongs to thee,
Rid me of that by only murdering me;
And let it justly to my charge be laid,
Thy royal person I would have betraide;
Thou shall not need by circumstance t'accuse me,
If I deny it, let the heavens refuse me.
My life's a blemish which does clowd thy name,
Take it away, and clear shall shine thy fame.
Yield to my suit, if ever pity moved thee,
In this show mercy, as I ever loved thee.
Notes of the Chronicle History.
Well knewst thou what a monster I would be,
When thou did build this Labyrinth for me.
IN the Cretean Labyrinth a monster was enclosed, called a Minotaur, the
history whereof is well known, but the Labyrinth was framed by Daedalus, with
so many (...)icate ways, that being entered, one could either hardly or never
return, being in manner of a maze, save that it was larger, the ways being
walld in on every side, out of the which Theseus by Ariadnes help (lending him
a clu^ of third) escaped. Some report that it was a house, having one half
bene^th the ground, another above, the chamber doors therein so deceitfully
enwrapped, and made to open so many wais, that it was held a matter almost
impossible to return.

Some have held it to have been an Allegory of man's life, true it is that the
comparison will hold, for what liker to a Labyrinth then the maze of life? But
it is affirmed by antiquity, that there was indeed such a building, though
Daedalus being a (...) applied to the workmans excellency, make it suspected:
for Daedalus is nothing else but engenious, or artificial. Hereupon it is
used among the ancient Poets, for any thing curiously wrought.

Rosamonds Labyrinth, whose ruins together with her well being paued with
square stone in the bottom, and also her Tower from which the Labirinth did
run, (are yet remaining) was altogether under ground, being vaults arched and
walld with brick and stone, almost inextricably wound one within another, by
which if at any time her lodging were laid about by the Queen, she might easly
avoid peril imminent, and if need be, by secret issues take the air abroad,
many furlongs round about Woodstock in Oxfordshire, wherein it was situated.
Thus much for Rosamonds labirinth. Whose strange Meanders turned every way.


Meander is a river in Lycia, a province of Natolia, or Asia minor, famous for
the sinuosity and often turning thereof, rising from certain hills in Maeonia,
heerevpon are intricate turnings by a transumtiue and metonimicall kind of
speech, called Meanders, for this River did so strangely path it self, that
the foot seemed to touch the head. Rose of the world, so does import my name,
Shame of the world, my lise has made the same.


It might be reported, how at Godstow, where this Rose of the world was
sumptuously interted, a certain Bishop in the visitation of his Diocese,
caused the monument which had bin erected to her honor, utterly to be
demolished, but be that severe chastisement of Rosamond then dead, at this time
also ouer-passed, lest she should seem to be the Shame of the world.

Henry to Rosamond.
WHen first the Post arrived at my Tent,
And brought the letters Rosamond had sent,
Think from his lips but what sweet comfort came,
When in mine ear he softly breathed thy name,
Straight I enjoin him of thy health to tell,
Longing to hear my Rosamond did well;
With new enqui^ies then I cut him short,
When of the same he gladly would report,
That with the earnest haste my tongue often trips,
Catching the words half spoke out of his lips:
This told, yet more I urge him to reveal,
To loose no time while I vn^ipt the seal.
The more I read, still do I err the more,
As though mistaking somewhat said before.
Missing the point, the doubtful sense is broken,
Speaking again, what I before had spoken,
Still in a swound, my heart reuiues and faints,
Twixt hopes, dispaires, twixt smiles and deep complaints.
As these sad accents sort in my desires,
Smoothe calmes, rough storms, sharp frosts, and raging (...)es,
Put on with boldness, and put back with fears,
My tongue with curses, when mine eyes with tears.
O how my heart at that black line did tremble!
That blotted paper should thy self resemble;
O were there paper but near half so white!
The Gods thereon their sacred laws would write
With pens of Angels wings, and for their ^^ke,
That heavenly Nactar, their immortal drink.
Maiesticke courage strives to have suppressed
This fearful passion stird up in my breast;
But still in vain the same I go about,
My heart must break within, or woe breaks out,
Am I at home pursued with private hate,
And war comes raging to my palace gate?
Is meager ^nuie stabbing at my throne,
Treason attending when I walk alone?
And am I branded with the curse of Rome,
And stand condemned by dreadful counsels dombe?
And by the pride of my rebellious son,
Rich Normandy with Armies overrun?
Fatal my birth, unfortunate my life,
Unkind my children, most unkind my wife.
Grief, cares, old age, suspicion to torment me,
Nothing on earth to quiet or content me,
So many woes, so many plagues to find,
Sickness of body, discontent of mind;
Hopes left, helps reft, life wronged, joy interdicted,
Banished, distressed, forsaken and afflicted,
Of all relief has fortune quite bereft me?
Only my love unto my comfort left me,
And is one beauty thought so great a thing,
To mittigate the sorrows of a King?
Barred of that choice the vulgar often prove,
Have we (then they) less privilege in love?
Is it a King, the woeful widow hears?
Is it a King dries up the Orphans tears?
Is it a King regards the Clyants cry?
Gives life to him by law condemnd to die?
Is it his care the Commonwealth that keeps,
As does the Nurse her babie whilst it sleeps?
And that poor king, of all those hopes prevented,
Unheard, vnhelp'd, vnpittied, unlamented,
Yet let me be with poverty oppressed,
Of earthly blessings robbed, and dispossessed,
Let me be scornde, rejected and reuilde,
From Kingdom, Country, and from Court exilde;
Let the worlds curse upon me still remain,
And let the last bring on the first again:
All miseries that wretched man may wound,
Leave for my comfort, only Rosamond;
For thee swift Time her speedy course does stay,
At thy command the Destinies obey;
Pity is dead that comes not from thine eyes,
And at thy feet, even mercy prostrate lies:
If I were feeble, rheumatike, or cold,
These were true signs that I were waxed old;
But I can march all day in massy steel,
Nor yet my arms unwieldy weight do feel,
Nor waked by night with bruise or bloody wound,
The tent my bed, no pillow but the ground:
For very age had I lain bedred long,
One smile of thine again could make me young.
Were there in Art a power but so divine,
As is in that sweet Angel-tongue of thine,
That great Enchantresse which once took such pains,
To force young blood in Aesons witherd veins,
And from groves, mountains, and the moorish Fen,
Used all the herbs ordainde to use of men,
And in the powerful potion that she makes,
Puts blood of men of birds, of beasts, of snakes,
Never had needed to have gone so far,
To seek the soiles where all those simples are,
One accent from thy lips, the blood more warmes,
Then all her philters, exorcismes and charms.
Thy presence has repaired in one day,
What many years and sorrows did decay,
And made fresh beauties fairest branches spring,
From wrinkled furrows of times ruining.
Even as the hungry winter-starued earth,
When she by nature labors toward her birth,
Still as the day upon the dark world creepes,
One blossom forth after another peepes,
Till the small flower whose root is now unbound,
Gets from the frostie prison of the ground,
Spreading the leaves unto the powerful noone,
Decked in fresh colors, smiles upon the sun.
Never unquiet care lodged in that breast,
Where but one thought of Rosamond did rest;
Nor thirst, nor travail, which on war attend,
E're brought the long day to desired end;
Nor yet did pale Fear, or lean Famine live,
Where hope of thee did any comfort give,
Ah what injustice then is this of thee
That thus the guiltless do condemn for me?
When only she (by means of my offense)
Redeemes thy pureness, and thy innocence,
When to our wills perforce obey they must,
That just in them, what e're in us unjust,
Of what we do, not them account we make,
The fault craves pardon for th'offenders sake,
And what to work a Princes will may merit,
Has deepst impression in the gentlest spirit;
Ift be my name that does thee so offend,
No more my self shall be mine own names friend,
And ^ft be that which thou do only hate,
That name, in my name, lastly has his date.
Say it is accu^st, and fatal, and dispraise it,
If written, blot it, if engraven, raze it.
Say that of all names it is a name of woe,
Once a Kings name, but now it is not so.
And when all this is done, I know ^vvill grieve thee,
And therefore (svveet) why should I now believe thee?
Nor should thou think those eyes with envy lower,
Which passing by thee, gaze up to thy tower;
But rather praise thine own which be so clear,
Which from the Turret like two stairs appear;
Above the sun does shine, beneath thine eye,
Mocking the heaven to make another sky,
The little stream which by thy tovver does glide,
Where often thou spendst the weary evening tide,
To view thee well his course would gladly stay,
As loath from thee to part so soon away;
And with salutes thy self would gladly greet,
And offer up those small drops at thy feet,
But finding that the envious banks restrain it,
T'excuse it self, does in this sort complain it,
And therefore this sad bubling murmr keeps,
And in this sort within the channel weeps.
And as thou do into the water look,
The fish which see thy shadow in the brook,
Forget to feed, and all amazed lie,
So daunted with the luster of thine eye.
And that sweet name which thou so much do wrong
In time shall be some famous Poets song;
And with the very sweetness of that name,
Lions and tigers men shall learn to tame.
The careful mother from her pensive breast,
With Rosamond shall bring her babe to rest;
The little birds, (by men's continual sound)
Shall learn to speak, and pr(...)le Rosamond.
And when in April they begin to sing,
Wi^h Rosamond shall welcome in the spring;
And she in whom all ra^ities are found,
Shall still be said to be a Rosamond.
The little flowers which dropping honied dew,
Which (as thou writst) do weep upon thy shue,
Not for thy fault (sweet Rosamond) do moan,
But weep for grief that thou so soon art gone,
For if thy foot ^uch Hemlocke as it goes,
That Hemlocke's made more sweeter than the Rose,
Of Jove or Neptune how they did betray,
Nor speak of I^o, or Amimone,
when she for whom Jove once became a Bull,
Comparde with thee, had been a tawny trull;
He a white Bull, and she a whiter Cow,
Yet he, nor she, near half so white as thou.
Long since (thou know) my care provided for
To lodge thee safe from jealous Ellenor,
The labyrinths conueyance guides thee so,
(Which only Vaghan, thou, and I do know)
If she do guard thee with a hundred eyes,
I have an hundred sub^ile Mercuries,
To watch that Argus which my love does keep,
Until eye, after eye, fall all to sleep.
Those stars look in by night, look in to see,
Wondering what star here on the earth should be.
As often the Moon amidst the silent night,
Has come to joy us with her friendly light,
And by the curtain helped mine eye to see
What (...) night and darkness hid from me;
When I have wished that she might ever sta^,
And other worl(...) might still enjoy the day:
What should I say? words, ^eares, and sighs be spent,
And want of (...) does further helps prevent:
My camp r^sounds with fearful shockes of war,
Yet in my breast the worser conflicts are;
Yet is my signal to the battles sound,
The blessed name of beauteous Rosamond.
Accursed be that heart, that tongue, that breath,
Should think, should speak, or whisper of thy death.
For in one smile, or lower from thy sweet eye,
Consists my life, my hope, my victory.
Sweet Woodstocke, where my Rosamond does rest,
Blessed in her, in whom thy King is blessed;
For though in France a while my body be,
(Sweet Paradise) my heart remains in thee.
Notes of the Chronicle History.
Am I at home pursued with private hate,
And war comes raging to my Palace gate?
RObert earl of Leicester, who took part with young king Henry, entered into
England with an army of 3000. Flemmings, and spoild the countries of Norsfolk
and Susfolke, being succored by many of the Kings private enemies. And am I
branded with the curse of Rome?


King Henry the second, the first Plantaginet, accused for the death of Tho.
Becket, archbishop of Canterbury, stain in the cathedral church, was accursed
by Pope Alexander, although he vrgde sufficient proof of his innocence in
the same, and offered to take upon him any penance, so he might escape the
curse and interdiction of the Realm. And by the pride of my rebellious son,
Rich Normandy with armies overrun.


Henry the young K. whom king Henry had caused to be crowned in his life (as he
hoped) both for his own good and the good of his Subjects, which indeed turned
to his own sorrow, and the trouble of the Realm, for he rebelled against him,
and raising a power, by the means of Jews king of France, and William K: of
Scots, who took part with him, invaded Normandy. Unkind my children, most
unkind my wise:


Never king more unfortunate then K: Henry, in the disobedience of his
children: first Henry, then G^ssrey, then Richard, then John, all at one time
or other, first or last, unnaturally rebelled against him; then the jealousy
of Elinor his Qu. who suspected his love to Rosamond, which grievous troubles
the devout of those times attributed to happen to him justly, for refusin: to
take on him the government of Jerusalem, offered to him by the patriarch there;
which country was mightily afflicted by the Souldane. Which only Vaghan, thou,
and I do know.


This Vaghan was a Knight whom the King exceedingly loved, who kept the Palace
at Woodstocke, and much of the Kings jewels and treasure, to whom the King
committed many of his secrets, and in whom he reposed such trust, that he
durst commit his love unto his charge.

King John to Matilda.
The Argument.
After that King John had assayed by all means possible, to win the fair and
chaste Matilda, to his unchaste and unlawful bed and by unjust courses and
false accusation had banished the Lord Robert Fitzwater her noble Father, and
many other of his alies, who justly withstood the desire of this wanton King,
seeking the dishonor of his fair and virtuous daughter; this chaste Lady,
still solicited by this lascivious King, flies unto Dunmow in Essex, where in a
Nunnery she becomes a Nun, whether the King (still persisting in his suit)
solicites her by his Epistle; her reply confirms her vowed and invincible
chastity, making known to the King her pure unspotted thoughts.

WHen these my Letters come unto thy view,
Think them not forcde, or faind, or strange, or new,
Thou know no way, no means, no course exempted,
Left now unsought, vnproou'd, or unattempted,
All rules, regardes, all secret helps of Art,
What knowledge, wit, experience can impart;
And in the old worlds Ceremonies doted,
Good days for love, times, hours and minutes noted;
And where Art left, love teaches more to find,
By signs in presence to express the mind.
Often has mine eye told thine eye, beauty grieved it,
And begd but for one look to have relieved it,
And still with thine eyes motion, mine eye moved,
Laboring for mercy, telling how it loved.
If blushed, I blushed, thy cheek pale, pale was mine,
My red, thy red, my whiteness answered thine;
If sighed, I sighed, alike both passion prove,
But thy sigh is for grief, my sigh for love;
If a word past, that insufficient were.
To help that word, mine eyes let forth a tear,
And if that tear did dull or senseless prove,
My heart would fetch a sigh, to make it move.
Often in thy face, one favor from the rest
I singled forth, that likes my fancy best;
This likes me most, another likes me more,
A third exceeding both those likde before;
Then one that does derive all wonder thence,
Then one whose rarenes passes excellence.
Whilst I behold thy Globe like rolling eye,
Thy lovely cheek (me thinks) stands smiling by
And tells me, those but shadows and supposes,
And bids me thither come and gather Roses;
Looking on that, thy brow does call to me
To come to it, if wonders I will see:
Now have I done, and now thy dimpled chinne
Again does tell me I but new begin,
And bids me yet to look upon thy lip,
Lest wondering least, the greatst I ouerslip;
My gazing eye, on this and this does sease,
Which surffets, yet cannot desire appease.
Then like I brown, (O lovely brown thy hair)
Only in brownenesse, beauty dwells there.
Then love I black, thine eye, ball black as jet,
Then clear, that ball is there in cristall set,
Then white, but snow, nor swan, nor yuorie please,
Then are thy teeth more whiter then all these;
In brown, in black, in pureness, and in white,
All love, all sweetes, all rarenes, all delight;
Thus thou vile thief, my stolen heart hence do carry,
And now thou fliest into a Sanctuary;
Fie peevish gerle, ungrateful unto Nature,
Did she to this end frame thee such a creature,
That thou her glory should increase thereby,
And thou aloue do scorn society?
Why, heaven made beauty like herself to view,
Not to be locked up in a smoaky mew,
A rosie-tainted feature is heavens gold,
Which all men joy to touch, all to behold.
It was enacted when the world begun,
That so rare beauty should not live a Nun.
But if this vow thou needs wilt undertake,
O were mine arms a Cloister for thy sake,
Still may his pains for ever be augmented,
This superstition that at first invented,
I^l might he thrive, that brought this custom hither,
That holy people might not live together.
A happy time, a good world was it then,
When holy women lived with holy men;
But kings in this yet priuiledgde may be,
I Will be a Monk, so I may live with thee.
Who would no(...)se to ring the mornings knell,
When thy sweet lips might be the sacring bell?
Or what is he not willingly would fast,
That on those lips might feast his lips at last?
Who unto Mattens early would not rise,
That might read by the light of thy fair eyes?
On worldly pleasures who would ever look,
That had thy curles his beads, thy brows his book?
Were thou the cross, to thee who would not creep?
And wish the cross, still in his arms to keep.
Sweet gerle, I Will take this holy habit on me,
Of mere devotion that is come upon me,
Holy Matilda, thou the Saint of mine,
I Will be thy servant, and my bed thy shrine.
When I do offer, be thy breast the Altar,
And when I pray thy (...) shall be my Psalter.
The beads that we will bid shall be sweet kisses,
Which we will number, if one pleasure misses,
And when an Auie comes to say Amen,
We will begin, and tell them over again,
Now all good fortune give me happy thrift,
As I should joy t'absol^e thee after shrift.
But see how much I do my self beguile,
And do mistake thy meaning all this while,
Thou tookst this vow to equal my desire
Because thou would have me to be a Friar,
And that we two should comfort one another,
A holy sister, and a holy brother,
Thou as a Votresse unto me alone,
She is most chaste that's but enjoyed of one,
Yea, now thy true devotion do I find,
And sure in this I much commend thy mind,
Else here thou do but ill ensample give,
And in a Nunry thus thou should not live.
Is It possible the house that thou art in
Should not be tucht, (though with a venial sin)
When such a she-priest comes her mass to say,
Twenty to one they all forget to pray?
Well may we wish they would their hearts amend,
When we be witness that their eyes offend,
All creatures have desires, or else some lie,
Let them think so that will, so will not I.
Do thou not think our ancestors were wise,
That these religious Cells did first devise?
As Hospitalls were for the sore and sick,
These for the crook'd, the hault, the stigmaticke,
Lest that their seed marked with deformity,
Should be a blemish to posterity.
Would heaven her beauty should be hid from sight,
Nere would she thus her self adorn with light,
With sparkling lamps; nor would she paint her throne,
But she delights to be gazed upon:
And when the golden glorious Sun goes down,
Would she put on her star-bestudded crown,
And in her masking suit the spangled sky,
Come forth to bride it in her reuelrie,
And gave this gift to all things in creation,
That they in this should imitate her fashion.
All things that fair, that pure, that glorious been,
Offer themselves of purpose to be seen;
In sinks and vaults, the ugly toades do dwell,
The devils since most ugly, they in hell:
Our mother Earth, nere glorious in her fruit,
Till by the Sun clad in her Tinsell suit.
Nor does she ever smile him in the face,
Till in his glorious arms he her embrace;
Which proves she has a soul, sense, and delight
Of generations feeling appetite.
Well hypocrite (in faith) would thou confess,
What ere thy tongue say, thy heart says no less.
Note but this one thing, (if naught else persuade)
Nature, of all things male, and female made,
Showing her self in our proportion plain,
For never made she any thing in vain;
For as thou art, should any have been thus,
She would have left ensample unto us.
The Turtle that is so true and chaste in love,
Shows by her mate something the spirit does move;
The Arabian bird that never is but one,
Is only chaste, because she is alone:
But had our mother Nature made them two,
They would have done as Doves and Sparrowes do;
But therefore made a Martire in desire,
And does her penance lastly in the fire;
So may they all be roasted quick that be
Apostataes to Nature, as is she.
Find me but one so young, so fair, so free,
(Wood, sude and sought, by him that now seeks thee)
But of thy mind, and here I undertake,
Strait to erect a Nunry for her sake;
O had thou tasted of these rare delights,
Ordainde each where to please great Princes sights,
To have their beauties and their wits admirde,
(Which is by nature of your sex desirde)
Attended by our trains, our pomp, our port,
Like Gods adorde abroad, kne^^d to in Court,
To be saluted with the cheerful cry,
Of highness, grace, and sovereign majesty;
But unto them that know not pleasures price,
All Is one, a prison, and a Paradise.
If in a dungeon, closde up from the light,
There is no difference twixt the day and night,
Whose palate never tasted dainty cates,
Thinks homely dishes princely delicates.
Alas poor girl, I pity thine estate,
That now thus long have lived disconsolate;
Why now at length let yet thy heart relent,
And call thy father back from banishment;
And with those princely honors here inuest him,
That awkward love, not hate has dispossessed him.
Call from exile, thy dear alies and friends,
To whom the fury of my grief extends;
And if thou take my counsel in this case,
I make no doubt thou shall have better grace,
And leave the Dunmow, that accursed Cell,
There let black night and melancholy dwell;
Come to the Court, where all joys shall receive thee,
And till that hour, yet with my grief I leave thee.
Notes of the Chronicle History.
THis Epistle of King John to Matilda, is much more poetical then historical,
making no mention at al of the occurrents of the time, or state, touching only
his love to her, and the extreamitie of his passions forced by his desires,
rightly fashioning the humor of this king, as has bin truly noted by the most
authnticall Writers; whose nature and disposition is truliest discerned in the
course of his love; first jesting at the ceremonies of the services of those
times, then going about by all strong and probable arguments, to reduce her to
pleasures and delights; next with promises of honor, which he thinks to be
last and greatest mean, and to have greatest power in her sex, with promise
of calling home of her friends, which he thought might be a great inducement to
his desires.

Matilda to King John.
NO sooner I received thy letters here,
Before I knew from whom, or whence they were
But sudden fear my bloodlesse veins does fill,
As though divining of some future ill:
And in a shiuering ecstasy I stood,
A chilly coldness runs through all my blood;
Opening thy letters, I shut up my rest,
And let strange cares into my quiet breast,
As though thy hard vnpittying hand had sent me,
Some new devised torture to torment me;
Well had I hoped, I had been now forgot,
Cast out with those things thou remembrest not:
And that proud beauty which enforst me hither,
Had with my name now perished together:
But O (I see) our hoped good deceiues us,
But what we would forgo, that seldom leaves us;
Thy blamefull lines bespotted so with sin,
Mine eyes would cleanse, ere they to read begin.
But I to wash an Indian go about,
For ill so hard set on, is hard got out.
I once determinde still to have been mute,
Only by silence to refell thy suit,
But this again did alter mine intent,
For some will say, that silence does consent:
Desire, with small encouraging grows bold,
And Hope, of every little thing takes hold.
I set me down at large to write my mind,
But now, nor pen, nor paper can I find;
For dread and passion, are so powerful over me,
That I discern not things that stand before me:
Finding the pen, the paper, and the wax,
This at command, and now invention lacks;
This sentence serves, and that my hand out-strikes,
That pleases well, and this as much mislikes,
I write, indite, I point, I raze, I quote,
I enterline, I blot, correct, I note;
I hope, despair, take courage, faint, disdain,
I make, allege, I imitate, I feign:
Now thus it must be, and now thus, and thus,
Bold, shame-fac'd, fearelesse, doubtful, timorous;
My faint hand writing, when my full eye reeds,
From every word strange passion still proceeds.
O! when the soul is fettered once in woe,
It Is strange what humors it does force us to;
A tear does drown a tear, sigh, sigh does smother,
This hinders that, that interrupts the other:
Th'ouer-watched weakness of a sick conceit,
Is that which makes small beauty seem so great,
Like things which hid in troubled waters lie,
Which crook'd, seem straight, if straight, seem contrary:
And thus our vain imagination shows it,
As it conceiues it, not as judgment knows it,
(As in a Mirrhor, if the same be true)
Such as your likeness, justly such are you:
But as you change your self, it changes there,
And shows you as you are, not as you were;
And with your motion does your shadow move,
If frown or smile; such the conceit of love.
Why tell me, is it possible the mind
A form in all deformity should find?
Within the compass of man's face we see,
How many sorts of several favors be;
And that the chin, the nose, the brow, the eye,
If great, if small flat, sharp, or if awry,
Alters proportion, alters the grace,
And makes a mighty difference in the face;
And in the world, scarce two so likely are,
One with the other which if you compare,
But being set before you both together,
A judging sight does soon distinguish either.
How woman like a weakness is it then?
O what strange madness so possesses men!
Bereft of sense; such senseless wonders seeing,
Without form, fashion, certainty, or being?
For which so many die to live in anguish,
Yet cannot live, if thus they should not languish;
That comfort yeeldes not, and yet hope denies not,
A life that lives not, and a death that dies not;
That hates us most, when most it speaks us fair,
Does promise all things, always paies with air,
Yet sometime does our greatest grief appease,
To double sorrow after little ease.
Like that which thy lascivious will does crave,
Which if once had, thou never more can have;
Which if thou get, in getting thou do waste it,
Taken, is lost, and perrish'd if thou have it;
Which if thou gain'st, thou never the more have won,
I loosing nothing, yet a(...) quite undone;
And yet of that, if that a King deprave me,
No King restores, though he a kingdom gave me.
Do thou of father and of friends deprive me?
And take thou from me all that heaven did give me?
What Nature claims by blood, alies or neerenesse,
Or friendship challenge, by regard or deerenesse.
Make me an Orphan ere my father die,
A woeful widow in virginity?
Is thy unbridled lust the cause of all?
And now thy flattering tongue bewailes my fall.
The dead man's grave with fained tears to fill,
So the devouring Crocodile does kill,
To harbor hate in show of sweetest things,
So in the Rose the poisned serpent stings.
To lurk far off, yet lodge destruction by,
The Basiliske does poison with the eye;
To call for aid, and then to lie in wait,
So the Hiena murders by deceit;
By sweet inticements, sudden death to bring,
So from the rocks th'alluring Mir-maides sing;
In greatest wants, t'inflict the greatest woe,
This is the utmost tyranny can do.
But where (I see) the tempest thus preuailes,
What use of ankors, or what need of sails?
Above us blustering winds and dreadful thunder,
The waters gape for our destruction under;
Here on this side the furious billows fly,
There rocks, there sands, and dangerous whirl-poole lie.
Is this the mean that mightiness approues?
And in this sort do Princes woo their loves?
Mildness would better suit with majesty,
Then rash revenge and rough severity.
O in what safety Temperance does rest,
Obtaining harbor in a sovereign breast.
Which if so praisefull in the meanest men,
In powerful Kings, how glorious is it then?
Alas, and fled I hither from my so,
That innocence should be betrayed so?
Is Court and Country both her enemy,
And no place found to shroud in chastity?
Each house for lust a harbor, and an Inn,
And every City a receite for sin;
And all do pity beauty in distress,
If beauty chaste, then only pitiless,
Thus is she made a tempting stale to lust,
Or vnreleeued, nedsly perrish must.
Lascivious Poets which abuse the truth,
Which often teach age to sin, infecting youth,
For the unchaste make trees and stones to mourn,
Or as they please, to other shapes do turn:
Cinyras daughter, whose incestuous mind,
Made her wrong Nature and dishonor Kind;
Long since by them is turnde into a Mir,
Whose dropping liquor ever weeps for her;
And in a fountain, Biblis does deplore
Her fault so vile and monsterous before:
Silla, which once her father did betray,
Is now a bird, (if all be true they say.)
She that with Phoebus did the foul offense,
Now metamorphosde into frankencense.
Other, to flowers, to odors, and to gum,
At least Jove's leman is a star become;
And more; they feign a thousand fond excuses,
To hide their scapes, and cover their abuses,
The virgin only they obscure and hide
Whilst the unchaste, by them are deifide;
Yet if a Vestals name be once expressed,
She must be set together with the rest.
I am not now, as when thou sawst me last,
That favor soon is vanished and past;
That Rosie-blush, lapt in a Lilly-vale,
Now with the morphew ouer-growne and pale,
And down my cheeks with showers of swelling tears,
Remain the furrows that continuance wears,
And in the circles of my withered eyes,
In aged wrinckles Beauty buried lies;
And in my grace, my presence, gesture, cheer,
Ruin, distress, woe, anguish, does appear.
That breast, that hand, that cheek, that eye, that brow,
Faded, decayed, fallen, darkened, wrinckled now;
Such was my beauty once, now is it such,
Once thought most rare, now altered more than much,
Nor I regard all that thou can protest,
My vovv is taken, I a Nun professed.
This vestall habit does content me more,
Then all the robes that yet I ever wore.
Had Rosamond, (a recluse of our sort)
Taken our Cloister, left the wanton Court,
Shadowing that beauty with a holy vale,
Which she (alas) too loosely set to sale,
She need not like an ugly Minotaur,
Have been looked up from jealious Ellenor,
But bin as famous by thy mothers wrongs,
As by thy father subject to all tongues.
To shadow sin, might can the most pretend,
Kings, but the conscience, all things can defend.
A stronger hand restraines our wilful powers,
A will must rule above this will of ours,
Not following what our vain desires do woo
For virtues sake, but what we (only) do.
And has my father chose to live exilde,
Before his eyes should see my youth defilde?
And to withstand a tyrants lewd desire,
Beheld his towers and castles razde with fire:
Yet never tucht with grief, so only I,
Exempt from shame might with true honor die.
And shall this jewel which so deerly cost,
Now after all, by my dishonor lost?
No, no, his reverend words, his holy tears,
Yet in my soul too deep impression bears
His latest farewell at his last depart,
More deeply is ingraued in my heart,
Nor shall that blot, by me his name shall have,
Bring his gray hairs with sorrow to his grave,
Better his tears to fall upon my tomb,
Then for my birth to curse my mothers womb.
Though Dunmow give no refuge here at all.
Dunmow can give my body burial.
If all remorcelesse, no teare-shedding eye,
My self will moan my self; so live, so die.
Notes of the Chronicle history.
THis Epistle contains no particular points of History, more than the
generality of the argument lays open; for after the banishment of the Lord
Robert Fitzwater, and that Matilda was become a Recluse at Dunmow, (from whence
this reply is imagined to be written.) the King still earnestly persisting in
his suit. Matild. (...)ith this chaste and constant denial, hopes yet at
length to find some comfortable remedy, and to rid her self of doubts, by
taking upon her this monasticke habit, and to show that she still bears in
mind his former cruelty, bred by the impatience of his lust, she remembers
him of her fathers banishment, and the lawless exile of her alies and friends.
Do thou of father and of friends deprive me?


Then complaining of her distress, that flying thither, thinking there to find
relief, she sees herself most assaild where she hoped to have found most
safety. Alas, and fled I hither from my for,
That, etc.


After again, standing upon the precise points of conscience, not to cast off
this habit she had taken. My vow in taken, I a Nun professed.


And at last laying open more particularly the miseries sustaind by her father
in England, the burning of his Castles and houses, which she proves to be for
her sake: as respecting only her honor, more then his native Country and his
own fortunes. And to withstand a tyrants lewd desire,
Beheld his Towers and Castles set on fire.


Knitting up her Epistle with a great and constant resolution. Though Dunmow
give no refuge here at all,
Dunmow can give my body burial.


Finis.
To the virtuous Lady, the Lady Anne Harrington, wife to the honorable
Gentleman, sir John Harrington Knight.
MY singular good Lady, your many virtues known in general to all, and your
gracious favors to my unworthy self, have confirmed that in me, which before I
knew you, I only saw by the light of other men's judgments. Honor seated in
your breast, finds her self adorned as in a rich Palace, making that
excellent which makes her admirable; which like the Sun (from thence)
begets most precious things of this earthly world, only by the virtue of
his rays, not the nature of the mould. Worth is best discerned by the worthy,
dejected minds want that pure fire which should give vigor to virtue. I refer
to your great thoughts (the impartial Judges of true affection) the unfeigned
zeal I have ever borne to your honorable service; and so rest your Ladyships
humbly to command.

Mich: Drayton.

Queen Isabell to Mortimer.
The Argument.
Queen Isabel, (the wife of Edward the second, called Edward Carnaruan,)
being the daughter of Philip de Beau, King of France, forsaken by the King her
husband, who delighted only in the company of Piers Gaueston, his minion and
favorite, and after his death seduced by the evil counsel of the Spencers. This
Queen thus left by her husband, even in the glory of her youth, drew into her
especial favor Roger Mortimer, Lord of Wigmore, a man of a mighty and
invincible spirit. This Lord Mortimer rising in arms against the King with
Thomas Earl of Lancaster, and the Barons, was taken ere he could gather his
power, and by the King committed to the tower of London. During his
imprisonment, he ordained a feast in honor of his birthday, to which he
invited Sir Stephen Segraue, Lieutenant of the Tower, and the rest of the
officers, where, by means of a drink prepared by the Queen, he cast them all
into a heavy sleep, and with Ladders of coards being ready prepared for the
purpose, he escapes and flies into France, whither she sends this Epistie,
complaining her own misfortunes, and greatly rejoicing at his safe escape.

THough such sweet comfort comes not now from her
As Englands Queen has sent to Mortimer.
Yet what that wants, which might my power approve
If lines can bring, this shall supply with love,
Me thinks affliction should not fright me so,
No^ should resume these sundry shapes of woe;
But when I feign would find the cause of this,
Thy absence shows me where the error is.
Often when I think of thy departing hence,
Sad sorrow then possesses every sense:
But finding thy dear blood preserved thereby,
And in thy life, my long-wisht liberty,
With that sweet thought my selff I only please
Amidst my grief, which sometimes gives me case,
Thus do extremest ills a joy possess,
And one woe makes another woe seem less.
That blessed night, that milde-aspected hour,
Wherein thou madst escape out of the Tower,
Shall consecrated evermore remain;
What gentle Planet in that hour did reign;
And shall be happy in the birth of men,
Which was chief lord of the Ascendant then.
O how I feared that sleepy juice I sent,
Might yet want power to further thine intent!
Or that some unseen mystery might lu^ke,
Which wanting order, kindly should not work;
Often did I wish those dreadful poysned lees,
That closde the euer-waking Dragons eyes,
Or I had had those sence-ber^auing stalks,
That grow in shady Proserpines dark walks;
Or those black weeds on Lethe banks below,
Or Lunary that does on Latmus flow;
Often did I fear this moist and soggy clime,
Or that the earth, waxt barren now with time,
Should not have herbs to help me in this case,
Such as do thrive on Indiaes parched face,
That morrow, when the blessed Sun did rise,
And shut the liddes of all heavens lesser eyes,
Forth from my palace by a secret staire,
I steal to Thames, as though to take the air;
And ask the gentle flood as it does glide,
Or thou did pass or perish by the tide?
If thou did perish, I desire the stream
To lay thee softly on her silver teame,
And bring thee to me to the quiet shore,
That with her tears thou might have some tears more.
When suddenly does rise a rougher gale,
With that (me thinks) the troubled waves look pale,
And sighing with that little gust that blows,
With this remembrance seem to knit their brows.
Even as this so daine passion does affright me,
The cheerful Sun breaks from a cloud to light me,
Then does the bottom evident appear,
As it would show me, that thou wast not there,
Whenas the water flowing where I stand,
Does seem to tell me, Thou art safe on land.
Did Bulloyne once a festival prepare,
For England, Almaine, Cicile, and Navarre?
When France envied those buildings (only blessed)
Gracde with the Orgies of my Bridal feast,
That English Edward should refuse my bed,
For that incestuous shameless Ganimed?
And in my place, upon his regal throne,
To set that gerle-boy, wanton Gaueston.
Betwixt the feature of my face and his,
My glass assures me no such difference is,
That a foul witches bastard should thereby
Be thought more worthy of his love than I.
What does avail us to be Princes heirs,
When we can boast our birth is only theirs?
When base dissembling flatterers shall deceive us,
Of all our famous ancestors did leave us;
And of our princely jewels and our dowres,
We but enjoy the least of what is ours;
when minions heads must wear our monarks crowns
To raise up dunghills with our famous towns;
When beggars-brats are wrapped in rich perfumes,
Their buzzard wings impt with our Eagles plumes;
And matched with the brave issue of our blood,
All the kingdom to their crauand brood.
Did Longshankes purchase with his conquering hand,
Albania, Gascoyne, Cambria, Ireland?
That young Carnarnan (his unhappy son)
Should give away all that his father won?
To back a stranger, prowdly bearing down
The brake alies and branches of the crown?
And did great Edward on his deathbed give
This charge to them which afterward should live,
That that proud Gascoyne banished the land,
No more should tread upon the English sand?
And have these great Lords in the quarrel stood,
And sealde his last will with their decrest blood,
That after all this fearful massacre,
The fall of Beauchamp, Lacy, Lancaster,
Another faithless favorite should arise,
To cloud the sun of our Nobilities?
And gloried I in Gauestons great fall,
That now a Spenser should succeed in all?
And that his ashes should another breed,
Which in his place and Empire should succeed;
That wanting one a kingdoms wealth to spend,
Of what that left, this now shall make an end;
To waste all that our father won before,
Nor leave our son a sword to conquer more.
Thus but in vain we fondly do resist,
Where power can do (even) all things as it list,
And with unjust men to debate of laws,
Is to give power to hurt a rightful cause;
Whilst Parlements must still redress their wrongs,
And we must starve for what to us belongs;
Our wealth but fuel to their fond excess,
And we must fast to feast their wantonness.
Think thou our wrongs then insufficient are,
To move our brother to religious war?
And if they were, yet Edward does detain
Homage for Pontiu, Guyne, and Aquytaine;
And if not that, yet has he broke the truce,
Thus all accurre, to put back all excuse.
The sisters wrong, ioynde with the brothers right,
Me thinks might urge him in this cause to fight.
Be all those people senseless of our harms,
Which for our Country ought have managed arms?
Is the brave Normans courage now forgot?
Or the bold Britains lost the use of shot?
The big-bonde Almaines, and stowt Brabanders,
Their warlike Pikes, and sharpe-edg'd Semiters?
Or do the Pickards let their Crosse-bowes lie,
Once like the Centaurs of old Thessalie?
Or if a valiant Leader be their lack,
Where thou art present, who should drive them back?
I do conjure thee by what is most dear,
By that great name of famous Mortimer,
By ancient Wigmors honorable cr^st,
The tombs where all thy famous grand-sires rest;
Or if than these, what more may thee approve,
Even by those vows of thy unfeigned love,
That thy great hopes may move the Christian King,
By foreign arms some comfort yet to bring,
To curb the power of traitors that rebel,
Against the right of princely Isabell.
Vain witlesle woman, why should I desire.
To add more heat to thy immortal fire?
To urge thee by the violence of hate,
To shake the pillars of thine own estate,
When whatsoever we intend to do,
To our misfortune ever sorts unto;
And nothing else remains for us beside,
But tears and coffins (only) to provide,
When still so long as Borough bears that name,
Time shall not blot out our deserved shame;
And whilst clear Trent her wonted course shall keep,
For our sad fall, her crystal drops shall weep.
All see our ruin on our backs is thrown,
And to our selves our sorrows are our own.
And Torlton now whose counsel should direct,
The first of all is slaundred with suspect;
For dangerous things dissembled seldom are,
Which many eyes attend with busy care.
What should I say? My griefs do still renew,
And but begin when I should bid adieu,
Few be my words, but manifold my woe,
And still I stay, the more I shiue to go.
As accents issue forth, griefs enter in,
And where I end, me thinks I but begin;
Till then fair time some greater good affords,
Take my loves payment in these ayrie words.

Might yet want power to further thine intent.

And ask the gentle stream as it does glide,

For England, Almaine, Cicile and Niuarr^?

To set that girle-boy, wanton Gaueston.


Noting the effeminacie and luxurious wantonness of Gaueston, the Kings

That after all this fearful massaker,
The fall of Beuchamp, Lasy, Lancaster.


Thomas, Earl of Lancaster, Guy, Earl of Warwick, and Henry Earl of

the ruin of so many Princes. And gloried I in Gauestons great fall,
That now a Spenser should succeed in all.


The two Hugh Spensers, the father and the son, after the death of Gaueston,

Wigmore in the marches of Wales, was the ancient house of the Mortimers, that

This was Adam Torlton, Bishop of Herford, that great Polititia^, who so highly

Mortimer to Queen Isabell.
AS thy salutes my sorrows do adiourne
So back to thee their interest I turn;
Though not in so great bounty (I confess)
As thy heroicke princely lines express:
For how should comfort issue from the breath,
Of one condemned, and long lodged up in death?
From murders rage thou did me once repriue,
Now in exile, my hopes thou do revive;
Twice all was taken, twice thou all did give,
And thus twice dead, thou make me twice to live.
This double life of mine, your only due,
You gave to me; I gave it back to you;
Never my escape had, I aduentur'd thus,
As did the sky-attempting Daedalus;
And yet to give more safety to my flight,
Have made a night of day, a day of night.
Nor had I backed the proud aspiring wall,
Which held without, my hopes, within, my fall,
Leaving the cords to tell where I had gone,
For gazing eyes with fear to look upon;
But that thy beauty (by a power divine)
Breathed a new life into this spirit of mine.
Drawn by the Sun of thy celestial eyes,
With fiery wings made passage through the skies,
The heavens did seem the charge of me to take,
And sea and land be friend me for thy sake;
Thames stopped her tide, to make me way to go,
As thou had charged her that it should be so;
The hollow marmuring winds their due time kept,
As they had rocked the world, while all things slept;
One billow bore me, and another drove me,
This strove to help me, and that strove to save me;
The brisling reeds moved with the air did chide me,
As they would tell me that they meant to hide me;
The pale-faced night beheld thy heavy cheer,
And would not let one little star appear,
But over all her smokie maptle hurled,
And in thick vapors mu(...)d up the world;
And the pure air became so calm and still,
As it had been obedient to my will;
And every thing disposde unto my rest,
As when on Seas the Alcion buildes her nest.
When those rough waves which late with fury rusht,
Slide smoothely on, and suddenly are hushed;
Nor Neptune lets his surges out so long,
As Nature is in bringing forth her young;
Nor let the Spencers glory in my chance,
That I should live an exile here in France:
That I from England banished should be,
But England rather banished from me:
More were her want, France our great blood should bear,
Then Englands loss should be to Mortimer.
My grandsire was the first since Arthurs reign,
That the Round-table rectifide again;
To whose great Court at Kenelworth did come,
The peerless knighthood of all Christendom:
Whose princely order, honored England more,
Then all the Conquests she atchiu'd before.
Never durst Scot set foot in English ground,
Nor on his back did English bear a wound,
Whilst Wigmore flourisht in our princely hopes,
And whilst our Ensign marched with Edwards troops:
Whilst famous Longshankes bones (in Fortunes scorn)
As sacred relics to the field were borne;
Nor ever did the valiant English doubt,
Whilst our brave battles guarded them about.
Nor did our wives and woeful mothers mourn,
The English blood that stained Banocksburne,
Whilst with his Minions sporting in his Tent,
Whole days and nights in banqueting were spent,
Until the Scots (which vndersafegard stood)
Made lavish havoc of the English blood?
And battered helmes lay scattered on the shore,
Where they in conquest had been borne before.
A thousand kingdoms will we seek from far,
As many Nations waste with civil war,
Where the disheuel'd ghastly Sea-nimph sings,
Or well-rigd ships shall stretch their swelling wings,
And drag their ankors through the sandy foam,
About the world in every clime to rome,
And those vnchristned Countries call our own,
Where scarce the name of England has bin known;
And in the dead-sea sink our houses fame,
From whose stern waves we first derived our name,
Before foul blacke-mouth'd infamy shall sing,
That Mortimer e're stooped unto a King.
And we will turn sterne-visag'd fury back,
To seek his spoil, who sought our utter sack:
And come to beard him in our native I Will,
E're he march forth to follow our exile.
And after all these boistrous stormy shockes,
Yet will we grapple with the chaulkie rocks:
Nor will we come like Pirates, or like the eues,
From mountains, forests, or sea-bordering Cleeues,
But fright the air with terror (when we come)
Of the stern trumpet, and the bellowing drum:
And in the field advance our plumy Crest,
And march upon fair Englands flowrie breast;
And Thames which once we for our life did swim,
Shaking our dewy tresses on her brim,
Shall bear my navy; vaunting in her pride,
Falling from Tanet with the powerful tide;
Which fertile Essex, and fair Kent shall see,
Spreading herflags along the pleasant lee,
When on her stemming poope she prowdly bears
The famous Ensigns of the Belgicke Peers,
And for the hateful sacrilegious sin,
Which by the Pope he stands accursed in,
The Canon text shall have a common gloss,
Receipts in parcels, shall be paid in gross.
This doctrine preachde, who from the Church does take,
At least shall trebble restitution make:
For which Rome sends her curses out from far,
Through the stern throat of terror-breathing war,
Till to th'vnpeopled shores she brings supplies
Of those industrious Roman Colonies.
And for his homage, by the which of old
Proud Edward Guyne and Aquitaine does hold.
Charles by inuasiue arms again shall take,
And send the English forces over the lake;
When Edwards fortune stands upon this chance,
To loose in England, or expulsde from France;
And all those towns great Longshankes left his son,
Now lost again, which once his father won.
Within their strong percullizde Ports shall lie,
And from their walls his sieges shall defy.
And by that firm and undissolved knot,
Betwixt their neighboring French, and bordring Scot,
Bruise now shall bring his Red-shanks from the seas,
From th'Iled Oreads, and the Hebrydes.
And to his western havens give free pass,
To land the warlike Irish Galiglasse,
Marching from Tweede to swelling Humber sands,
Wasting along the Northern netherlands.
And wanting those which should his power sustain,
Consumde with slaughter in his bloody reign,
Our warlike sword shall drive him from his throne,
Where he shall lie for us to tread upon;
And those great lords now after their attaints,
Canonized amongst the English Saints;
And by the superstitious people thought,
That by their Relics, miracles are wrought,
And think that flood much virtue does retain,
Which took the blood of famous Bohun slain;
Continuing the remembrance of the thing,
To make the people more abhor their King.
Nor shall a Spenser (be he never so great)
Possess our Wigmore, our renowned seat.
To raze the ancient Trophies of our race,
With our deserts their monuments to grace;
Nor shall he lead our valiant marchers forth,
To make the Spensers famous in the North;
Nor be the Gardants of the British pales,
Defending England, and preserving Wales.
At first our troubles easily reculde;
But now grown headstrong hardly to be ruled;
With grauest counsel all must be directed,
Where plainest shows are openly suspected;
For where mis-hap our error does assault,
There does it eassiest make us see our fault,
Then (sweet) repress all fond and wilful spleen,
Two things to be a woman, and a Queen;
Keep close the cindars, lest the fire should burn,
It is not this which yet must serve our turn.
And if I do not much mistake the thing,
The next supply shall greater comfort bring;
Till when I leave my Princess for a while,
Live thou in rest, though I live in exile.

At what time the two Mortimers, this Roger lord of Wigmore and his uncle Roger

With strong ladders made of cords provided him for the purpose, he escaped out

desperate attempt they bred astonishment to the beholders. Nor let the Spensers

The two Hugh Spensers, the father, and the son, then being so highly favored
