Finis.
To the virtuous Lady, the Lady Francis Goodere, wife to sir Henry Goodere,
Knight.
MY very gracious and good Mistress, the love and duty I bare unto your father
whilst he lived, now after his decease is to your hereditarie; to whom by the
blessing of your birth he left his virtues. Who bequeathed you those which were
his, gave you whatsoever good is mine, as devoted to his, he being gone, whom
I honored so much whilst he lived; which you may justly challenge by all
laws of thankfulness. My self having been a witness of your excellent
education, and mild disposition (as I may say) ever from your Cradle, dedicate
this Epistle of this virtuous and godly Lady to your self; so like her in all
perfection, both of wisdom and learning, which I pray you accept, till time
shall enable me to leave you some greater monument of my love.

M. Drayton.

The Lady Jane Gray to the Lord Gilford Dudley.
The Argument.
After the death of that virtuous young Prince King Edward the sixth, the son
of that famous King Henry the eight, Jane the daughter of Henry Gray, Duke of
Suffolk by the consent of John Dudley Duke of Northumberland, was proclaimed
Queen of England, being married to Gilford Dudley, the fourth son of the
foresaid Duke of Northumberland; which match was concluded by their ambitious
fathers, who went about by this means, to bring the Crown unto their children
and to dispossess the Princess Mary eldest daughter to King Henry the eight,
heir to King Edward her brother. Queen Mary rising in Arms to claim her
rightful Crown, takes the said Jane Gray and the Lord Gilford her husband,
being lodged in the Tower for their more safety, which place being lastly
their Palace, by this means became their prison, where being severed in
sundry prisons, they write these Epistles one to another.

MIne own dear Lord, since thou art locked from me,
In this disguise my love must steal to thee,
Since to renew all loves, all kindness past,
This refuge scarcely left, yet this the last.
My Keeper coming, I of thee enquire,
Who with thy greeting answers my desire,
Which my tongue willing to return again,
Grief stops my words, and I but strive in vain;
Wherewith amazde, away in haste he goes,
When through my lips, my heart thrusts forth my woes,
Whenas the doors that make a doleful sound,
Drive back my words, that in the noise are drowned,
Which somewhat hushed, the Echo does record,
And twice or thrice reiterates my word;
When like an adverse wind in Isis course,
Against the tide bending his boistrous force;
But when the flood has wrought it self about,
He following on, does headlong thrust it out;
Thus strive my fighes with tears e're they begin,
And breaking out, again sighs drive them in.
A thousand forms present my troubled thought,
Yet prove abortiue when they forth are brought,
The depth of woe with words we hardely sound,
Sorrow is so insensibly profound:
As tears do fall and rise, sighs come and go,
So do these numbers ebb, so do they flow.
These briny tears do make my incke look pale,
My incke clothes tears in this sad mourning veil,
The letters mourners, weep with my dim eye,
The paper pale, grieved at my misery.
Yet miserable our selves, why should we deem,
Since none is so, but in his own esteem?
Who in distress from resolution flies,
Is rightly said, to yield to miseries;
They which begot us, did beget this sin,
They first begun, what did our grief begin,
we tasted not, it was they which did rebel,
Not our offense, but in their fall we fell;
They which a crown would to my Lord have linckt,
All hope of life and liberty extinct;
A subject borne, a Sovereign to have been,
Has made me now, nor subject, nor a Queen.
Ah vile ambition, how do thou deceive us,
which show us heaven and yet in hell do leave us?
Seldom untouched does innocence escape,
when error comes in good counsels shape,
A lawful title countercheckes proud might,
The weakest things become strong props to right.
Then my dear Lord, although affliction grieve us,
Yet let our spotless innocence relieve us.
Death but an acted passion does appear,
where truth gives courage, and the conscience clear,
And let thy comfort thus consist in mine,
That I bear part of whatsoever is thine;
As when we liude untouched with these disgraces,
whenas our kingdom was our dear embraces;
At Durham Palace, where sweet Hymen sang,
whose buildings with our nuptial music rang:
when Prothalamions praisde that happy day,
wherein great Dudley matched with noble Gray,
when they deuisde to lincke by wedlockes band,
The house of Suffolk to Northumberland;
Our fatal Dukedom to your Dukedom bound,
To frame this building on so weak a ground.
For what auailes a lawless usurpation,
which gives a Scepter, but not rules a Nation?
Only the surfeit of a vain opinion,
What gives content, gives what exceedes dominion.
When first mine ears were pierced with the fame,
Of Jane proclaimed by a Princess name,
A sudden fright my trembling heart appalls,
The fear of conscience enters iron walls.
Thrice happy for our fathers had it been,
If what we fearde, they wisely had foreseen,
And kept a mean gate in an humble path,
To have escapde the heavens impetuous wrath,
The true-bred Eagle strongly bears the wind,
And not each bird that is near unto their kind,
That like a King, does from the clouds command,
The fearful foul that moves but near the Land,
Though Mary be from mighty Kings descended,
My blood not from Plantaginet pretended;
My gransire Brandon did our house advance,
By princely Mary, Dowager of France;
The fruit of that fair stock which did combine,
And Yorkes sweet branch with Lancasters entwine,
And in one stalk did happily unite,
The pure vermilion Rose, with purer white;
I the untimely slip of that rich stem,
Whose golden bud brings forth a Diadem.
But oh forgive me Lord, it is not I,
Nor do I boast of this, but learn to die,
Whilst we were as our selves conjoined then,
Nature to nature, now an alien.
The purest blood, polluted is in blood,
Neerenes contemned, if sovereignty withstood;
A Diadem once dazeling the eye,
The day too dark to see affinity;
And where the arm is stretched to reach a Crown,
Friendship is broke, the dearest thing thrown down;
For what great Henry most strove to avoid,
The heavens have built, where earth would have destroide,
And seating Edward on his regal throne,
He gives to Mary, all that was his own,
By death assuring what by life is theirs,
The lawful claim of Henry's lawful he^res.
By mortal laws the bound may be diuorc'd,
But heavens decree by no means can be forced:
That rules the case, when men have all decreed,
Who took him hence, foresaw who should succeed,
For we in vain rely on human laws,
When heaven stands forth to plead the righteous cause,
Thus rule the heavens in their continual course,
That yeeldes to fate, that does not yield to force.
Man's wit does build for time but to devour,
But virtue's free from time and fortunes power;
Then my kind Lord, sweet Gilford be not grieved,
The soul is heavenly, and from heaven relieved;
And as we once have plighted troth together,
Now let us make exchange of minds to either;
To thy fair breast take my resolved mind,
Armde against black despair, and all her kind,
And to my bosom breathe that soul of thine,
There to be made as perfect as is mine;
So shall our faith as firmly be approved,
As I of thee, or thou of me beloved.
This life no life, were thou not dear to me,
Nor this no death, were I not woe for thee.
Thou my dear husband, and my Lord before,
But truly learn to die, thou shall be more.
Now live by prayer, on heaven fix all thy thought,
And surely find, what e're by zeal is sought;
For each good motion that the soul awakes,
A heavenly figure sees, from whence it takes
That sweet resemblance, which by power of kind,
Forms (like it self) an image in the mind,
And in our faith the operations be,
Of that divineness which through that we see;
Which never erres, but accidentally,
By our frail fleshes imbecillitie;
By each temptation ouer-apt to slide,
Except our spirit becomes our bodies guide;
For as these Towers our bodies do enclose
Their prisons, so unto our souls suppose
Our bodies, stopping that celestial light,
As these do hinder our exterior sight;
Whereon death seasing, does discharge the debt,
And us at blessed liberty does set.
Then draw thy forces all unto thy heart,
The strongest fortress of this earthly part,
And on these three let thy assurance lie,
On faith, repentance, and humility;
By which to heaven ascending by degrees,
Persist in prayer upon your bended knees;
Whereon if you assuredly be staide,
You need in peril not to be distnaide,
Which still shall keep you that you shall not fall,
For any peril that you can appall;
The key of heaven thus will^ you, you shall bear,
And grace you guiding, give you entrance there,
And you of those celestial joys possess,
Which mortal tongue's unable to express.
Then thank the heaven, preparing us this room,
Crowning our heads with glorious martiredome,
Before the black and dismal days begin,
The days of all idolatry and sin,
Not suffering us to see that wicked age,
When persecution vehemently shall rage,
When tyranny n^w tortures shall invent,
Inflicting vengeance on the innocent.
Yet heaven forbids, that Mary's womb shall bring,
Englands fair Scepter to a foreign King,
But unto fair Elizabeth shall leave it,
Which broken, hurt, and wounded, shall receive it;
And on her temples having placde the Crown,
Root out the dregs Idolatry has sown;
And Syons glory shall again restore,
Laid ruin, waste, and desolate before;
And from black sinders, and rude heaps of stones,
Shall gather up the Martires sacred bones,
And shall extirpe the power of Rome again,
And cast aside the heavy yoke of Spain.
Farewell sweet Gilford, know our end is near,
Heaven is our home, we are but strangers here.
Let us make haste to go unto the blessed,
Which from these weary worldly labors rest,
And with these lines my dearest Lord I greet thee,
Until in heaven thy Jane again shall meet thee.

Presently upon the death of King Edward, the Lady Jane was taken as Queen,
conveyed by water to the Tower of London for her safety, and after proclaimed
in diverse parts of the realm, as so ordained by king Edwards Letters-pattents,
and his will. My Grandsire Brandon did our house advance
By princely Mary, dowager of France.

Brandon Duke of Suffolk, by the French Queen, by which Frauncis he had this
Lady Jane: this Mary the French Queen, was daughter to king Henry the seventh,
by Elizabeth his Queen, which happy marriage conjoined the two noble families
of Lancaster and York. For what great Henry most strove to avoid,


Noting the distrust that King Henry the eight ever had in the Princess Mary

A prophecy of Queen Mary's barrenness, and of the happy and glorious
reign of Queen Elizabeth, her restoring of Religion, the abolishing of the
Romish servitude, and casting aside the yoke of Spain.

Gilford Dudley to Jane Gray.
AS the Swanne singing at his dying hour,
So I reply from my imprisning tower:
O could there be that power but in my verse,
To express the grief my wounded heart does pierce!
The very walls that straightly thee enclose,
Would surely weep at reading of my woes;
Let your eyes lend, I Will pay you every tear,
And give you intrest, if you do forbear,
Drop for a drop, and if you will needs have lone,
I will repay you franckly, two for one.
Perhaps you will think (your sorrows to appease)
That words of comfort fitter were than these:
True, and in you when such perfection lives,
As in most grief most comfort always gives:
And think not (Jane) that cowardly I faint,
To beg man's mercy by my sad complaint,
That death so much my courage can control,
At the departing of my living roll.
For if one life a thousand lives could be,
All those too few to consummate with thee,
When thou this cross so patiently do bear,
As if thou were incapable of fear,
And do no more this desolution fly,
Than if long age constrained thee to die:
Yet it is strange thou art become my foe,
And only now add'st most unto my woe,
Not that I loathe, that most did me delight,
But that so long deprived of thy sight;
For when I speak, and would complain my wrong.
Straitwayes thy name possesses all my tongue,
As thou before me ouermore did lie
The present object to my longing eye.
No ominous starte did at thy birth tide shine,
That might of thy sad destiny divine;
It Is only I that did thy fall persuade,
And thou by me a sacrifice art made,
As in those countries where the loving wives
With their kind husbands end their happy lives,
And crowned with garlands in their Brides attire,
Burn with his body in the funeral fire;
And she the worthiest reckned is of all,
Whom loast the peril seems to appall.
I boast not of Northumberland great name,
Nor of Ket conquered, adding to our fame,
When he to Norffolke with his armies sped,
And thence in chains the rebels captive led;
And brought safe peace returning to our doors,
Yet soread his glory on the eastern shores,
Not of my brothers, from whose natural grace,
Virtue may spring to beautify our Race,
Not of Grates match my children borne by thee,
Of the great blood indoubtedly to be,
But of thy virtues only do I boast,
That wherein I may justly glory most:
I craved no kingdoms though I thee did crave,
It might suffice thy only self to have,
Yet let me say how-euer it befell,
Me thinks a Crown should have become thee well:
For sure thy wisdom merited (or none)
To have been heard with wonder from a throne.
When from thy lips the counsel to each deed,
Does as from some wise oracle proceed,
And more esteemd thy virtues were to me,
Then all that else might ever come by thee;
So chaste thy love, so innocent thy life,
As being a virgin when thou were a wife,
So great a gift the heaven on me bestowed,
As giving that it nothing could have owed,
Such was the good I did possess of late,
Ere worldly cares disturbde our quiet state,
Ere trouble did in every place abound,
And angry war our former peace did wound.
This is all that ambition us affordes,
One crown is guarded with a thousand swords,
To mean estates, mean sorrows are out shown,
But crowns h(...) cares whose workings be unknown,
When Dudley led his armies to the east,
Of our whole forces generally possessed,
What then was thought his enter prise could let,
Whom a grave counsel freely did abet,
That had the judgment of the powrefull laws,
In every point to justify the cause,
The holy Church a helping hand that laid,
Who would have thought that these could not have swayde:
But what alas can parlements avail,
Where Mary's right must Edwards Act(...)
When Suffolkes power does Suffolkes hopes withstand,
Northumberland does leave Northumberland;
And they that should our greatness undergo,
Us, and our actions only overthrow,
Ere greatness gained we give it all our heart,
But being once come, could wish it would depart,
And indescreetly follow that so fast,
Which overtaken punishes our haste;
If any one do pity our offense,
Let him be sure that it be far from hence:
Here is no place for any one that shall,
So much as (once) commiserate our fall;
And we of mercy vainly should but think,
Our timelesse tears the insatiate earth does drink.
All lamentations utterly forlorn,
Dying before they fully can be borne.
Mothers that should their woeful children rue,
Fathers in death too kindly bid adue.
Friends their dear farewell lovingly to take,
The faithful servant weeping for our sake.
Brothers and sisters waiting on our beer,
Mourners to tell what we were living hear:
But we alas deprived are of all,
So fatal is our miserable fall,
And where at first for safety we were shut
Now in dark prison woefully are put,
And from height of our ambitious state,
Lie to repent our arrogance too late,
To thy persuasion thus I then reply,
Hold on thy course resolved still to die,
And when we shall so happily be gone,
Leave it to heaven to give the rightful throne,
And with that health regreet I thee again,
Which I of late did gladly entertain.

Seldom has it ever been known of any woman endued with such wonderful
gifts, as was this Lady, both for her wisdom and learning, of whose skill in
the tongues one reports by this Epigram.

Miraris Ianam Graio, sermon valere,
Quo ^^^mum nata est tempore Graia fuit.
When Dudley led his armies to the East.
The Duke of Northumberland prepared his power at London for his expedition
against the Rebels in Norfolk, and making haste away, appointed the rest of
his forces to meet him at Newmarket Heath: of whom this saying is reported,
that passing through Shorditch, the Lord Gray in his company, seeing the
people in great numbers came to see him, he said, the people press to see us,
but none bid God speed us. Whom a grave Council freely did abet.


John Dudley Duke of Northumberland, when he went out against Queen Mary, had
his commission sealed for the generalship of the Army, by the consent of the
whole Council of the land; insomuch that passing through the Council chamber
at his departure, the Earl of Arundell wished that he might have gone with
him in that expedition, and to spend his blood in the quarrel. When Suffolks
power does Suffolks hopes withstand,
Northumberland does lean Northumberland.


The Suffolk men were the first that ever resorted to Queen Mary in her
distress, repayring to her succors, whilst she remained both at Keningall,
and at Fermingham Castle, still increasing her aids, until the Duke of
Northumberland, was lest forsaken at Cambridge.

Finis.
THE worlds fair Rose, and Henry's frostie fire,
John's tyranny; and chaste Matilda's wrong,
The Enraged Queen, and furious Mortimer,
The scourge of France, and his chaste love I song,
Deposed Richard, Isabell exiled,
The gallant Tudor, and fair Katherine,
Duke Humfrey, and old Cobhams hapless child,
Courageous Pole, and that brave spritfull Queen,
Edward, and the delicious London Dame,
Brandon, and that rich dowager of France,
Surrey, with his fair paragon of fame,
Dudleys mishap, and virtuous Grayes mischaunce;
Their several loves since I before have shown,
Now give me leave, at last to sing mine own.
To the Reader of his Poems. Sonet. 1.
INto these loves who but for passion looks,
At this first sight, here let them lay them by,
And seek elsewhere in turning other books,
Which better may his labor satisfy,
No far-fetched sigh shall ever wound my breast,
Love from mine eye, a tear shall never w^ing,
Nor in ah-mees my whining Sonnets dressed,
(A Libertine) fantastickly I sing;
My verse is the true image of my mind,
Ever in motion, still desiring change,
The choice of all variety inclined,
And in all humors sportiuely I range,
My active Muse is of the worlds right strain,
That cannot long one fashion entertain.
The second to the Reader. Sonet. 2
MAny there be excelling in this kind,
Whose well tricked rimes with all invention swel;
Let each commend as best shall like his mind,
Some Sidney, Constable, some Daniell.
That thus their names familiarly I sing,
Let none think them disparaged to be,
Poor men with reverence may speak of a King,
And so may these be spoken of by me;
My wanton verse nere keeps one certain stay,
But now, at hand; then, seeks invention far,
And with each little motion runs astray,
Wild, madding, iocund, and irregular;
Like me that lust, my honest merry rimes,
Nor care for Critic, nor regard the times.
IDEA.
Sonnet 1.
THine eyes taught me the alphabet of love,
To kon my crosse-row ere I learnd to spell,
For I was apt, a Scholar like to prove,
Gave me sweet looks whenas I learned well,
Vows were my vowells when I then begun,
At my first lesson in thy sacred name,
My consonants the next when I had done,
Words consonant, and sounding to thy fame;
My liquids then, were liquide cristall tears,
My cares, my mutes, so mute to crave relief,
My doleful dipthongs, were my life's dispaires,
Redoubling sighs, the accents of my grief;
My loves Schoole-mistris now has taught me so,
That I can read a story of my woe.
Sonnet. 2.
MY heart was slain, and none but you and I,
who should I think the murder should commit?
Since but your self, there was no creature by
But only I, guiltless of murthring it.
It slew it self; the verdict on the view
Do quit the dead, and me not accessory;
Well, well, I fear it will be proved by you,
The evidence so great a proof does carry.
But O, see, see, we need enquire no further,
Upon your lips the scarlet drops are found,
And in your eye, the boy that did the murder,
Your cheeks yet pale since first they gave the wound.
By this, I see, how ever things be past,
Yet heaven will still have murder out at last.
Sonnet 3.
TAking my pen, with words to cast my woes,
Duly to count the sum of all my cares,
I find, my grief innumerable grows,
The recknings rise to millions of dispaires,
And thus dividing of my fatal hours,
The payments of my love I read, and cross
Substracting, set my sweets unto my sowers,
My joys arerage leads me to my loss;
And thus mine eyes a debtour to thine eye,
Which by extortion gains all their looks,
My heart has paid such grievous usury,
That all his wealth lies in thy beauties books.
And all is thine which has been due to me,
And I a Bankrupt, quite undone by thee.
An allusion to Narcissus. Sonnet. 4.
Beauty sometime in all her glory crowned,
Passing by that clear fountain of thine eye,
Her sunshine face there chauncing to epsy,
Forgot herself, deeming she had been drowned,
And thus whilst beauty, on her beauty gazed,
Whothen (yet living) thought she had been dying,
And yet in death some hope of life espying,
With her own rare perfections so amazed,
Twixt joy and grief, yet with a smiling frowning,
The glorious sunbeams of her eyes bright shining,
And she on her own destenie divining,
Cast her self, to save herself by drowning;
The well of Nectar, pau'd with pearl and gold,
Where she remains for all eyes to behold.
Sonnet. 5.
NOthing but no and I, and I and no,
How falls it out so strangely you reply?
I tell ye (Fair) i will not be answered so,
With this affirming no, denying I,
I say I love, you slender answer I,
I say you love, you pule me out a no;
I say I die, you echo me with I,
Save me I cry, you sigh me out a no:
Must woe and I, have naught but no and I,
No I am I, if I no more can have,
Answer no more, with silence make reply,
And let me take my self what I do crave;
Let no and I, with I and you be so,
Then answer no, and I, and I, and no.
To Harmony. Sonnet. 6.
Love once would dance within my Mistress eye.
And wanting music fitting for the place,
Swore that I should the instrument supply,
And suddenly presents me with her face;
Straitwaies my pulse play lively in my veins,
My panting breath does keep a meaner time,
My q^au'ring artires be the tenours strains,
My trembling sinews serve the counterchime,
My hollow sighs the deepest b^se do bear,
True diapason in distincted sound;
My panting heart the trebble makes the air,
And desken^s fineli^ on the musikes ground;
Thus like a Luteor Vial did I he,
Whilst he proud slave dauncde galliards in her eye.
Sonnet 7.
Love in an humor plaide the prodigal,
And bids my senses to a solemn feast,
Yet more to grace the company withal,
Inuit^s my ha^t to be the chiefest guest;
No other drink would serve this gluttons turn,
But precious tears distilling from mine eine,
Which with my sighs this Epicure does burn,
Quaffing carowses in this costly wine,
Where, in his cups overcome with foul excess,
Begins to play a swaggering ruffins part,
And at the banquet, in his drunkenness
S(...) my dear friend, his kind and truest heart;
A gent^e warning friends, thus may you see,
What it is to keep a drunkard company.
To the Moon. Sonct. 8
PHoebe look down, and here behold in me,
The elements within thy sphere enclosed,
How kindly Nature placed them under thee,
And in my world, see how they are disposed;
My hope is earth, the lowest, cold and dry,
The grosser mother of deep melancholy,
Water my tears, coolde with humidity,
Wan, flegmaticke, inclinde by Nature wholly;
My sighs, the air, hot, moist, ascending higher,
Subtle of sanguine, did in my ha^ts dolor,
My thoughts they be the element of fi^e,
Hot, dry, and piercing, still inclinde to choler,
Thine eye the Orb unto all these, from whence
Proceeds the effects of powerful influence.
To Lunacie. Sonnet. 9.
AS other men, so I my self do muse.
Why in this sort I wrest invention so,
And why these giddy metaphors I use,
Leaving the path the greater part do go;
I will resolve you; I am lunaticke,
And ever this in madmen you shall find,
What they last thought on when the brain grew sick,
In most distraction keep that still in mind.
Thus talking idely in this bedlam fit,
Reason and I (you must conceive) are twain,
It Is nine years now, since first I lost my wit,
Bear with me then, though troubled be my brain;
With diet and correction, men distraught,
(Not too far past) may to their wits be brought.
Sonnet. 10.
TO nothing fitter can I thee compare,
Then to the son of some rich penny father,
Who having now brought on his end with care,
Leaves to his son all he had heaped together;
This new rich nouice, lavish of his chest,
To one man gives, and on an other spends,
Then here he riots, yet amongst the rest,
Haps to send some to one true honest friend.
Thy gifts thou in obscurity do waste,
False friends thy kindenes, borne but to deceive thee,
Thy love, that is on the unworthy placed,
Time has thy beauty, which with age will leave thee;
Only that little which to me was lent,
I give thee back, when all the rest is spent.
Sonnet. 11.
YOu not alone, when you are still alone,
O God from you that I could private be,
Since you one were, I never since was one,
Since you in me, my self since out of me,
Transported from my self into your being,
Though either distant, present yet to either,
Senseless with too much joy, each other seeing,
And only absent when we are together.
Give me my self, and take your self again,
Devise some means but how I may forsake you,
So much is mine that does with you remain,
That taking what is mine, with me I take you;
You do bewitch me, O that I could fly,
From my self you, or from your own self I.
To the Soul. Sonnet. 12.
THat learned father, which so firmly proves
The Soul of man immortal and divine,
And does the several offices define:
Anima Gives her that name as she the body moves,
Amor Then is she love embracing Charity,
Animus Moving a will in us, it is the mind,
Men's Retaining knowledge, still the same in kind;
Memoria As intellectual it is the memory,
Ratio In iudgeing, Reason only is her name,
Sensus In speedy apprehension it is sense,
Conscientia In right or wrong, they call her conscience:
Spiritus The spirit, when it to Godward does inflame.
These of the soul the several functions be,
Which my heart lightened by thy love does see.
To the Shadow. Sonnet. 13.
LEtters and lines we see are soon defaced,
Mettells do waste, and fret with cankers rust,
The Diamond shall once consume to dust;
And freshest colors with foul stains disgraced,
Paper and incke, can paint but naked words,
To write with blood, offorce offends the sight,
And if with tears, I find them all too light,
And sighs and signs, a seely hope affords.
O sweetest shadow, how thou seru'st my turn,
Which still shall be, as long as there is sun,
Nor whilst the world is, never shall be done,
Whilst Moon shall shine, or any fire shall burn:
That every thing whence shadow does proceed,
May in his shadow, my loves story reed,
Sonnet. 14.
IF he from heaven that filch'd that living fire,
Condemned by Jove to end lesle torment be,
I greatly marvel how you still go free,
That far beyond Prometheus did aspire?
The fire he stole although of heavenly kind,
Which from above he craftily did take,
Of liuelesse clods us living men to make,
Again bestowed in temper of the mind.
^ut vou brok into heavens immortal store,
Where virtue, honor, wit and beauty lay,
Which taking thence, you have escaped away,
Yet stand as free as e're you did before;
But old Prometheus punished for his rape,
Thus poor thieves suffer, when the greater escape.
Sonnet. 15.
VIewing the glass of my youths miseries,
I see the face of my deformed ears,
With withered brows, all wrinckled with dispaires,
That for my youth the tears fall from mine eyes,
Then in these tears the mirrors of these eyes,
Thy fairest youth and be (...)y do I see,
Imprinted there by looking still on thee;
Thus midst my woes, ten thousand joys arise.
Yet in these joys the shadows of my good,
In this fa^e limmed ground as white as snow,
Painted the blackest image of my woe,
With murthring hands imbrude in mine own blood;
And in this image his dark clowdy eyes,
My life, and love, I here anatomi^e.
To the Phoeniae. Sonnet 16.
Within the compass of this spacious round,
Amongst all birds the Phoenix is alone,
Which but by you could never have been known.
None like to that, none like to you is found,
Heap your own virtues seasoned by their sun,
On heavenly top of your divine desire;
Then with your beauty set the same on fire,
So by your death, your life shall be begun.
Your self thus burned in this sacred flame,
With your own sweetness all the heavens perfuming,
And still increasing as you are consuming,
Shall spring again from th'ashes of your fame,
And mounting up shall to the heavens ascend,
So may you live, past world, past fame, past end.
To Time. Sonnet. 17.
STay, stay, sweet Time, behold or e're thou pass,
From world to world, thou long have fought to see,
That wonder now wherein all wonders be,
Where heaven beholds her in a mortal glass:
Nay look thee Time in this celestial glass,
And thy youth past, in this suite mirror see,
The first worlds beauty in the infancy,
What it was then, what thou before it was.
Now pass on Time, to af^r-worlds tell this:
(And yet shall tell) but truly what has been,
That they may say what former time has seen,
And heaven may joy to think on past worlds blis:
Here make a period Time, and say for me,
She was, whose like again shall never be.
To the Celestial numbers. Sonnet. 18.
Unto the world, to learning, and to heaven,
Three nines there are, to every one a nine,
One number of the earth, the other both divine,
One woman now makes three odd numbers even:
Nine orders first of Angels be in heaven,
Nine Muses do with learning still frequent,
These with the Gods are ever resident;
Nine worthy ones unto the world were given:
My worthy one to these nine worthies adds,
And my fair Muse, one Muse unto the nine,
And my good Angel in my soul divine,
With one more order, these nine orders gladdeth:
My Muse, my worthy, and my Angel then,
Makes every one of these three nines a ten.
To Humor. Sonnet. 19.
YOu cannot love my pretty heart and why?
There was a time you told me that you would,
But now again you will the same deny,
If it might pease you, would to God you could;
What will you hate? nay that you will not neither,
Nor love, nor hate, how then? what will you do,
What will you keep a mean then betwixt either?
Or will you love me, and yet hate me too?
Yet serves not this, what next, what other shift?
You will, and will not, what a coil is here?
I see your craft, now I perceive your drift,
And all this while I was mistaken there:
Your love and hate is this, I now do prove you,
You love in hate, by hate to make me love you.
Sonnet. 20.
AN evil spirit your beauty haunts me still,
Wherewith (alas) I have been long possessed,
Which cease not to tempt me unto ill,
Nor gives me once but one poor minutes rest:
In me it speaks whether I sleep or wake,
And when by means to drive it out I try,
With greater torments then it me does take,
And tortures me in most extreamitie,
Before my face, it lays all my dispaires,
And hastes me on unto a sudden death;
Now tempting me to drown my self in tears,
And then in sighing to give up my breath;
Thus am I still prouokde to every evil,
By this good wicked spirit, sweet Angel devil.
To the Spheres. Sonnet. 21.
THou which do guide this little world of love,
Thy planets mansions here thou may behold,
My brow the sphere where Saturn still does move,
wrinckled with cares, withered, dry and cold;
Mine eyes the Orb where Jupiter does trace,
Which gently smile because they look on thee,
Mars in my swartie visage takes his place,
Made lean with love, where furious conflicts be:
Sol in my breast with his hot scorching flame,
But in my heart alone does Venus reign;
Mercury my hands, the Organs of my fame,
Luna my wavering and unconstant vain;
The starry heaven thy praise by me expressed,
Thou the first mouer, guiding all the rest.
To Folly. Sonnet. 22.
WIth fools and children good discretion bears,
Then honest people bear with Love and me,
Nor older yet, nor wiser made by years,
Amongst the rest of fools and children be,
Love's still a baby, places with gawdes and joys,
And like a wanton, sports with every feather,
And idiots still are running after boys,
Then fools and children fitt'st to go together,
He still as young as when he first was borne,
No wiser I, then when as young as he,
You that be hold us laugh us not to scorn,
Give Nature thanks you are not such as we:
Yet fools and children sometimes tell in play,
Some wise in show, more fools indeed then they.
Sonnet. 23.
Love banished heaven, in earth was held in scorn.
Wandering ab^oad in need and beggary,
And wanting friends, though of a God desse borne,
Yet crau'de the alms of such as passed ^y,
I like a man, douote and charitable;
Clothed the naked, lodged this wandering guest,
With sighs and tears still furnishing his table,
With what might make the miserable blessed;
But this ungrateful for my good desert.
Enticde my thoughts against me to conspire,
Who gave consent to steal away my heart.
And set my breast his lodging on a fire:
Well, well, my friends, when beggars grow thus bold,
No marvel then though charity grow cold.
Sonnet 24.
I Hear some say, this man is not in love,
Who, can he love? a likely thing they say:
Read but his verse, and it will easly prove,
O judge not rashly (gentle Sir) I pray,
Because I loosely trifle in this sort,
As one that fame his sorrows would beguile:
You now suppose me, all this time in sport,
And please your felfe with this conceit the while.
You shallow censures, sometime see you not
In greatest perils some men pleasant be,
Where fame by death is only to be got;
They resolute, so stands the case with me;
Where other men in depth of passion cry,
I laugh at Fortune, as in jest to die.
Sonnet 25.
O Why should nature niggardly restrain.
The Southern nations relish not our tongue,
Else should my lines glide on the waves of R(...),
And crown the Pirens with my living song;
But bounded thus to Scotland get you forth,
Thence take you wing unto the Orcades,
There let my verse get glory in the north,
Making my sighs to thawe the frozen seas,
And let the Bards within that Irish i will,
To whom my Muse with firi^ wings shall pass,
Call back the stiff neckt rebels from exile,
And mollify the slaughtring Galliglasse;
And when my flowing numbers they rehearse,
Let wolves and bears be charmed with my verse.
To Despair. Sonnet 26.
I Ever love, where never hope appears,
Yet hope draws on my neuer-hoping care,
And my life's hope would die but for despair,
My neuer-certaine joy, breeds euer-certaine fears,
Vncertaine-dread, gives wings unto my hope,
Yet my hopes wings are laden so with fear,
As they cannot ascend to my hopes sphere,
Yet fear gives them more than a heavenly scope;
Yet this large room is bounded with despair,
So my love is still fettered with vain hope,
And liberty depriues him of his scope,
And thus am I imprisond in the air;
Then sweet despair, a while hold up thy head,
Or all my hope for sorrow will be dead.
To Fantasy. Sonnet 27.
I Gave my faith to Love, Love his to me,
That he and I sworn brothers should remain,
Thus faith received, faith given back again,
Who would imagine bond more sure could be?
Love flies to her, yet holds he my faith taken,
As from my virtue raising my offense,
Making me guilty by mine innocence;
And only bond by being so forsaken,
He makes her ask what I before had vowed,
Giving her that, which he had given me,
I bound by him, and he by her made free.
Who ever so hard breach of faith allowd?
Speak you that should of right and wrong discuss,
Was right ere wronged, or wrong ere righted thus?
Sonnet 28.
TO such as say, thy love I ouer-prise,
And do not stick to term my praises folly,
Against these folks that think themselves so wise,
I thus appose my force of reason wholly,
Though I give more, then well affords my state,
In which expense the most suppose me vain,
Would yield them nothing at the casiest rate,
Yet at this price, returns me trebble gain,
The value not unskillful how to use,
And I give much, because I gain thereby,
I that thus take, or they that thus refuse,
Whether are these deceived then, or I?
In every thing I hold this maxim still,
The circumstance does make it good or ill.
To the Senses. Sonnet 29.
WHen conquering love did first my heart assail,
Unto mine aid I sommond every sense,
Doubting if that proud tirant should prevail,
My heart should suffer for mine eyes offense;
But he with beauty first corrupted sight,
My hearing bribde with her tongues harmony,
My taste by her sweet lips drawn with delight,
My smelling won with her breaths spicerie;
But when my touching came to play his part,
(The King of senses, greater than the rest)
He yeeldes love up the keis unto my heart,
And tells the other how they should be blessed:
And thus by those of whom I hopde for aid,
To cruel love my soul was first betraide.
To the Vestalls. Sonnet 30.
THose Priests which first the Vestall fire begun,
Which might be borrowed from no earthly flame,
Deuisde a ves^ell to receive the Sun,
Being steadfastly opposed to the same;
Where, with sweet wood, laid curiously by Ar^^,
Whereon the Sun might by reflection beat,
Receiving strength from every secret part,
The fuel kindled with colestiall heat.
Thy blessed eyes, the Sun which lights this fire,
My holy thoughts, they be the Vestall flame,
The precious odours be my chaste desire,
My breast the fuel which includes the sa;
Thou art my Vesta, thou my goddess art,
Thy hallowed temple one lie is my heart.
Sonnet 31.
ME thinks I see some crooked Mimicks jeere,
And ^axe my Muse with this fantastic grace,
Turning my papers, asks, what have we here?
Making withal, some filthy an^ike face;
I fear no censure, nor what thou can say,
Nor shall my spirit one jo^e of vigor lose,
Think thou my wit shall keep the packe-horse way,
That every d^dgen low invention goes?
Since Sonnets thus in bundles are impressed,
And every drudge does dull our sa^iate e^re?
Think thou my Love shall in those rags be dressed,
That every dowdie, every t^ull does wea^e?
Unto my pitch no common judgment flies,
I scorn all earthly dung-bred scarabies.
To the river Ankor. Sonnet 32.
Our floudes Queen Thames; for ships and swans is crowned,
And stately Seuerne for her shoares is praised,
The cristall Trent, for Foords and Fish renowned,
And Anons fame, to Albions cliues is raised.
Carlegion Chester, vaunts her holy Dee,
York many wonders of her Owse can tell,
The Peake her Dove, whose banks so fertile be,
And Kent will say, her Medway does excel,
Cotswold commends her Isis and her Tame,
Our northern borders boast of Tweedes fair flood,
Our western parts extol her Wilis fame,
And old Legea brags of Danish blood;
Ardens sweet Ankor let thy glory be,
That fair Idea she does live by thee.
To Imagination. Sonnet 33.
WHilst yet mine eyes do surfeit with delight,
My woeful heart imprisond in my breast,
Wishes to be transformed in my sight,
That it like those, by looking might be blessed,
But whilst mine eyes thus greedily do gaze,
Finding their objects ouer-soone depart,
These now the others happiness do praise,
Wishing themselves that they had bin my heart;
That eyes were heart, or that the heart were eyes,
As covetous the others use to have;
But finding reason, their request denies,
This to each other mutually they crave;
That since the one cannot the other be,
That eyes could think, or that my heart could see.
To Admiration. Sonnet 34.
Marvel not Love, though I thy power admire,
Ravished a world beyond the farthest thought,
That knowing more than ever has bin taught,
That I am only sta^u'd in my desire;
Marvel not Love, though I thy power admire,
Aiming at things exceeding all perfection,
To wisdoms self to minister direction,
That I am only starved in my desire;
Marvel not Love, though I thy power admire,
Though my conceit I further seem to bend,
Than possibly invention can extend,
And yet am only starved in my desire;
If thou will wonder, here is the wonder Love,
That this to me does yet no wonder prove,
To Miracle. Sonnet 35.
SOme misbelieuing, and profane in love,
When I do speak of miracles by thee,
May say that thou art flattered by me,
Who only write, my skill in verse to prove.
See miracles, ye unbelieving see,
A dumb-borne Muse, made to express the mind,
A cripple hand to write, yet lame by kind,
One by thy name, the other touching thee;
Blind were mine eyes, till they were seen of thine,
And mine ears deaf, by thy fame healed be,
My vices curde, by virtues sprung from thee,
My hopes revived which long in grave had live:
All unclean thoughts, foul spirits cast out in me,
Only by virtue that proceeds from thee.
To Wonder. Sonnet 36.
REading sometime, my sorrows to beguile,
I find old Poets hills and floods admire,
One, he does wonder monster breeding Nyle,
Another maruells sulphur Aetn^es fire.
Now broad-brimd Indus, then of Pindus height,
Pe^ion and Ossa, frostie Cauc^se old,
The Delian Cinthus, then Olympus weight,
Slow Arrer, frantic Gallus, Cydnus cold.
Some Ganges, Ister, and of Tagus tell,
Some whirle-poole Po, and sliding Hypasis,
Some old Pernassus where the Muses dwell,
Some Helicon, and some fair Simois;
A fools think I, had you Idea seen,
Poor brooks and banks, had no such woonders been
Sonnet 37.
Dear, why should you command me to my (...)
When now the night does sommon all to sleep?
Me thinks this time becomes Lovers best,
Night was ordaind together friends to keep,
How happy are all other living things,
Which though the day disjoyne by several flight,
The quiet evening yet together brings,
And each returns unto his love at night.
O thou that art^ so cu^^eous unto all,
Why should thou Night abuse me only thus,
That every crea^ure to his kind do call,
And yet it is thou do only sever us:
Well could I wish it would be ever day,
If when night comes you bid me go away.
Sonnet 38.
SItting alone, Love bids me go and write,
Reason pluckes back, commanding me to stay,
Boasting that she does still direct the way,
Or else love were unable to indite;
Love growing angry, vexed at the spleen,
And scorning Reasons maimed argument,
Strait taxes Reason, wanting to invent,
Where she with Love conuersing has not been,
Reason reproched with this coy disdain,
Dispiteth Love, and laugheth at her folly,
And Love contemning Reasons reason wholly,
Thought it in weight too light by many a grain.
Reason put back, does out of sight remove,
And Love alone finds reason in my love.
Sonnet 39.
SOme, when in time they of their loves do tell,
With flames and lightning their exordiums paint,
Some call on heaven, some invoke on hell,
And Fates and Furies with their woes acquaint,
Elysium is too high a seat for me,
I will not come in Stix or Phlegiton,
The thriee three Muses but too wanton be,
Like they that lust, I care not, I will none.
Spiteful Errinis frights me with her looks,
My manhood dares not with foul Ate mell,
I quake to look on Heccats charming books,
I still fear bug-beares in Apolloes Cell.
I pass not for Minorua, nor Astrea,
Only I call upon divine Idea.
Sonnet. 40.
MY heart the a^uile where my thoughts do beat,
My words the hammers fashioning my desire,
My breast the forge, including all the heat,
Love is the fuel which maintains the fire:
My sighs the bellows which the same increases,
Filling mine ears with noise and nightly groaning,
Toiling with pain, my labor never cease,
In grievous passions my woes still bemoning:
Mine eyes with tears against the fire striving,
Whose scorching gleed my heart to cinders turns;
But with those drops, the flame again reviving,
Still more and more unto my torment burns:
With Sisiphus thus do I role the stone,
And turn the wheel with damned Ixion.
Sonnet. 41.
WHy do I speak of joy, or write of love,
When my heart is the very den of horror,
And in my soul the pains of hell I prove,
With all his torments and infernal terror?
What should I say, what yet remains to do?
My brain is dry with weeping all too long,
My sighs be spent in yttring of my woe,
And I want words wherewith to tell my wrong:
But still distracted in Loves lunacie,
And bedlam-like thus rauing in my grief,
Now rail upon her hair, now on her eye,
Now call her Goddess, then I call her thief;
Now I deny her, then I do confess her,
Now do I curse her, then again I bless her.
Sonnet. 42.
SOme men there be which like my method well,
And do commend the strangenes of my vain,
Some say, I have a passing pleasing strain,
Some say, that in my humor I excel:
Some, who not kindly relish my conceit,
They say (as Poets do) I use to feign,
And in bare words paint out my passions pain.
Thus sundry men their sundry minds repeat;
I pass not I, how men affected be,
Nor who commends, or discommends my verse,
It pleases me, if I my woes rehearse,
And in my lines, if she my love may see:
Only my comfort still consists in this,
Writing her praise, I cannot write amisso.
Sonnet. 43.
Why should your fair eyes with such sovereign grace,
Dispearse their rays on every vulgar spirit,
Whilst I in darkness in the self same place,
Get not one glance to recompense my merit:
So does the plowman gaze the wandering star,
And only rests contented with the light,
That never learnd what constellations are,
Beyond the bent of his unknowing fight.
O why should beauty (custom to obey)
To their gross sense apply her self so ill?
Would God I were as ignorant as they,
When I am made unhappy by my skill;
Only compeld on this poor good to boast,
Heavens are not kind to the that know them most.
Sonnet. 44.
Whilst thus my pen strives to eternize thee,
Age rules my lines with wrinckles in my face,
Where in the map of all my misery,
Is modeld out the world of my disgrace,
Whilst in despite of tyrannizing times,
Medea-like I make thee young again,
Prowdly thou scorust my world-outwearing rimes,
And murther'st Virtue with thy coy disdain;
And though in youth my youth untimely perish,
To keep thee from oblivion and the grave,
Ensuing ages yet my rimes shall cherish,
When I entombed my better part shall save;
And though this earthly body fade and die,
My name shall mount upon eternity.
Sonnet. 45.
Muses which sadly sit about my chair,
Drowned in the tears extorted by my lines,
With heavy sighs whilst thus I break the air,
Painting my passions in these sad dissignes;
Since she disdaines to bless my happy verse,
The strong-built Trophies to her living fame,
Ever henceforth my bosom be your hearse,
Wherein the world shall now entombe her name;
Enclose my music you poor senseless walls,
Since she is deaf and will not hear my mones,
Soften your selves with every tear that falls,
Whilst I like Orpheus sing to trees and stones;
Which with my plaint seem yet with pity moved,
Kinder then she who I so long have lived.
Sonnet. 46.
PLain path'd Experience the vnlearneds guide,
Her simple followers evidently shows,
Sometime what schoolmen scarcely can decide,
Nor yet wise Reason absolutely knows:
In making trial of a murder wrought,
If the yile actor of the he^nous deed,
Near the dead body happily be brought.
Often has been proved the breathless coarse will bleed;
She coming near that my poor heart has slain,
Long since departed (to the world no more)
The ancient wounds no longer can contain,
But fall to bleeding as they did before:
But what of this? should she to death be led.
It furthers justice, but helps not the dead.
Sonnet. 47.
IN pride of wit, when high desire of fame
Gave life and courage to my laboring (...)
And first the sound and virtue of my name,
Won grace and credit in the ears of men;
With those the througed Theaters that press.
I in the circuit for the Laurel strove,
Where the full praise I freely must confess,
In heat of blood and modest mind might move:
With showts and claps at every little pawse,
When the proud round on every side has rung.
Sadly I sit unmoved with the applawse,
As though to me it nothing did belong:
No public glory vainly I pursue.
The praise I strive, is to eternize you.
Sonnet. 48.
CUpid, dumb I doll, peevish faint of Love,
No more shall thou nor saint nor idol be,
No god art thou, Loves goddess she does prove,
Of all thine honor she has robbed thee:
Thy bow old broke, is peecde with half desire,
Her bow is beauty with ten thousand strings,
And every one of purest golden wire;
The least, of force to conquer hoasts of Kings:
Thy shafts be spent, and she (to war appointed)
Hides in those Cristall quiuers of her eyes,
More arrows with hart-piercing mettle pointed,
Then there be stars at midnight in the skies:
With these she steals men's hearts for her relief,
Yet happy he that is robbed of such a thief.
Sonnet. 49.
THou leaden brain which censur'st what I write
And say my lines be dull, and do not move.
I marvel not thou feel'st not my delight,
Which never feltst my fiery tuch of love:
But thou whose pen has like a Packe-horse served,
Whose stomach unto gaul has turned thy food,
Whose senses like poor prisners hunger-staru'd.
Whose grief has parched thy body, dride thy blood;
Thou which have scorned life, and hated death,
And in a moment mad, sober, glad and sorry,
Thou which have bann'd thy thoughts, and cursed thy birth
With thousand plagues more then in purgatory,
Thou thus whose spirit Love in his fire refines,
Come thou and read, admire and plawd my lines.
Sonnet. 50.
AS in some Countries far remote from hence,
The wretched creature destined to die,
Having the judgment due to his offense,
By Surgeons begged, their Art on him to try:
Which on the living work without remorce,
First make incision on each maistring vain,
Then stanch the bleeding, then transperce the coarse,
And with their balmes recure the wounds again,
Then poison and with Physic him restore,
Not that they fear the hopeless man to kill,
But their experience to increase the more;
Even so my Mistress works upon my ill,
By curing me, and killing me each hour,
Only to show her beauties sovereign power.
Sonnet. 51.
CAlling mind since first my love begun,
Th'incertaine times often varying in their course,
How things still unexpectedly have run,
As please the fates, by their resistlesse force:
Lastly, mine eyes amazedly have seen,
Essex great fall, Tyroue his peace to gain,
The quiet end of that long-liuing Queen,
This Kings fair entrance, and our peace with Spain,
We and the Dutch at length our selves to sever,
Thus the world does, and evermore shall reele,
Yet to my goddess am I constant ever;
How ere blind fortune turn her giddie wheel:
Though heaven and earth prove both to me untrue,
Yet am I still inuiolate to you.
An alusion to Dedalus and Icarus. Sonnet 52.
MY heart imprisoned in a hopeless I Will,
Peopled with Armies of pale jealous eyes,
The shores beset with thousand secret spies,
Must pass by air, or else die in exile;
He framde him wings with feathers of his thought,
Which by their nature learnd to mount the sky,
And with the same he practiced to fly,
Till he himself this Eagles Art had taught:
Thus soaring still, not looking once below,
So near thine eyes celestial sun aspired,
That with the rays his wafting pineons fired:
Thus was the wanton cause of his own woe,
Down fell he in thy beauties Ocean drenched,
Yet there he burns in fire that is never quenched.
Another to the River Ankor. Sonnet 53.
Clear Ankor, on whose siluer-sanded shore,
My soule-shrin'd saint, my fair Idea lies,
O blessed brook, whose milke-white swans adore
That cristall stream refined by her eyes,
Where sweet mirth-breathing Zephire in the spring,
Gently distills his Nectar-dropping showers,
Where Nightingales in Arden sit and sing,
Amongst the dainty dew-impearled flowers;
Say thus fair Brook, when thou shall see thy Queen,
Lo here thy sheep heard spent his wandering years;
And in these shades dear Nymph he often has been.
And here to thee he sacrifizde his tears:
Fair Arden, thou my Tempe art alone,
And thou sweet Ankor art my Helicon.
Sonnet. 54.
YEt read at last the story of my woe,
The drery abstracts of my endless cares,
With my like sorrow enterlined so,
Smokde with my sighs, and blotted with my tears;
The sad memorialls of my miseries,
Pend^n the grief of mine afflicted ghost;
My life's complaint in doleful Elegies,
With so pure love as time could never boast:
Receive the incence which I offer here,
By my strong faith ascending to thy fame,
My zeal, my hope, my vows, my praise, my prayer,
My souls oblations to thy sacred name:
Which name my Muse to highest heaven shall raise,
By chaste desire, true love, and virtues praise.
Sonnet 55.
MY Fair, if thou will register my love,
More then worlds volumes shall thereof arise;
Preserve my tears, and thou thy self shall prove,
A second flood down raining from mine eyes:
Note but my sighs, and thine eyes shall behold,
The sunbeams smothered with immortal smoke;
And if by thee my prayers may be enrold,
They heaven and earth to pity shall provoke;
Look thou into my breast, and thou shall see,
Chaste holy vows for my souls sacrifice,
That soul (sweet Maid) which so has honored thee,
Erecting Trophies to thy sacred eyes;
Those eyes to my heart shining ever bright,
When darkness has obscurde each other light.
An allusion to the Aegl^ts. Sonnet 56.
MY thoughts bred up with Eagle-birds of love,
And for their virtues I desirde to know;
Upon the neast I set them forth, to prove
If they were of the Eagles kind, or no.
But they no sooner saw my sun appear,
But on her rays with gazing eyes they stood,
Which proou'd my birds delighted in the air,
And that they came of this rare kingly brood.
But now their plumes full summde with sweet desire,
To show their kind, began to clime the skies:
Do what I could, my Eglets would aspire,
Strait mounting up to thy celestial eyes.
And thus (my Fair) my thoughts away be flowne,
And from my breast into thine eyes be gone.
Sonnet 57.
YOu best discerned of my interior eyes,
And yet your graces outwardly divine,
Whose dear remembrance in my bosom lies,
Too rich a relic for so poor a shrine:
You in whom Nature chose herself to view,
When she her own perfection would admire,
Bestowing all her excellence on you;
At whose pure eyes Love lights his hallowed fire,
Even as a man that in some trance has seen,
More than his wondering vttrance can unfold,
That rapt in spirit in better worlds has been,
So must your praise distractedly be told;
Most of all short, when I should show you most,
In your perfections altogether lost.
Sonnet 58.
IN former times, such as had store of coin,
In wars at home, or when for conquests bound,
For fear that some their treasures should purloyne,
Gave it to keep to spirits within the ground;
And to attend it, them so strongly tide,
Till they returned, home when they never came,
Such as by art to get the same have tried,
From the strong spirit by no means get the same,
Nearer you come, that further flies away,
Striving to hold it strongly in the deep:
Even as this spirit, so she alone does play,
With those rich Beauties heaven gives her to keep:
Pity so left, to coldenes of her blood,
Not to avail her, nor do others good.
To Proverb. Sonnet 59.
AS Love and I, late harbourde in one Inn,
With Proueths thus each other entertain;
In love there is no lack, thus I begin:
Fair words makes fools, replieth he again:
That spares to speak, does spare to speed (quoth I)
As well (says he) too forward as too slow.
Fortune assistes the boldest, I reply:
A hasty man (quoth he) never wanted woe.
Labor is light, where love (quoth I) does pay,
(Says he) light burdens heavy, if far borne:
(Quoth I) the maine lost, cast the by away:
You have spunne a fair thread, he replies in scorn.
And having thus a while each other thwarted,
Fools as we met, so fools again we parted.
Sonnet 60.
DEfine my love, and tell the joys of heaven,
Express my woes, and show the pains of hell,
Declare what fate unlucky stars have given,
And ask a world upon my life to dwell.
Make known that faith, unkindness could not move;
Compare my worth with others base desert,
Let virtue be the tuch-stone of my love,
So may the heavens read wonders in my heart;
Behold the clouds which have eclipsde my sun,
And view the crosses which my course does let;
Tell me, if ever since the world begun,
So fair a rising had so foul a set:
And by all means, let foul unkindness prove,
And show a second to so pure a love.
Sonnet 61.
WHen first I ended, then I first began,
The more I travel, further from my rest,
Where most I lost, there most of all I wanne,
Pined with hunger, rising from a feast.
Me thinks I flee, yet want I legs to go,
Wise in conceit, in act a very sot,
Ravished with joy amidst a hell of woe,
What most I seem, that surest am I not.
I build my hopes a world above the sky,
Yet with the Mole I creep into the earth,
In plenty am I ^^aru'd with penury,
And yet I surffet in the greatest dearth:
I have, I want, despair, and yet desire,
Burned in a sea of ice, and drowned amidst a fire.
Sonnet 62.
TRuce gentle Love, a parlee now I crave,
Me thinks it is long since first these wars begun,
Nor thou nor I, the better yet can have:
Bad is the match where neither party won.
I offer free conditions of fair peace,
My heart for hostage, that it shall remain,
Discharge our forces here, let malice cease,
So for my pledge, thou give me pledge again.
Or if nothing but death will serve thy turn,
Still thirsting for subversion of my state;
Do what thou can, raze, massacre, and burn,
Let the world see the utmost of thy hate:
I send defiance, since if overthrown,
Thou vanquishing, the conquest is mine own.
Certain other Sonnets to great and worthy Personages.
To the high and mighty Prince, James king of Scots. Sonnet 61.
NOt thy grave Counsels, nor thy subjects love,
Nor all that famous Scottish royalty,
Or what thy sovereign greatness may approve,
Others in vain do but historifie,
When thine own glory from thy self does spring,
As though thou did all meaner praises scornee
Of Kings a Poet, and the Poets King,
They Princes, but thou Prophets do adorn;
Whilst others by their Empires are renowned,
Thou do enrich thy Scotland with renown,
And Kings can but with diadems be crowned,
But with thy laurel thou do crown thy Crown;
That they whose pens (even) life to Kings do give,
In thee a King, shall seek themselves to live.
To Lucy Countess of Bedford. Son. 61.
GReat Lady, essence of my chiefest good,
Of the most pure and finest tempred spirit,
Adorned with gifts ennobled by thy blood,
Which by descent true virtue do inherit:
That virtue which no fortune can deprive,
Which thou by birth take from thy gracious mother,
Whose royal minds with equal motion strive,
Which most in honor shall excel the other;
Unto thy fame my Muse herself shall task,
Which rainst upon me thy sweet golden showers,
And but thy self, no subject will I ask,
Upon whose praise my soul shall spend her powers.
Sweet Lady yet, grace this poor Muse of mine,
whose faith, whose zeal, whose life, whose al is thine.
To the Lady Anne Harington. Sonnet. 62.
MAdam, my words cannot express my mind,
My zealous kindness to make known to you,
When your deserts all severally I find;
In this attempt of me do crave their due:
Your gracious kindenes first does claim my heart,
Your bounty bids my hand to make it known,
Of me your virtues each do challenge part,
And leave me thus the least that is mine own:
What should commend your modesty and wit,
Is by your wit and modesty commended,
And stands dumb, in most admiring it,
And where it should begin, is only ended;
Returning this your praises only due,
And to your self, say you are only you.
To the Lady L. S. Sonet 63.
BRight star of Beauty, on whose cie-lids sit,
A thousand Nymph-like and enamoured graces,
The goddesses of memory and wit,
which in due order take their several places,
In whose dear bosom, sweet delicious love,
Lays down his quiver, that he once did bear,
Since he that blessed Paradise did prove,
Forsook his mothers lap to sport him there.
Let others strive to entertain with words,
My soul is of another temper made;
I hold it vile that vulgar wit affords,
Devouring time my faith shall not invade:
Still let my praise be honored thus by you,
Be you most worthy, whilst I be most true.
To sir Anthony Cook. Sonet 64.
VOuchsafe to grace these rude vnpollisht rimes,
Which but for you had slept in sable night,
And come abroad now in these glorious times,
Can hardly brook the pureness of the light.
But since you see their destenie is such,
That in the world their fortune they must try,
Perhaps they better shall abide the tuch,
Wearing your name their gracious liuerie.
Yet these mine own, I wrong not other men,
Nor traffike further then this happy clime,
Nor filch from Ports, not from Petrarchs pen,
A fault too common in this latter time;
Divine sir Philip, I avouch thy writ,
I am no picke-purse of another wit.
Finis.
The Legend of Robert Duke of Normandy.
WHat time soft night had silently begun,
To steal by minutes on the long-liu'd days,
The furious dog pursuing of the sun,
Whose noisome breath adds feruor to his rays,
That to the earth sends many a sad disease:
Which then inflamed with his intemprate fires,
Her self in light habiliments attires.
When the rathe morning newly but awake,
Scarce with fresh beauty burnished her brows,
Her self beholding in the general Lake,
(To which she paies her neuer-ceasing vows,)
With the new day me willingly to rowze;
Down to fair Thames I softly took my way,
Where the mild winds continually do play.
Striving to fancy his chaste breast to move,
Whereas all pleasures plentifully flow,
When him along the wanton tide does ^houe,
And to keep back they easily do blow,
Still meet him coming, thinking him too slow:
He forcing waves to check their hot embrace,
They fanning breath upon his cristall face.
Still forward sallying from his bounteous ^ource,
Along the shores lasciuio^sly does strain,
And often times retreating in his course,
As to his fountain he would back again,
Or turnde to look upon his silver (...);
With coy regards the goodly soil he greetes,
Till with fair Medway happily he meets.
Steering my compass by the wandering stream,
whose flight might teach me (...)es ne'r-turning howrs,
Delighted thus as in a pretty dream,
Where pleasure wholly had possessed my powers;
Yet looking back on Londons c(...)g Towers,
So Troy, thought I, her stately head did rear,
Whose crazed ribs the furrowing plow does eire.
Weary at length, a willow-tree I found,
Which on the bank of this great torrent stood,
Whose root, with rich grass greatly did abound,
(Forced by the moisture of the surging flood)
Ordained it seemde to sport her Nimphish brood;
Whose curled top denied the heavens great eye,
To view the stock he was maintained by.
The Lark that learnes observance to the Sun,
Quauers her clear notes in the quiet air,
That on the rivers murmuring base does run,
And the pl(...)sde heavens, their fairest livery ware,
The place such pleasure gently does prepare;
The flowers my scent, the flood my taste to steep,
Each sense thus s^ted, (...)ed me asleep.
When in a dream it seemed unto me,
Triumphall music from the flood arose,
As when the Sovereign we embargde do see,
And by fair London for his pleasure rows,
whose tender welcome the glad City shows;
The people swarming thick upon the shores,
And the curlde water ouer-spread with oa^es.
A troop of Nimphs came suddenly on land,
In the full end of this triumphall sound,
And me incompast, taking hand in hand,
Casting themselves about me in a round,
And so down set them on the easy ground;
Their sober eyes cast with a modest grace,
Upon my swarth and melancholy face.
Next, twixt two Ladies came a goodly Knight,
As newly brought from some distressefull place,
To me who seemed some right worthy wight,
Though his attire were miserable base,
Many deep furrows in his manly face;
And though cold age had frosted his fair hairs,
It rather seemde with sorrow then with years.
The one a Lady of a p^incely port,
Leading this sad Lord, scarcely that could stand,
The other fleering in disdainefull sort,
with scornful iestures drew him by the hand,
who lame and blind, yet bound with many a band;
which (I perceived nearer as they came)
That this was Fortune, that more constant fame.
Fame on the right hand in a robe of gold,
whose train old Time obsequiously did bear,
whereon in rich Embraudry was enrolde,
The acts of all the worthies ever wear,
which all might read depainted lively there,
Set down in lofty well composed verse,
First the great deeds of Heroes to rehearse.
On her fair breast she two broad Tablets wore,
Of cristall the one, the other Ebony,
where were engraven all the names of yore,
In the large Toombe of lasting memory,
Or the black book of endless obloquy;
The first with Poets and with Conquerours pilde,
That with base worldlings every where defilde.
And in her words appeared (as a wonder)
Her instant force and after-during might,
which softly spoke, far off were heard to thunder,
About the world that quickly took their flight,
And brought the most obscurest things to light:
That still the farther off, the greater still
Did sound our good, or manifest our ill.
Fortune, as blind as he whom she did lead,
Her feature often changing in an hour,
Fantastically carrying her head,
Soon would she smile, and suddenly would lower,
And with one breath, her words both sweet and sower
Upon her foes she amorously would glance,
And on her followers coily look askance.
About her neck in manner of a chain,
Torn diadems and broken scepters hung,
If any on her steadfastly did lean,
Them to the ground di^dainefully she flung;
And in this order as she passt along,
Great bags of gold out of her bosom drew,
Which to the unworthiest evermore she threw.
A duskie veil which hid her sightlesse eyes,
Like clouds that cover our uncertain lives,
wherein were portraide direful tragedies,
Fools wearing Crowns, and wisemen clogged in gives,
All things how she preposterously contriues;
That as a map her regency discouers,
In Camps, in Courts, and in the fate of lovers.
An easy bancke near to this place there was,
A seat fair Flora used to sit upon,
Curling her fair locks in this liquid glass,
Putting her rich gems and attirings on,
Fitter then this about us was there none;
Here set they down this poor distressed man,
And in this sort proud Fortune thus began.
Behold this Duke of Normandy quoth she,
The heir of William, Conqueror of this I Will,
Appealing to be iustifi'd by thee,
(whose tragedy this Poet must compile)
He of all other that I hold most vile,
His birth being markde with my unlucky brand,
For whom I see thou come preparde to stand.
What art thou but a tumor of the mind,
A bubble blown up with deceitful breath,
Which never yet exactly were definde,
In whom no wise man t'rereposed faith,
Speaking of few well until after death,
That from loose humor have thy timelesse birth,
Unknown to heaven, and less esteemde on earth?
First in opinion had thou thy creation,
On whom thou still do seruilely attend,
Like whom thou long retainst not any fashion,
But with the world uncertainly do wend,
Which as a post, does up and down thee send;
Without profane tongs, thou could never rise,
Nor be vpholden, were it not with lies.
In every corner prying like a thief,
And through each cranny subtilely do creep,
Apt to report, and easy of belief:
What Is he whose counsel thou did ever keep,
That into clossets sawcily darste peepe^
Telling for truth, what thou can but suppose,
And that divulging, thou should not disclose.
With extreme toil and labor thou art sought,
The way is danger leads to thy Cell,
Only with blood thy favor must be bought,
And who would have thee, fetches thee from hell,
Where thou impalde with fire and sword do dwell,
And when thou art in all this peril found,
What art thou? only but a tinckling sound.
Such as the world holds (of all other) base,
And of the rest reproachfully does scorn,
That amongst men sit in the seruilst place,
These be the creatures which thou do subborne,
Those wait on fame, whose weeds be nearly worn,
Yet these poor wretches come not unto thee,
Unless preferrde and dignifide by me.
Thy trumpe such men supposed to advance,
Is but as those fantastically deem,
Whom folly, youth, or vanity intrance,
Only to sound sufficing but to seem,
(Which the wise sort a dotage but esteem;)
And with this toy the humerous abusing,
Their wilful error, and thy fault excusing.
Except in peril thou do not appear,
And yet not then, but with intreates and wooing.
Flying often times when thou art very near
At hand, diminishd and augmented going,
On slightest things the greatest cost bestowing;
In promising their losses to repair,
When the performance is but only air.
On balefull hearses as the fittest grounds,
(Written with blood) thy sad memorialls lie,
Whose letters are immedicable wounds,
Only fit objects for the weeping eye,
And from the dust thou worth do only try;
And what sometime thou falsely did deprave,
Thou do acknowledge only in the grave.
The mighty Orb is witness of my power,
And how I reign with the eternal fates,
With whom I sit in council every hour,
On th'alterations of the times and states,
Them setting down their changes and their dates,
In fore-apointing every thing to come,
Until the great and universal doom.
The stars to me an everlasting book,
In that eternal register the sky,
Whose mighty volumes I do ouer-looke,
Still turning over the leaves of destiny;
which man I to inuiolate, deny,
And his frail will imperiously control,
By such strong clauses as I there enrole.
Predestination giving me a being,
whose depth man's wit could never thoroughly sound,
Into those secrets have I only seeing,
wherein wise Reason does herself confound,
Searching where doubts do more and more abound;
where sacred texts unlock the way to me,
To lighten those that will my glory see.
What names old Poets to their gods did give,
were ouely figures to express my might,
To show the virtues that in medo live,
And my great power in this all-moouing wight,
And all their altars unto me were dight;
Up alterations evermore did bring,
Matter whereon, continually they sing.
Still most uncertain varying in my course,
Yet in all changes aim one certain end,
Crossing man's forecast (he may know my force)
Still foe to none, to none a perfect friend,
To him least thought of, soonest I do send;
That all should find I worthily bestow,
Nor reasonvrge, but that I hold it so.
Forth of my lap I power abundant bliss,
All good proceeds from my all-giuing hand,
By me, man happy or unhappy is;
For whom I stick, or whom I do with stand,
And it is I am friendships only band;
And upon which, all greedily take hold,
Which being broke, love suddenly grows cold.
Pawsing, she frowns, when suddenly withal,
A fearful noise arises from the stood,
As when a tempest furiously does fall,
within the thick waste of some ancient wood,
That in amazement every mortal stood;
As though her words such powerfulness did bear,
That every thing her minaces did fear.
When fame yet smiling, mildely thus replies,
Alas quoth she, what labor thou have lost,
what wondrous mists thou casts before our eyes?
Yet will the gain not countervaile the cost;
what would thou say if thou had cause to boast,
which sett'st thy state forth in such wondrous sort,
Which but thy self, none ever could report^
A thing constrained only by event,
Breeding in some a transitory terror;
A, what men will, that comes by accident,
And only named to excuse their error.
What then is Fortune? or who does prefer her?
Or who to thee so foolish is to lean,
Which weak tradition only does maintain?
A toy whereon the doting world does dream,
Soothed by that uncertain observation
Of all attempts, that being the extreme,
Fastneth thereby on weak imagination;
Yet notwithstanding all this usurpation,
Unto thy self art incidently loathing
Most, when thou woldst be, that art rightly nothing.
That slightly by insinuating thus,
And under so allowable pretense,
Closely incroacheth on man's Genius,
In good and evil taking refidence,
And having got some small pre-eminence,
Unto thy self a being that would frame,
Finds in conclusion only but a name.
Those ignorant which made a god of Nature,
And Natures God divinely never knew,
Were those to Fortune did direct a stature,
From whom thy worship ignorantly grew,
Which being adored foolishly by few,
Grounded thy looser and uncertain laws,
Upon so weak and indigent a cause.
First, stouth did bear thee in her sleepy cell,
And thee with ease dishonourably fed,
Delivering thee with Cowardice to dwell,
Which with base thoughts continually thee bred.
By superstition idely being led:
A lewd imposture after did thee make,
Whom for a goddess fools do only take.
And as thy followers, nothing do forecast.
And as thou art improuident, as light;
And this the chiefest property thou have,
That against virtue thou bendst all thy might,
With whom thou wagest a continual fight;
The yielding spirit in fetters thou do bind,
But weak and slavish to the constant mind.
Such is thy froward and malignant kind,
That thou do all things crossely in despite,
Thou art inamored of a barbarous hind,
Whom thou do make thy only favorite,
None but the base in baseness does delight;
For were thou heavenly; thou in love would be,
with that which nearest does resemble thee.
But I alone the herald am of heaven,
Whose spacious kingdom shetcheth far and wide,
To every coast as swift as lightning driven,
And on the sunne-beames gloriously I ride;
Now mount I up, now down again I slide:
I register the worlds eternal hours,
That know the hid will of the immortal powers.
Men to the stars, me guiding them, do clime,
That all dimensions perfectly express,
And I alone the vanquisher of time,
Bearing that sweere that cures deaths bitterness,
That do all labor plentifully bless,
That all obstruse profundities impart,
Leading man through the tedious ways of Art.
My palace placed betwixt earth and skies,
Which many a Tower ambitiously vpheares,
Whereof the windows are composde of eyes,
The walls as strongly edifide of ears,
where every thing in heaven and earth appears,
Nothing so softly whispered in the round,
But through my palace presently does sound.
And underfoot floor'd all about with drums,
The rafters trumpets admirably clear,
Sounding alowde each name that thither comes,
The crannies tongues, and talking every where,
And all things past do in remembrance bear:
The doors unlock with every little breath,
And open wide with every word man says.
And throwout hung with arms and conquered spoils,
The posts whereon the goodly roof does stand,
Are Pillars graven with Herculian toiles,
Th'atchieuements great of many a warrelike hand,
Both in the christened and in heathen land;
Done by those Nobles that are most renowned,
Which there by me immortally are crowned.
Here in the bodies likeness whilst it lives,
Appear the thoughts proceeding from the mind,
To which the place a glorious habit gives,
When unto me they freely are resignde,
To be preserved there, by my power refinde;
That when the body by plae death does perish,
Then does this place the minds true Image cherish.
My beauty never Fades, but as newborn,
As years increase, so ever waxing young,
My strength is not diminished, nor worn,
What weakeneth all things, makes me only strong,
Nor am I subject unto worldly wrong,
The rape of time I carelessly defy,
Nor am I awde by all his tyranny.
The brow of heaven my monuments contain,
which is the mighty register of Fame,
which there in fiery characters remain,
The gorgeous seeling of the immortal frame,
The Constellations publishing my name
where my memorialls evermore abide,
In those pure bodies highly glorifide.
F(...) having ended, Fortune next began,
Further to urge what she before had said,
when (lo quoth she) duke Robert is the man,
which as my prisners I in bonds do lead,
For whom thou comst against me here to plead,
Whom I alone deprined of his crown,
Who can raise him that Fortune will have down?
A fitter instance (Fame replying) none,
Then is Duke Robert, Fortune do thy worst,
Greater to man thy might was never shown,
Doing on him what ever Fortune durst;
And since thy turn allotted thee the first,
Proceed; see which the Norman Duke shall have,
Whether that Fame, or Fortune and the grave.
Quoth Fortune then, I found th'vnstedfast star,
Whose lucklesse working limited his fate,
That marked his sad nativity with war,
And brothers most unnatural debate,
Public sedition and with private hate,
And on that good his father him begun,
Grounded the wrack and down fall of this son.
What bounteous nature franckly did bestow,
Wherein her best she strained her to try,
Thereby, himself I made him overthrow,
Above you both so powerful am I;
His breast to all so openly did lie,
Judged from his fashion differing so far,
For peace too mild, too merciful for war.
And yet the courage that he did inherit,
And from the greatness of his blood did take,
Though shrouded in so peaceable a spirit,
When now his wrong so roughly did awake,
Forthwith such fury violently brake,
As made the world impartially to see,
All humane actions managed by me.
That till revenge was wholly him bere^t,
Opposde against so absolute a power,
And him to lean on nothing being left,
when danger most him threatened to devour,
Unto the period of the utmost hour;
Him flattering still with promise of my love,
Did make him all extreamities to prove.
That whilst his father with the Norman sword,
On fruitful England prosperous entrance made,
I cast the project that this youthful Lord,
In the mean time should Normandy invade;
And with as proved and powerful a blade,
Him I persuaded (constantly by this)
To make his own yet doubtful to be his.
That Robert daily in disgrace might run,
As still the Conqueror toward his end did grow,
who well in years, thus vexed by his son,
which now his will so openly did show;
His state devised wisely to bestow,
For his own safety that his days to close.
He might himself more quietly repose.
And that lest time might cool his weakened blood,
This lucklesse war by lingering I supplide,
That whilst Duke Robert justly censured stood,
Under the weight of his unnatural pride;
In heat of all, this Conqueror William did,
Setting young Rufus on th'vnrightfull throne,
Leaving h(...) struggling for his own.
Which in small time so many mischiefs bred,
As sundry plagues on Williams of-springs sent,
Attaining to so violent a head,
which policy not after could prevent;
when to destruction all things headlong went,
And in the end, as consumating all,
Was Roberts irrecouerable fall.
When none could prosperous Nonmandie dissuade,
From sending ensigns to the English field,
Brother opposde the brother to invade,
Sword against sword, shield menaced to shield,
whose equal worth to other scorn to yield;
One arm a front, the others furious stroke,
Scepter with septer violently broke.
These sundry soiles, in both of which was sown,
By so approved and fortunate a hand,
The seed to both might prosprously have grown,
By their (...) in a mutual band;
Now when these Princes opposite do stand;
what them should foster, greater wounds them lent,
Then the prowd'st power that Europe could have sent.
Having my self won William in his life,
This conquered Realm to Rufus that did give,
Getting by strength what he did leave in strife,
Those to molest that after him should live;
In this advantage cunningly I drive,
T'afflict his issue with a general ill,
Yet the extreme in Robert to fulfill.
As when stowt Odo (that with William held,
Daily pricked forward by proud Lanfrancks spite,
Both powerful Prelates rigorously compeld,
