Rufus to leave abetting Roberts right,
Drawing both Mortaines and Mongomeries might,
Mangling the I Will with many a grievous scarre,
Scarcely yet cured of the former war.
That being set in so direct a way,
Strong friends at hand his enterprise to beck,
Ready before him when his entrance lay,
Of all supplide that he did lately lack,
Him I persuade the remedy to slack,
Stopping the course which he did lately run,
All to undo that he had ever done.
Thus did I stir up that unkindly rage,
That did so far prevail upon his blood,
And at my pleasure did again assuage,
When now this heat in sted might him have stood;
Thus with his humor altered I my mood,
That first by Arms his vigor he might lose,
Which then laid down gave strength unto his foes.
That by concluding this untimely peace,
I might thereby a lingering war begin,
That whilst these tumults did a little cease,
Craft more advantage cunningly might win;
Thus let I treason secretly in,
Giving deceitful Policy the kay,
To the fair closet where his counsels lay,
Thus reconciling outwardly a friend,
I drew an inward and a dangerous foe,
That all his wit ambitiously did lend,
To clothe his treasons in a virtuous show;
Which were contrived ^so currantly to go,
That secret malice strengthened more and more,
Lastly, should prove more dangerous then before.
And now poor Fame, my power to thee addressed,
And thee mine only instrument I made,
That whilst these brothers at this pass do rest,
Him to the wars I won thee to persuade,
With those that now were going to invade,
With great Duke Godfrey pressing for his bands,
From Pagans power t'regaine the holy lands.
His youthful humor finely thus I feed,
The mean most fit to draw him forth abroad,
When now at home his presence most should need,
In foreign lands to fasten his abode,
Him in this order only I bestowed;
That William dying, Robert being gone,
Henry might seat him on his brothers throne.
So sweet the sounds of these aduent'rous Arms,
And every sense so strougly they do bind,
That he has now no feeling of his harms,
So far away transported is his mind,
Declaring well the greatness of his kind;
That him so high and forcibly does bear,
As when most cause, he least his ill does fear.
Him having thrown into eternal thrall,
Wisely fore-casting how the same should be,
When every thing made fit unto his fall,
Which none could hinder, though the most foresee,
For which I made an instrument of thee;
For where destruction sadly I pretend,
Mischief like lines, all to their center bend.
He gone, and William yielded up the breath,
The younger Henry covetous of reign,
Offered so fairly by his brothers death,
whilst Robert does in Palestine remain,
And now a Kingdom easily might gain;
what by his power and science to persuade,
Himself a Monarch absolutely made.
Whilst this great Duke embraced is by thee,
which thou as thine do absolutely claim,
Finding mere shadows only missing me,
And idle Castles in the air does frame;
Lot, such a mighty Monarchesse is Fame,
That what she gives, so easy is to bear,
As none therefore needs violence to fear.
Until returning from those holy wars,
So highly honored with the Pagans flight,
From foreign battles unto civil ^arres,
And getting others for his own to fight,
Enforced to use the utmost of his might;
with that rich sword in Pagan blood imbrued,
Himself to save by his own friends pursued.
When wanting sums, the sinews of his force,
which his great courage quickly comes to find,
Even in the high speed of his forward course,
So skilfully I managed his mind,
That I a way out readily did find;
To his destruction, Henry to supply,
His future safety happily to buy.
Him by all ways to amity to win,
Not fully yet established as he would,
Having thus far already gotten in,
Setting himself substantially to hold,
By the francke offers of bewitching gold;
The yearly tribute from his Crown to rise,
Which might all former injuries suffice.
Which entertaind by confident belief,
By which to pass his purposes were brought,
Not yet suspicious of this secret thief,
By which he soon and cunningly was caught;
Of which the least when princely Robert thought,
Even in a moment did annoy him more,
Then all their power could ever do before.
Which to this great Lord utterly unknown,
Not understanding, easily could not fly,
Into his way that subtly was thrown,
which to avoid, Duke Robert looked too hie,
Into good minds fraud does the soonest pry;
whose pliant nature I securely chose,
To work what form it pleased me to dispose.
This fatal tribute cutting off the claim,
A lawful Prince to Englands Empire laid,
His former right does altogether maime,
As they agreed yearly to be paid;
Thereon relying after being staid,
As from a fountain plenteously did spring,
The efficient cause of Roberts ruining.
Whenas his friends so well to him that meant,
And for this day did wholly them prepare,
Seeing him thus their purpose to prevent,
And how thereby it was like with him to fare;
Now upon Henry bended all their care,
Giving their power their peace with him to make,
Gathered at first the Norman part to take.
And I by whom yet every thing had been,
Since Norman William conquest here begun,
To show my self the worlds emperious Queen,
Direct my course against his eldest son,
Things falling out disastrously to run,
On Englands part against Normandy to stand,
Conquered but lately by the Norman hand.
The conquest William made upon this I Will,
When Norman blood the Englishmen did tame,
That native now enriched with her style,
Turnde with revenge to captiuate the same,
As back descending whence it lately came,
As Norman power did English thrall provoke,
That Norman necks should bear the English yoke.
For which, being up, and fallne to open Arms,
Such mortal hate between them daily grows,
And pressing in where deadly peril swarms,
The angry brothers dealing furious blows,
Back to regaine what they before did loose,
Either to quit him of the others thrall,
Either to rise, or evermore to fail.
Him on whom late in Palestine I smilde,
Returnde from France now dreadfully I frown,
Being called home that chiefly is exilde,
And in his kingdom only leaves his crown,
Him in the deluge of mischance to drown:
Lastly, himself contemptfully does lose,
Leaving his realm to his injurious foes.
Which home to England prisner do him bring,
Left as a spoil and prey unto his own,
Become her captive should have bin her king,
Such was the lot upon his life was thrown,
Where he remaining in continual moan,
Prescribed to one poor solitary place,
Whose lawful bounds the ocean did embrace,
Could humane knowledge comprehend my hate,
Or reason sound the depth of things divine,
The world amazed at Duke Roberts state,
Might think no might to be comparde with mine,
And all the chances unto me resign,
In Roberts fall apparently to see,
Amongst the stars the strength that rests in me.
That sword upon him which resumes such power,
Yet is too weak to consumate his days,
Time, whose swift course does every thing devour,
In his most need, prolongs him with delays,
Whilst he his brothers tyranny obeies,
That he in life a thousand deaths might die,
In every course so forcible am I.
And whilst in such extremity he lies,
Depriude of all comfort but the blessed light,
Yet it was not this that could my rage suffice,
But to abridge that, rob him of his sight,
To suit his days directly with the night;
That, that to all men lastly which should be,
Due to the wretchedst, him denide by me.
That Robert so infortunately blind,
No outward object night dispearse his care,
The better to illuminate his mind,
To see his sorrows thoroughly what they are,
To do so much unto this Prince I dare,
That being deprived of that which was the chief,
Did of the other amplify the grief.
And when bereaved of his nightly rest,
With the remembrance of so great a wrong,
Fastening so deeply on his pensive breast,
His heart the while that violently stung,
Nature in him does show herself so strong;
That grief, which many does of life deprive,
Seems to preserve and keeps him still alive.
Him I denide his enemy to kill,
Nor by his own hand wretchedly to die,
That life unto him should be loathsome still,
And that death from him evermore should fly,
Making them both to him an enemy;
Willing to die by life, him double killing,
Urged to live, twice dying he unwilling,
So many years as he has worn a Crown,
So many years as he has hopde to rise,
So many years upon him did I frown,
So many years he lives without his eyes,
So many years in dying e're he dies,
So many years shut up in prison strong,
Sorrow does make the shortest time seem long.
Thus sway I in the course of earthly things,
That time might work him everlasting spite,
To show how I can tyrannize on Kings,
And in the fall of great ones do delight,
In fined things my working infinite;
All worldly changes at my will disposed,
For that in me all wonder is enclosed.
At Fortunes speech amazed whilst they stand,
And Fame herself yet wondreth at his woe,
When from Duke Robert Fortune takes her hand,
Whose misery she thus had let them know;
When now to answer her dispightfull foe,
Fame from deep silence seeming to awake,
Thus for her Client modestly bespake.
Quoth she, returning from renowned Rome,
Seeking my self in Europe to advance,
To win her Princes to regaine the tomb,
Which had been lost by her misgouernance,
Calling to England, Germany and France
(At length) persuaded happily by me,
From Pagan hands fair Palestine to free.
That holy hermit long that did bemone
This their so great and evident a loss,
With famous Godfrey forwards that was gone,
Bearing the banner of the bloody Cross.
Now whilst in so fair forwardnes it was,
And every care attentive now did stand,
To this great buisnes only then in hand.
Thither did all the noblest spirits resort,
Which I that time successefully did bring,
Allured by the confident report
That from so great an enterprise did spring.
T'aduenture in so popular a thing;
And no man deemed worthy to be mine,
That was not forward in this great design.
Where now this duke the Conqu'rors eldest son,
Which with his birthright Normandy did wrest,
When of what else his noble father won,
His brother Rufus strongly was possessed;
Which whilst he strives from his hands to rest,
This great attempt now gloriously broke forth,
Which was by me divulged through the north.
Which having got free entrance to his care,
Such entertainment happily did find,
As no persuasion suffers to be there,
From this high purpose to divert his mind,
And being so religiously inclinde,
wooed with this offer fitly does prepare
Himself to furnish to this great affair.
That kingdom he does carelessly neglect,
His brother Rufus wrongfully does keep,
And only that does constantly respect,
where he once in his sepulcher did sleep,
At whose dear death the very rocks did weep,
His crown of gold this christian Prince does scorn,
So much he loved him that was crowned with thorn.
The want that him did grievously oppress,
Of those great sums in leuying power were spent,
Himself again of England to possess,
Much hinders his religious intent,
Yet could not this his purpose so prevent,
Although a while it seemd delay to make,
Of that which he did bravely undertake.
Wherefore this noble and high spirited Lord.
whilst now his buisnes stands at this stay,
And since his state no better could afford,
In gage to Henry Normandy does lay,
Providing first his soldiers how to pay,
Rather himself chose kingdomlesse to leave,
His countries hopes then basely to deceive.
To his victorious ensign comes from far,
Th'inlled Red-shanks touched with no remorse,
The light-foote Irish that with darts do war,
The Scot so much delighting in his horse,
The English Archer of a Lions force,
The valiant Norman most his troops among,
With the brave Britton wonderfully strong.
Remote from comfort in this colder clime,
To other Countries kindly does you bring,
(And wisely teach you to redeem the time,
Whence your eternal memory might spring)
Unto the place whereas the heavenly King.
Your dear redemption happily began,
Living on earth, which was both God and man.
Poor Ilanders which in the Oceans chain,
Too long imprisoned from the cheerful day,
Your warlike leader brings you to the maine,
Which to my Court does show the open way,
And his victorious hand becomes the key;
Unto so high adventures that you bears,
Glory to you, and honor to your heirs.
And does thereto so zealously proceed,
As those fair locks his temples that adorn,
Until the great Jerusalem were freed,
He made a vow should never more be shorn,
Which since they so religiously were worn;
In every eye did beautify him more,
Then did the Crown of Normandy before.
Whilst he still on his upright course does hold,
As we the sequel briefly shall relate,
Bearing himself (as worthily he could)
And best became his dignity and state,
Teaching how his themselves should moderate,
Not following life, so with his chance content,
Nor flying death, so truly valient.
So did he all his faculties bestow,
That every thing exactly might be done,
That due foresight before the act might go,
Others gross errors happily to shun,
Wisely to finish well that was begun;
Justly directed in the course of things,
By the straight rule from sound experience springs.
Idle regards of greatness that did scorn,
Careless of pomp, magnificent to be,
That man reputing to be noblest borne,
That was the most magnanimous and free,
In honor so impartial was he,
Esteeming titles meritlesse and naught,
Unless with danger absolutely bought.
Giving the soldier comfortable words,
And often imbalmes his well-receiued wound,
To him that needed maintenance affords,
To brave attempts encouraging the sound,
Never dismaide in any perrill found,
His Tent a seat of judgment to the grieved,
And as a Court to those should be releeu'd.
So perfect was that rarifying fire,
That did compose and rectify his mind,
Unto that place that raised his desire,
Above the usual compass of his kind,
And from the world so clearly him refinde,
As him did wholly consecrate to glory,
A subject fit whereon to build a story.
Who in embassage to the Emperor sent,
Passing along through Macedon and Thrace,
Never did sleep but only in his tent,
Till he reviewd that famous Godfreis face,
Nor till he came unto that hallowed place,
Never did repose his body in a bed,
Such were the cares possess his troubled head.
O wherefore then great singer of thy days,
Renowned Tasso in thy noble story,
Should thou be taxed as partial in his praise,
And yet so much should set forth others glory?
Me thinks for this thou should be inly sorry,
That thou should leave another to recite,
That which so much thou did neglect to write.
There was not found in all the christian host,
Any than he more forward to the field,
Nor their battallions could another boast,
To bear himself more bravely with his shield,
So well his arms this noble Duke could wield,
As such a one he properly should be,
That^ I did mean to consecrate to me.
Of so approved and deliver force,
Charging his lance or brandishing his blade,
Whether on foot, or managing his horse,
That open passage through the ranckes he made,
At all assaies so happy to invade,
That were he absent in the charge or chase,
It was supposde the day did loose the grace.
In doubtful fights where danger soon'st did fall,
He would be present ever by his will,
And where the Christians for supplies did call,
Thither through peril Robert pressed still,
To help by valor, or relieve by skill;
To every place so prouidently seeing,
As power in him had absolutely being.
When in the morn his Courser he bestrid,
He seemd composde essentially of fire,
But from the field he ever drowping rid,
As he were vanquished only to retire,
Nearest his rest, the furth'st from his desire,
And in the spoils his soldiers share the crowns,
They rich in gold, he only rich in wounds.
And when the fair Jerusalem was won,
And king thereof they gladly him would make,
All worldly titles he so much does shun,
As he refusde the charge on him to take,
One the vain world that clearly did forsake,
So far it was from his religious mind,
To mix things vile with those of heavenly kind.
No triumph did his victories adorn,
But his high praise for sinful man that did,
Nor other mark of victory is worn,
But that red Cross to tell him crucifide,
All other glories that himself denide:
A holy life so willingly he leads,
In dealing alms, and bidding of his beads.
Thus a poor Pilgrim he returns again,
For glittring arms in Palmers homely gray,
Leaving his Lords to lead his warrelike train,
Whilst he alone comes sadly on the way,
Dealing abroad his lately purchasde pray;
An aged staff his careful hand does hold,
That with a lance his heathen foe controlde.
But now to end this long continued strife,
Henceforth thy malice takes no further place,
The hate thou bar'st him ended with his life,
By thee his spirit can suffer no disgrace,
Now in mine arms his virtues I embrace,
His body thine, his crosses witness be,
But mine his mind, that from thy power is free.
Thou gave up rule when he gave up his breath,
And where thou end'st, even there did I begin,
Thy strength was buried in his timelesse death,
when as thy Conqueror lastly came I in,
That all thou gotst from thee again did win;
To whom thy right thou wholly did resign,
That all thou had was absolutely mine.
To the base world then Fortune get thee back,
The same with drery tragedies to fill,
There by thy power bring all things unto wrack,
And on weak mortalles only work thy will,
And since so much thou do delight in ill,
Hear his complaint, who wanting eyes to see,
May give thee sight, which art as blind as he.
At her great words amazed whilst they stand,
The Prince which looked dreadfully and grimme,
Bearing his eyes in his distressefull hand,
Whose places stood with blood unto the brimme,
In the great anguish shaking every limme;
After deep sighs and lamentable throws,
Thus began at length to utter forth his woes.
Says he, farewell, the lights are now put out,
And where they were, is buried all my joy,
That are with darkness compassed about,
Which tyranny did wilfully destroy,
To breed my more perpetual annoy,
That even that sense I only might forgo;
That could alone give comfort to my woe.
You which beheld fair Palestine restorde,
And from profane hands of the Pagans freed,
The Sepulcher of that most gracious Lord,
And seen the mount where his dear wounds did bleed
That with these sights my zealous soul did feed;
Since from your functions night does you disseuer,
Seclude me now from worldly joys for ever.
They saw no Sun, nor did they view the day,
Except a candle, they beheld no light,
Strong walls before those blessings kept away,
What could be feard'? they could not hurt the night,
For then tears wholly hindered them of sight;
O then from whence should Henry's hate arise,
Though I saw nothing that I should have eyes?
The wretchedst thing the most despisedst beast,
Enioyes that sense as generally as we,
The very Gnat, or what than that is least,
Of sight by nature kindly is made free,
What thing has mouth to feed, but eyes to see,
O that a tyrant then should me deprane,
Of that which else all living creatures have:
Whilst yet the light did mittigate my moan,
Tears found a mean to sound my sorrows deep,
But now ay me, that comfort being gone,
By wanting eyes wherewith I erst did weep,
My cares alas concealed I must keep;
O God, that blindness stealing my delight,
Should above all things give my sorrow sight
Where sometime stood the beauties of this face,
Those lamps once lighted with the vestall flame,
Is now a dungeon, a distressed place,
A harbor fit for infamy and shame,
Which but with horror none can scarcely name;
Out of whose dark grates misery and grief,
Starued for vengeance, daily beg relief.
The day abhorres me, and me still does fly,
Night still me follows, yet too long does stay,
This never comes though it be ever nie,
And this in coming vanishes away,
What now me booteth either night or day;
All Is one, still day, or be it ever night,
Since one to me the darkness and the light.
You wherewith once my comforts I did view,
Th'alcouering heaven and glory that it bears,
No more the same shall e're be seen of you,
That happy sight that every mortal cheares,
No more to me for ever now appears;
Betake you selves unto your darkesome cell,
And bid the world eternally farewell.
His speech thus ending, Fortune discontent,
Turning herself as she away would fly,
Playing with babes and fools incontinent,
As never tutch'd with humane misery,
As what she was herself to verify;
And strait forgetting what she had to tell,
To other speech and girl^sh laughter fell.
When graceful Fame conueying thence her charge,
With all these troops that did to her resort,
Gave me this book, wherein was writ at large,
His life set out in admirable sort,
T'amaze the world with this so true report,
But Fortune angry with her foe therefore,
Gave me this gift, that I should still be pore.
FINIS.
The Legend of Matilda
IF yet a Muse there happily remain,
That is by truth so diligently taught,
As vninstructed wantonly to feign,
Declares but what modestly she ought;
If this be such which I so long have sought,
I crave by her my life may be reuealde,
By black oblivion enuiously concealde.
Oh if such favor I might hope to find,
Here in this world yet once to live again,
As I yet lastly might express my mind,
By the endeavor of a powrefull pen,
In all my sorrows happy were I then;
Three hundredth years by all men ouer-past,
Finding one friend to pity me at last.
O you of him so happily elect,
Whom I entreat to prosecute my story,
Lady most dear, most worthy all respect,
The worlds best jewel, and your sexes glory,
It shall suffice me, be Idea sorry,
Reading my Legend sadly in his verse,
Which now alone must serve me for a her^^.
(...) you the pattern by whose perfect view,
Like your fair self he wisely may me make,
For sure alive none fitter is then you,
Whose form unspotted chastity may take,
Be you propitious, for whose only sake
For me I know he will gladly do his best,
So you and I may equally be blessed.
Bright Rosamond so highly that is graced,
Inroled in the register of same,
That in our saincted calendar is placed,
By him who strives to stellifie her name;
Yet will the modest say she was too blame,
Though full of state and pleasing be his rhyme,
Yet can his skill not expiate her crime.
The wife of Shore winnes general applause,
Finding a pen laborious in her praise,
^lstr^d revived to plead her pitied cause,
After the envy of so many days,
Happie's the man their glories highest can raise;
Thus the loose wanton liked is of many,
Vice shall find friends, but virtue seldom any.
To vaunt of my Nobility were vain,
Which were I know, not bettered by the best,
Nor should beseem an honorable strain,
And me a maiden fits not of the rest,
Nor worldly titles fondly will suggest;
A virtuous life I mean to boast alone,
Our birth our syres, our virtues be our own:
It Is shame to fetch our long descent from Kings,
If from the gods derived thou should be,
The old atchieuements of those wondrous things,
Which thou thy self then liuedst not to see,
What be their acts material unto thee?
Staining that blood and honor that was theirs,
Which could not leave their virtues to their heirs.
Heaven powered down more abundance on my birth,
Then it before had usually bestowed,
And was in me so bountiful to earth,
As though the fullness meaning to have showed,
On me it so immeasurably flowed,
That such a shape with such a spirit inspired,
Did of the wisest make me most desired.
Upon my brow sat Beauty in her pride,
Unto the world as ministering her law,
And unto all such riches did divide,
As unto her all generally did draw;
And yet mine eye did keep them so in awe,
As that which only could true virtues measure,
Ordainde by Nature to preserve her treasure.
My carrige such as might content the wise,
My speech such comely decen^^e retain,
As of the younger was not deemde precise,
Nor of the aged was accounted vain;
So well instructed to observe the mean,
So well compact, and native was cach good,
That did coher^ with temper of my blood.
Nature in me did such perfections vary,
As that the least allowed not of compare,
And yet so well did teach me them to carry,
That then themselves did make them seem more rare,
As in my portion suffering none to share,
In her fair grace by placing me so hie,
That there should sit the darling of the sky.
When Fame began my beauty first to blaze,
That soon became too lavish of the same,
Having her trumpet laden with my praise,
That every place was filled with my name,
For which report thou too much were too blame;
But unto thee is beauty subject still,
Which I may say, is causer of our ill.
This jealous monster has a thousand eyes,
Her ayrie body has as many wings,
Now about earth, now up to heaven she flies,
And here and there with every breath she flings,
Even from the deep her messages she brings,
Nothing so secret but to her appears,
As apt to credit every thing she hears.
And Princes ears as open to report,
As skill in blazing beauty to a King,
Subject unto the censure of the Court,
From whence Fame carries, thither she does bring,
And which soever she does lowdly ring,
Thither ah me unhappily she brought,
Where I my bar unfortunately caught.
There stood my beauty boldly for the prize,
Where the most clear and perfectst judgments be,
And of the same the most judicial eyes,
Did give the gole impartially to me;
So did I stand unparalleled and free,
And like a comet in the euenings sky,
Struck with amazement every wondering eye.
This it was possessed the breast of princely John,
This on his hart-strings endless music made,
This wholly won him unto it alone,
And fully did his faculties invade,
From which not reason ever could dissuade;
This taught his eyes their due attendance still,
Holding the reins which ruled his princely will.
When yet my father fortunate in Court,
And by his blood ranck'd equal with the best,
Having his quick ear touched with this report,
Which yet the news but hardly could digest,
And on my youth his only care did rest;
Straightly pursues it by those secret spies,
As still in Courts attend on Princes eyes.
And he thus while who seemed but to sleep,
Till he the Princes purposes could sound,
And to himself yet secretly did keep,
What he but late had prouidently found;
So well that wise Lord could conceal his wound,
That well foresaw how daugerous it would prove,
To cross the course of his impatient love.
When having found how violent a flame,
Unbridled will had kindled in the King,
If on the sudden he should stop the same,
A greater inconvenience might bring,
Which being known so dangerous a thing,
Me does bethink him fittest to persuade,
E're for my safety further means he made.
Dear girl quoth he, thou see who does await,
T'intrap that beauty bred to be thy foe,
Being so fair and delicate a bait,
Tempting all eyes themselves there to bestow,
Whose power the King is taught too soon to know;
Of his desire that what the end may be,
Thy youth may fear my knowledge does foresee.
And for thou live publicly in Court,
Whose privilege does every mean protect,
Where the ensample of the greatest sort,
Does more then opportunity effect,
None thriuing there that dwell upon respect,
Being a lottery where but few do win,
Falsehood th'aduenture, and the prize but sin.
Subt'ly opposing to thy longing sight,
What may to pleasure possibly provoke,
And fitly fashioned unto thy delight,
That with the grauest strikes too great a stroke,
Having withal emperious power thy cloak;
With such strong reasons on her part propounded,
As may leave virtue seemingly confounded.
Many the ways inducing to thy fall,
And to thy safety none is left to guide thee,
And when thy danger greatest is of all,
Even then thy succor soonest is denide thee:
So sundry means from virtue to divide thee,
Having with all mortality about thee,
Frailty with in, temptation set without thee.
The leachers tongue is never void of guile,
Nor wants he tears when he would win his pray,
The subtilst tempter has the smoothest style,
Sirens sing sweetly when they would betray,
Lust of it self had never any stay,
Nor to contain it bounds could have deuisde,
That when most filled, is least of all suffisde.
With every mean and majesty is fraught,
That all things has contained in his power,
And who will conquer, leaves no mean vnsaught,
Soft golden drops did pierce the brazen Tower,
Watching the advantage of each passing hour,
Time offering still each hour to do amiss,
Thy banefull poison spiced with thy bliss.
And when this heady and vnseasned rage,
Which in his blood does violently reign,
Time, that the heat shall peceably assuage,
Shall show the more apparently thy stain,
Which unto ages ever shall remain;
Sin in a chain leads on her sister shame,
And both in gyues fast fettered to defame.
Kings use their loves as garments they have worn,
Or as the meat whereon they fully fed,
The Saint once gone, who does the shrine adorn?
Or what is Nectar carelessly if shed,
what Princes wealth redeemes thy maidenhead,
which should be held as precious as thy breath,
whose desolution consumates thy death.
The stately Eagle on his height does stand,
And from the maine the fearful foul does smite,
Yet scorns to tuch it lying on the land,
When he has felt the sweet of his delight,
But leaves the same a prey to every kite;
With much we surffet, plenty makes us poor,
The vvretched Indian spurns the golden ore.
When now he points the periode with a tear,
which in my bosom made so great a breach,
As every precept firmly fixed there,
And still his council unto me did preach,
A father so effectually should teach,
That then his words I after ever found,
Written on so immaculate a ground.
The youthful king deluded but the while,
That in his breast did bear this quenchlesse fire,
Whilst flattring hope his senses does beguile,
That with fresh life still quickened his desire,
And gone so far now meant not to retire;
Thinks if that aptly winning him but place,
By love or power to purchase him my grace.
Which still deferring, found he still did fail,
Nor to his mind aught (kindly) took effect.
Courageously resolving to assail,
That other means does utterly neglect,
In spite what fear could any way object,
And finding time, not booting to be mute,
Thus to me lastly did prefer his suit.
Dear maid (quoth he) when Nature had ordained
Thee to the world her workmanship to bring,
All other creatures knowing she had stained,
By so divine and excellent a thing,
Only therefore to gratify a King,
Sealed thee the Charter dated at thy birth,
Mirror of heaven, the wonder of the earth.
Hoord not thy beauty, heaven does give thee store,
Pity such treasure should lie idely dead,
Which being imparted shall increase the more,
And by the interest evermore be fed,
To be man's comfort that was only bred,
which of it self is of such power and might,
As like the sun joys all things with the sight.
From those bright stars such streams of lightning glide
As through the eyes do wound the very heart,
Whose virtues may be sundry ways applide,
Hurting and healing like Achilles dart;
Such bounty Nature did to them impart,
Those lamps two planets clearer then the seven,
That with their splendour light the world to heaven.
Had Art such colors as could truly show,
Each rare perfection rightly in his kind,
And on each one sufficiently bestow,
Unto the glory properly assignde,
Painting the beauties aptly to the mind,
But O alone thy excellence is such,
As words though many lessen worth so much.
He is thy king, who is become thy subject,
Sometimes thy Lord now servant to thy love,
Thy graceful features be his only object,
Who for thy sake a thousand deaths durst prove,
A Princes prayer should some compassion move;
Let woolues and bears be cruel in their kinds,
But women meek and have relenting minds.
Daine (dear) to look upon these brimfull eyes,
With tides of tears continually frequented,
Where hope without food hunger-staruen lies,
which to betray me trecherously consented,
That for the fact being lawfully conuented,
Iudgde in these waters still to have their being,
For their presumption thy perfections seeing.
Sit thou commanding under mine estate,
Having thy tresses honored with my Crown,
As not unworthy of a meaner fate
And make the prowd'st to tremble with a frown,
Raise whom thou will, cast who^ it please thee down:
And be my love renowned through this I Will,
With all the titles fame may thee instile.
What if my Queen repining at our bliss,
Thee as did Juno Jove's fair darling keep,
Mine I Will preserve as that great god did his,
Wise Mercury lulld Argus eyes to sleep,
Love ever laughs when jealousy does weep,
When most she stirs, our power shall keep her under,
She may raise storms, but we do rule the thunder.
Thus having made the entrance to his love,
Which he supposde assuredly in time,
Of better tidings messenger might prove,
By which he after to his joys might clime,
And of my youth now being in the prime,
Leaves me, not knowing well which way to turn me,
warmed with the fire which unawares might burn me.
Upon my weakness that so strongly wrought,
Whilst in my breast a mutiny arose,
Fear and Desire a doubtful combat fought,
As like two eager and ambitious foes,
This strives to win, the other fears to loose;
By this often cleared, and by that accused,
Flattred by that most, most which me abused.
And in my self that well suspected treason,
Knowing who watched to have me for his pray,
And in so apt and dangerous a season,
when youth and beauty bare so great a sway,
And where he battery still to me might lay,
who girt so strongly every way about,
well might suspect I could not long hold out.
All doubts end fears cast utterly aside,
Resolved at last away from Court to go,
Whither it pleased my happy stars to guide,
There I my self determinde to bestow,
Until time might this passion ouer-blow,
Or if at least it wrought not the extrusion,
Might lend me power to help my resolution.
When he whose care this while me not forsook,
That many a sweet sleep for my safety brake,
Much that was pleased with the course I took,
As one that truly suffered for my sake,
Did his abode at Baynards Castle make,
which since the Court so happly did leave one,
To his protection kindly did receive me.
Whence sorrow seemed utterly exilde,
wherein my life I long before did waste,
The present time that happily beguilde,
with thought of that which was already past,
where I was now so fortunately placed;
Even as a bird escaped the Fowlers snare,
whom former danger warns to beware.
When now the King whose purposes were crossd,
which this evasion subt'ly did prevent,
And that the mean to which he trusted most,
was that which most did frustrate his intent,
Seeing his suit preposterously went,
Another course bethinkes himself to run,
Else now as far off, as when first begun.
Thenceforth devising to dissolve the mass,
That lay so full betwixt him and the light,
That in his suit so great a hindrance was
And least exspected wrought him most dispite,
Finding the cause that all things went not right,
Casts forth with my father to remove,
To make the way more cleerer to his love.
When scarcely cured of that sickely qual(...)
And that my heart was happily at case,
But as a ship that in a gentle calm,
Floates up and down upon the quiet seas,
By some rough gust some adverse star does raise,
Driven again into the troubled maine,
wh(...) well had hopde securely to have lain.
The powrefull Prince whom I did thus reject,
Heereon in Court does first this Peer disgrace,
Thereby to give the people to suspect,
To Offend in some thing sitting near his place,
Them it (by all means) urging to embrace;
Which if he clearly modeld out, that path
Should give a passage freely to his wrath.
And giving color to his ran'ckrous hate,
By such false council as to him he drew,
Cunning in all the stratagems of state,
My guiltless father ceaselesly pursue,
Whose kingly power too quickly overthrew
Him which I hoped me succor should have given,
Till from all refuge absolutely driven.
And not their clear and intellectual sight,
Into the quarrel that did thoroughly look,
Nor our alies that to their utmost might
Against his proceeding that our party took,
Unto the end that never me forsook;
Could the effect of his great power prevent,
To stay from France my banished father sent.
Not all his service to his sovereign done,
In war as valiant as in council sound,
Which from this Prince compassion might have won,
To him that faithful evermore was found,
How deeply thou ingratitude do wound:
Sure first devised to no other end,
But to grieve those whom nothing could offend.
Ay me forsaken, left unto my foe,
Thus by my fortune fro wardly betraide,
Never poor maiden was besieged so,
And all depressed that should lend me aid,
Such weight the heavens upon my birth had laid,
Yet her due merit virtue never loses,
Against her fair course though heaven it self opposes.
Embarkt for France his sd dejected eyes,
Filled with tears in plentifullest store,
His parting threatened by the lowering skies,
Then under sail from sight of any shore,
Fear him behind, and sorrow him before;
Wasting withal his sad laments in vain,
To the rude waters only to complain.
When like a dear before the hounds imboste,
when him his strength begins to forsake,
Leaves the smoothe lawnes to which he trusted most,
And to the covert does it self betake,
Doubling that creepes from brake again to brake;
Thus still I shift me from the Princes face,
which has me now continually in chase.
The coast now clear, suspicion laid to rest,
And each thing fit to further his intent,
which with much pleasure quieted his breast,
That every thing so prosperously went,
And if the rest successefully consent,
Of former aid that being quite forsaken,
He hopes the fort may easily be taken.
A Princes arms are stretched from shore to shore,
Kings sleeping see with eyes of other men,
Craft finds a key to open every door,
Little it boots in walls my self to pen,
The lamb enclosed in the lions den;
whose watchful eyes too easily descride me,
And found me soonst, where ^ur'st I thought to hide me.
My paths by spies are diligently noted,
Over me he holds so vigilant a watch,
And on my beauty he so fondly doted,
That at my looks he enuiously did catch;
Ready that stood attending at my ^atch,
where jealous love continually did ward,
Treason my handmaide, Falshoode on my guarde.
Wherefore since this so badly sorted out,
He to my shifts so narrowly me drove,
Another course must needesly cast about,
Where safer harbor happily to have,
Since insufficient this was me to save,
His power so spacious every way did lie,
That still I stood in his ambitious eye.
And fear which taught me every way to prove,
When I of many long time did debate,
Me at the last it pleased the powers to move,
To take upon me a religious state,
The holy Cloister none might violate;
Where after all these storms I did endure,
There yet at last might hope to live secure.
Wherefore to Dunmow secretly conuaide,
Unto a house that sometime was begun,
By Iuga, of our ancestry, a maid,
At whose great charge this Monastry was done,
In which she after did become a Nun;
And kept her order strictly with the rest,
Which in that place virginity professed.
Where I my self did secretly bestow,
From the vain world which I too long had tried,
One whom affliction taught my self to know,
My youth and beauty gently that did chide,
And me instructing as a skillful guide;
Printed with all such coldness in my blood,
That it might so perpetuate my good.
The King who with an envious eye did see,
His power deluded, strongly discontent,
who thence his power not possibly could free,
Which his sad breast does grievously torment;
which since that I so wilfully was bent,
And he past hope now ever to enjoy me,
Resolves by some means lastly to destroy me.
And he that knew one fit for such a fact,
To whom he durst his secret thoughts impart,
One that for him would any thing enact,
And in performance wanted not his Art,
That had a strong hand, a relentlesse heart;
On him the King (in madness so enraged)
Imposde my death, himself thereto that gagged.
Who making haste the fatal deed to do,
Thither repaires, but not as from the King,
One that did well know what belonged thereto,
Nor therein needed any tutoring,
But as one sent upon some needful thing;
with a smooth countenance and with settled brows,
Obtaines to get in where I paid my vows.
Where I alone and to his tale exposde,
As one to him a willing ear that lent,
Aye me, too soon himself to me disclosde,
And who it was that him unto me sent,
From point to point, relating his intent;
Which whilst I stood struck dumb with this invasion,
He thus pursues me strongly with persuasion.
Says he but hear how greatly thou do err,
Fondly to dote upon thine own perfection,
Whenas the King thee highly will prefer,
And that his power desires thy protection;
So indiscreetly sort not thy election,
To shut up in a melancholy cell,
That in the Court ordained was to dwell.
How dangerous is so bountiful an offer,
If thy neglect do retchlesly abuse it,
who was it ever that did see a coffer,
Filled with gold, and proffered did refuse it;
Greater thy fault the more thou do excuse it,
Thy self condemning in thine own good hap,
Refusing treasure cast into thy lap.
Wrong not thy fair youth, nor the world deprive
Of that great riches Nature freely lent,
Pity it were they by niggardize should thrive,
whose wealth by waxing craves to be spent,
For which thou after justly shall be shent;
Like to some rich churl burying his pelf,
Both to wrong others, and to starve himself.
What is this vain, this idle reputation,
which to the show you seemingly respect,
Only the weakness of imagination.
which in conclusion works no effect?
Less then that, can the worshippers protect,
That only stands upon fading breath,
And has at once the being, and the death.
A fear that grew from doting superstition,
To which still weak credulity is prone,
And only since maintained by tradition,
Into our ears impertinently blown,
By folly gathered, as by error sown;
Which us still threatening, hinders our desires,
Yet all it shows us be but painted fires.
Thee let it like this Monastry to leave,
Which youth and beauty justly may forsake,
Do not the Prince of those high joys bereave,
Which happy him eternally may make,
Which sends me else thy life away to take;
For dead to him if needsly thou will prove,
Die to thy self, and buried with his love.
Rage that resumde the color of his face,
Whose eye seemde as the Basalisks to kill,
The horror of the solitary place,
Being so fit wherein to work his will,
Each good omitted, every present ill;
Which all do seem my overthrow to further,
By fear dissuaded, menaced by murder.
In this so great and peremptory trial,
With strong temptations grievously afflicted,
With many a yielding, many a denial,
Often times acquitted, oftentimes convicted,
Whilst fear before me lively stands depicted,
And at the instant by a little breath,
Gives me my life, or sends me unto death.
When now my soul that gathred all her powers,
Which in this need might friendly give her aid,
The resolution of so many hours,
whereon herself she confidently staide;
(In this distress their help together laid,)
Making the state which she maintained good,
Expeld the fear, usurping on by blood.
The which my tongue did modestly enlarge,
From those strict limits terror it confinde,
My grieved bosom sadly to discharge,
And my lost spirits did liberally vnbinds;
To my clear eyes their residence resignde,
And strongly there mine honor to maintain,
Checked his presumption with a chaste disdain.
Finding me thus inuiolably bent,
He for my death that only did abide,
Having a poison murdring by the seent,
Unto the organ of that sense applide;
which for the same when fittest time he spide,
Unto my nosthrills forcibly did strain,
Which at an instant wrought my deadly baine.
With his rude tuch (my veil disordered then)
My face discovering, whose delitious check
Tinckted with crimson, fading soon again,
with such a sweetenes as made death even meek,
Seeming to him, beholding it even like
Unto a spark extinguished to the eye,
Breaks forth in fire e're suddenly it die.
And whilst thereat amazed he does stand,
wherein he such an excellency saw,
Ruing the spoil done by his fatal hand,
whom nothing else my beauty now did awe,
And from his eyes would force him tears to draw;
Of which deprived and settled even as dead,
Greeuing for me that it had none to shed.
When life retreating gently toward the heart,
(On whom cold death invasion now did make,
winning by little every outward part)
As more and more her succors her forsake,
To this last fort enforced her to betake,
To him whom sadly yet did me behold,
Thus with mild speech my grief I did unfold.
Is this the gift the King on me bestows,
which in this sort he sends thee to present me?
I am his friend, what gives he to his foes,
If this in token of his love be sent me?
His pleasure thus, it must not discontent me;
Yet after sure a proucrhe this shall prove,
The gift King John bestowed upon his love.
When all that race to memory are set,
And by their statues their atchieuements done
which won abroad, and which at home did get.
From son to sire, from sire unto the son,
Graced with the spoils that gloriously they won;
O that of him it only should be said,
This was that King, the murderer of a maid!
O keep it safely from the ears of Fame,
That none do hear of this unhallowed deed,
To him be secret and conceal his shame,
Lest after ages hap the same to reed,
And in their eyes the very letters bleed;
O let the grave my innocence hold,
Ere of a King so heinous sin be told.
Uttering my mind my sorrow to assuage,
The heavy burden of my pensive breast,
The poison now that inwardly did rage,
The present vigor forcibly expressed,
Me no way suffering to declare the rest;
Longer for him it was now no time to stay,
And death called on to hasten me away.
Thus in my closet being left alone.
Upon the floor uncomfortably lying,
The deed committed and the murderer gone,
Almost arrived at the point of dying,
Some of the sisters me by chance espying,
Calls all the rest that in most woeful plight,
Came to behold this miserable sight.
Where like a rose by an unkindly blast,
Among many buds that round about it grow,
The withered leaves improsp'rously does cast.
Yet all the rest their sovereign beauties show,
Amidst this goodly sisterhood even so;
Nipt with cold death untimely did I fade,
Whilst they about me pitteous wailing made.
When as my poor soul in her sudden flight,
Neglects the organ of each several sense,
with all that horror could the same affright,
Being disturbed in her parting hence,
Only constrained for her best defense,
Unto her spotless innocence to take her,
Which her not leaves when all the rest forsake her.
When all our pleasures are but childrens toys,
And as mere shadows presently do pass,
As years increasing, waning are our joys,
As we forget our favors in a glass,
Even as a tale of that which never was;
Death our delights continually does sever,
Virtue alone abandoneth us never.
And now my spirit thus liberally enlargde,
By gently flitting from this earthly room,
The debt to Nature faithfully dischargde,
And at the hour conditiond on my toombe;
Such is the heavens inevitable doombe,
Me Baynards Castle to the earth did bring,
D(...) again my place of burying.
Now scarsly was my breathless body cold,
But every where my tragedy was spread,
For tatling Fame in every place had told,
My resolution being lately dead,
Ruing my blood so prodigally shed,
And to my father flies with this mischance,
Which then remained in the Court of Frarce.
His loss too great to be bewailde with tears,
Nor were there found words to express his woe,
Terror it self so settled in his ears,
No more might enter, nothing out might go,
O while again should grief distract me (...)?
Enough of sorrow is already shown,
And telling his renewing of mine own.
Me t'shall suffice my fortunes to relate,
And bear the burden of my proper ill,
If I express my pitiful estate,
It Is all I ask, and I obtain my will,
For why, true sorrow needs not others skill,
It is too much the bitterness we taste,
without remembrance when the same is past.
Somesaie, the King repentant for this deed,
whenas remorce to thoughts thereof him drove,
Poorly disguised in a Pilgrims weed,
Offered his tears on my untimely grave,
For which, no doubt but heaven his sin forgave;
And did thereby my grieved spirit appease,
H^^^^ing contrite, I happly at ease.
Thus told my story, I my state devise,
To you sweet Madam, fitt'st with you to r^st,
which do my virtues daily exercise,
That be impressed in your patient breast,
By whom alone I rightliest am expressed;
For whom my praise (it grieves me) is too scant,
Whose happy name an Epethite shall want.
Then most dear Lady for a maidens sake,
To shead one tear, if gently you but daine,
For all my wrongs it full amends may make,
And be my pass to the Elizian plain,
In your chast^ eyes such power there does remain^,
As can the afflicted prosprously deliver,
Happy be they may look upon them ever.
FINIS.
The Legend of Pierce Gaueston.
FRom gloomy shadows of eternal night,
Shut up in darkness where I long did dwell,
O here behold me miserable wight,
Lastly, inuokt my tragedy to tell;
Give me then leave my sorrows to impart,
Somewhat to ease my poor afflicted heart.
Goddess of Arts and Arms, Pallas divine,
Let thy bright fawchion lend me Cipresse bughes,
Be thou assisting to this Poet of mine,
With funeral wreathes incompassing his brows,
Pitying my case when none would hear me weep,
To tell my sorrows, lays his own to sleep.
And mournefulst maiden of the sacred Nine,
That balefull sounds immoueably do breathe,
With thy swollen visage, and thy blubbred eine,
I unto thee my sad complaints bequeathe;
Matter that yields sufficient for thy glory,
If thou exactly prosecute my story.
Tell how the stars my wandering state did guide,
Th'unconstant turns of every changing hour,
Of many a low ebb, many as high a tide,
Many a smoothe calm, many a stormy shower,
The height whereto I lastly did ascend,
My strange beginning, and my fatal end.
When Edward sat upon the English throne,
Long-shankes that so victoriously did reign;
First of that name, and second unto none,
In all to knighthoode ever did pertain,
My life begun, and then begun my bliss,
Even in those days, those happy days of his.
So much did virtue gracious hearts inflame,
Promotion then not purchased with gold,
And in those times he that desired fame,
Bought it of them that it full dearly sold,
Hateful excess so much did not devour,
Laws had less force, and honesty more power.
And since that time so violently prays
Upon those ages that even holiest be,
Let me remember those more happy days,
In these sad hours my grieved eyes do see,
With greater grief that makes me these deplore,
When I do think of those that were before.
And Muse, to thee I sadly then appeal,
Since thou my life wilt need^ly have me show,
That I by thee may faithfully reveal,
Even what the most inquisitiue would know,
Whilst that my soul here bodied did abide,
In the vain world that pampred mein pride.
From Gascony our name and our descent,
Of which my father naturally was borne,
In all his wars that with king Edward went,
To him a (...)ege man and a soldier sworn:
And in his country ventred his estate.
To follow him that seemd to govern fate.
Whose trust that great king highly did employ,
And near his person took him for the same,
Who with my self, but then a little boy,
Unto the Court of famous England came;
Whereas the King for service he had done,
Made me a page unto the Prince his son.
In me what shape that man's did not excel,
Where every part such harmony did beat,
As in this model Nature seemd to tell,
It Was not perfection if it were not there,
As every age reserved his rarest feature,
Thereof to make so excellent a creature.
My looks the powrefull adamants to love,
Which unto them attracted every sight,
With which the same was fixed or did move,
As svmpathizing naturally delight,
That where my thoughts intended to surprise,
I at my pleasure conqu'red with mine eyes.
If even the best in Paintings curious art,
In some rare piece his workmanship should show,
Imag(...)on helping with her part,
vv^^n the hand had done the utmost it could do
Unto that body modelling a mind,
Such a one was I the mirror of my kind.
This was the bait was laid for Edwards love,
That bred the league of amity thereby,
That no misfortune after could remove,
when she the utmost of her force did try,
Nor death it self retained power to sunder,
Friendship seld seen, and in the world a wonder.
Here on this earth the only mean thou art,
Whereby we hold intelligence with heaven,
And it is thou that only do impart,
All good can to mortality be given;
That (...)red bo^d that never can be broken,
O word divine to be with reu'r^nce spoken!
With this sweet Prince in height of worldly bliss;
whilst Tutors care his wandering ears did guide,
I lived enjoying whatsoever was his,
who never my pleasures any thing denide,
Whose dear affection still me so attended,
As on my joys his happiness depended.
Whether that it my rare perfections were,
That won my youth such favor in his eye,
Or that the heavens to whom I seemde so dear,
On me down showered this blessing from the sky;
I cannot tell, but well it did direct,
That could produce such wonderful effect.
Thou Arc of heaven where wonders are enrouled,
O depth of Nature, who can look unto thee?
What might he be that has thy doom controuled?
Or has the key of Reason to undo thee?
Thy works divine, which thine alone do know,
Shallow man's wit, too short for things below.
The soul her liking subt'ly does epsy,
In the high power that is to her assignde,
By the clear sight discovering through the eye,
The thing agreeing aptliest with her kind;
And by each motion quickly apprehends,
That which it self past humane sense extends.
This Edward in the April of his age,
Whilst yet the crown sat on his fathers head,
Like that great love with his raped Phrigian page,
Me with Ambrosiall delicacies fed;
He might command that was the Sovereigns son,
And what I said, that only must be done.
My will a law autentically past,
My yea by him was never crossd with no,
Who in affection chained was so fast,
He as my shadow still with me did go,
To me this Prince so pliant was in all,
Still as an echo answering to my call.
My smiles his life, his heaven was in my sight,
And his delight confinde by my desire,
Who from my clear eyes borrowed all his light,
As pal(...)i would Cinthia from her brothers fire;
My cheek the pillow where he laid his head,
My brow his book, my bosom was his bed.
Like fair Idalia bent to amorous sportes,
With young Adonis in the wanton shade,
Figuring her passions in as sundry sorts,
As he to her indeerements to persuade
Eithers affections happily to move,
With all the tender daliances of love.
The table (thus) of our delight was laid,
Scru'd with what dainties pleasure could devise,
And many a Siren music sweetly played,
(O that youth had us wherewith to suffice)
whilst we on that vnsatiately do feed,
Which our confusion afterward did breed.
For still I spurd his violent desire,
Holding the reins wherewith he ruled the sun,
My blandishment the fuel to the fire,
In which to fry already he begun;
waxing his wings taught him Art to fly,
Who on his back might bear me through the sky.
Whilst the vain world upon us still did win,
Inticde his flatteries steadfastly to trust,
Loosing the clew which led us safely in,
Are lost within this Labyrinth of lust;
For when the flesh is nussed once in vice,
The sweet of sin makes hell a paradise
Who thy deceits vile world yet ever told,
In thee what is that is not extremely ill?
A shop where poison's only to be sold,
whose very entrance instantly does kill,
where all deformed wickedness do dwell,
And all thy ways guide head long into hell.
The King that saw his hopeful son betraide,
That like young Phaeton ventred on the skies,
Perceived his course with danger hardly staide,
(For he was grave and prouidently wise;)
That wanting skill to master youths desire,
Might by misguidance set his throne on fire.
This was a corsiue to King Edwards days,
That without ceasing fed upon his bones,
That in the day bereaved him of his case,
Breaking his night-sleepe with unquiet groans;
That did depresse and burdened him down,
More then the weight that sat upon his Crown.
When now their judgment that severely tried,
The matter whence this malladie first grew,
Likewise must now a remedy provide,
To prevent the peril likely to ensue,
The cause must end e're the effect could cease,
Else (well) of one, there many might increase.
When such in Court my opposites as were,
On all advantage that could wisely play,
Who did to me inuetterate malice bear,
That for their purpose found so fair a way;
On this their forces instantly did ground,
My name and fame perpetually to wound.
And the time fit for venting their untruth,
Me into hate more forcibly to bring,
Stick not to charge the looseness of my youth,
To Offend even in th'vnnaturalest thing,
And old fore-passed outrages awake,
With all that me contemptible might make.
Wherefore the Prince to priuacie bestowed
In Realms remote I banished to rome,
Censured of all men fitst to be abroad,
That had betraide my honest trust at home,
A diudg'd to die, if after I were found,
The day prescribed upon the English ground.
So much astonished with the sudden blow,
That I became insensible of pain,
Until awaked with sharpness of my woe,
I saw the wound which open did remain;
By which my joys still fainted more and more,
No hope at all me ever to restore.
Even as a Turtle for her faithful make,
Whose youth her dear virginity enioyde,
Sits shrouded in some solitary brake,
With melancholy pensiveness annoide;
Thus without comfort sit I all alone,
From the sweet Prince infortunately gone.
My beauty once which sdainde the summers sight,
Beaten with bleake and chilly winter storms,
Those tender limbs must travel day and night,
So often hugged in Edwards princely Arms;
Those eyes often viewing pleasure in her pride,
With fearful objects every way supplide.
And whilst these storms me strangely thus did toss,
where I my self confined yet in France,
Thwarted the while with many a grievous cross,
Inseperables to my sad mischance;
Others that stem'd the current of the time,
By which I fell, proved afterward to clime.
Camelion-like the world does alter hue,
And as false Proteus puts on sundry shapes,
One change scarce gone, another does ensue,
This filled, that likewise for promotion gapes;
Thus did they swarm like Bees about the brim,
Some drowned, and some as dang'rously swim.
And some on whom the higher powers looked fair,
Yet of the season little seemde to vaunt,
For there were clouds hung in the troubled air,
which showed something to their desire did want,
That forced them stoop which otherwise would fly,
Whilst with much care they fading honor buy.
When restless Time that never turns again,
Whose winged feet are sliding with the sun,
By the fleet hours attending on his train,
His resolution fatally begun;
In a just course eftsoone to bring about,
That which long since the wiser sort did doubt.
For whilst the King does seriously attend,
His long-hop'd voyage to the Holy Land,
For which his subjects mighty sums did lewd,
Even whilst this buisnes only was in hand;
All on the sudden happily does fall,
The death of Edward quickly altered all.
Should I assay his virtues to report,
To do the honor due unto his name,
My mean endeavors should come far too short,
And I thereby should greatly wrong the same;
But leave it to some sacred Muse to tell,
Upon whose life a Poets pen might dwell.
His princely body scarsly wrapped in lead,
Before his mournful obsequies were done,
But that the Crown was set on Edwards head,
With whom too soon my happy days begun;
After black night like brightness of the day.
All former sorrows vanished away.
When now Carnaruan calls within a while,
Whom Edward Long-shanks hated to the death,
He whom the father lately did exile,
Is to the son as precious as his breath,
What the old inscribed the younger forth did blot,
Kings wills performed, and dead men's words forgot.
When the wind wafts me to that happy place.
And soon did set me safely on that shore,
From whence I seemde but banished for a space,
That my return might honored be the more;
Unto this new King happily to leave me,
Whose princely arms were ready to receive me.
Who would have seen how that kind Roman dame.
O^e-come with joy did yield her latest breath,
Hersonne returning laden with such fame,
When thankful Rome had mourned for his death,
Might here behold her personated right,
When I approached to the Princes sight.
My Jove now Lord of the Ascendant is,
In an aspect that promisde happy speed,
Whilst in that luckie influence of his,
Some praisde the course wherein I did proceed;
Yet it to some prodigiously appears,
Telling the troubles of ensuing years.
When like to Midas all I touched was gold,
Powered as it was once down into Danaes lap,
For I obtained any thing I would,
Fortune had yet so lotted out my hap;
The chests of great men like to Oceans are,
To whom all floods by course do still repare.
The Isle of Man he first unto me gave,
To show how high I in his grace did stand,
But fearing me sufficient not to have,
I next received from his bounteous hand,
Fair Wallingford that anciently had been,
The wealthy dower of many an English Queen.
The sums his father had been leuying long,
By impositions for the war abroad,
Other his princely benefits among,
At once on me he bounteously bestowed;
When those which saw how much on me he cast,
Soon found his wealth sufficed not his waste.
He gives me then chief Secretaries place.
Thereby to train me in affairs of state,
And those high rooms that I did hold to grace,
Me Earl of Cornwall franckly did create;
And that in Court he freely might partake me,
Of England Lord high Chamberlain did make me.
And that he would more strongly me alie,
To back me against their insolent ambition,
Does his fair Cosen unto me affie,
A Lady of right virtuous condition,
which his dear sister prosperously bare,
To the Earl of Gloucester blood-ennobled Clare.
O sacred bounty, mother of content,
Fautresse and happy nourisher of Arts,
That give success to every high intent,
The Conqueror of the most noblest hearts:
High grace into mortality infused,
Pity it is that e're thou wast abused.
When those that did my banishment procure,
Still in my bosom hated did abide,
And they before that could me not endure,
Are now much more impatient of my pride;
For emulation ever did attend,
Upon the great, and shall unto the end.
And into favor closly working those,
That from mean places lifted up by me,
And factious spirits being fittest to oppose,
Them that perhaps too powerful else might be;
That even against envy raised by my hand,
Me must uphold to make themselves to stand.
And since the frame by fortune so contrived,
To give protect to my ambitious ways,
Urging thereby their hate to me derived,
From those hie honors (...) upon me lays,
Drawing the King my courses to partake,
Still to maintain what he himself did make.
Thus does my youth still exercise extremes,
My heed fond rashness to forerunne my fall,
My wit mere folly, and my hopes but dreams,
My council serves my self but to inthrall,
That me abused with a vain illusion,
When all I did intending my confusion.
And now the King, to hasten his repair,
Himself by marriage highly to advance,
With Isabel a Princess, young and fair,
As was her father Philip king of France,
When now the more to perfect my command,
Leaves unto me protection of the land.
My power confirmed so absolute withal.
That I drank pleasure in a plenteous cup,
when there was none me to account to call,
All to my hands so freely rendered up,
That earth to me no greater bliss could bring,
Except to make me greater than a King.
When being now got as high as I could clime,
That the vain world thus bountifully blessed,
Franckly embrace the benefit of time,
Fully t'enioy that freely I possessed;
Strongly maintaining he was worse than mad,
Fondly to spare, a Princes wealth that had.
Their counsels when continually I crosst,
As scorning their authority and blood;
And in those things concernd their honor's most,
In those against them ever most I stood:
And things most public privately extend,
To feed my riot that had never end.
When lastly Fortune like a treacherous foe,
That had so long attended on my fall,
In the plain path wherein I was to go,
Lays many a bait, to train me on withal;
Till by her skill she cunningly had brought me,
Unto the place where at her will she caught me.
The mighty business falling then in hand,
Triumphs ordained to welcome his return
(Before the French in honor of the land)
with all my power I labourd to adiourne;
Till all their charge was lastly over thrown,
who likde to have seen no glory but mine own.
Thus every thing me forward still does set,
Even as an engine forcing by the slight,
One mischief thus a second does beget,
And that does lead the other but to right;
Yet every one himself employing wholly,
In their just course to prosecute my folly.
Which when they found how still I did retain,
The Ambitious course wherein I first began,
And lastly felt, that under my disdain
Into contempt continually they ran;
Take arms at once to remedy their wrong,
which their cold spirits had suffered but too long.
Me boldly charging to abuse the King
A wastefull spender of his needful treasure,
A secret thief of many a sacred thing,
And that I led him to unlawful pleasure,
That never did in any thing delight,
But what might please my sensual appetite.
That as a scourge upon the land was sent,
Whose hateful life the cause had only been,
The State so universally was rent,
Whose ill increasing every day was seen;
I was reproached openly of many,
Who pitti'd none, not pitti'd now of any.
And for opposde by men of greatest might,
(The King my danger that had wisely waide)
That did pursue me with such deadly spite,
Me into Ireland secretly conuaide,
Until he might my peace again procure,
And his own safety better might assure.
As one whose house remedilesly burning,
Seeing his goods now heapt together lost,
His grief no whit disburthen'd by his mourning,
Taking some one thing that he loves most,
To some remote place does with that retire,
Leaving the rest to the mercy of the fire.
Yet he so much that still did me esteem,
That ever strove to cover my disgrace,
To make my absence otherwise to seem,
And to the world to bear a fairer Face,
Lest my exile suggested by their hate,
Might overthrow my liuelihoode and estate.
By their near council that were him about,
His Deputy of Ireland does me make,
And causde it each-where to be given out,
My journey thither instantly to take,
To stop their mouths that gladly would embrace,
Any report might tend to my disgrace.
There lived I in that honorable sort,
As to my high place purchased renown;
with no less bounty managing a court,
Then hourly craved th'reuenues of a Crown;
To me his love such sovereignty did bring.
That though he raignde, I absolutely King.
Few weeks there went but some the channel crosst,
With some such present Princes hold in price,
Some jewel that him infinitely cost,
Or sumptuous roabe of excellent devise;
When they that saw what he upon me cast,
Found that his treasure long time could not last.
And since the flood me followed in this wise,
His fulness I as amply entertain,
It might show folly to be found precise,
That to refuse which fell as did the rain,
Such as no age before did ever win,
And since his being, yet has never bin.
When now th'abated Baronry that found,
The cunning used for covering of my flight,
That me but shifted to a surer ground,
On which so vainly they bestowed their might;
Perceived far off where greater peril rose,
Then they could find how rightly to dispose.
Like those that strive to stop some violent sourse,
whose plenty Nature planted not for bounds,
Climes above all the opposers of his course,
Or let at large the neighboring plain surrounds,
That in it self s'enated is blessed,
That will the more be, more it depressed.
And fearing now the force I had abroad,
who knew the way the Irish hearts to win,
Fitter by far at home to be bestowed,
(And to the State more safety found therein)
where though my riots they could not prevent,
Yet might survey the giddy course I went.
Whereof they scarsly entertained the thought,
And did thereto but seemingly descend,
But that his love immediately it caught,
Nor cares he by it what they do intend;
Plot what they could, so he thereby might gain him,
That with delight, which still did entertain him.
O how thy presence majesty commands!
That so severely humbles every eye,
whose only self acts more than many hands,
In thee such virtue secretly does lie,
Having about thee the high power of fate,
Art both emperious over love and hate.
He that occasion never did neglect,
That aught unto my happiness might win,
My buisnes did so carefully effect,
That ever was so fortunate therein,
As he to pass does prouidently bring,
Before deemde so impossible a thing.
And Messengers immediately are sent,
Me into England instantly to call,
Authorizde by the general consent,
Although not likde of inwardly of all;
Yet, it was sufficient that it freedom gave me,
But to be there where he desirde to have me.
My sails now swelling with a prosperous wind,
The boisterous seas do homage to mine eyes,
That much above their usual course are kind,
All lowering clouds abandoning the skies,
Nothing discerned that any whit might fear me,
Fortune herself sittes at the helm to steare me.
What time the King his progress then did take,
That part of Wales pretending now to see,
For which he forward instantly did make,
which was (indeed) there but to meet with me,
with all the favor that he could devise,
To give me honor in the public eyes.
Where for my landing long he did provide,
That ^ought might want intending my delight,
And at each place as leasurely we ride,
Did (...) with some pleasing sight,
And unto L^ndon to the pompous Court,
Bring, me in the magnificentest sort.
Which proved but spurs to my vntamde desire,
Giving the (...) to my (...)ious will,
^ho let me forth unto my full ca^^re,
On places slippe^y, and my manage ill,
Small my forefight, and overmuch my haste,
That me alas infortunately cast.
The Princes ear, that having at command,
That who would aught have, me must entertain,
And yet before it passde my gripple hand,
Got the greatst share unto my private gain;
Nor card I what from any I did wring,
Many thereby too much impouerishing.
And daily begging of Monopolies,
Taking the lands belonging to the Crown,
(...) ^ence transporting those commodities,
(...) England useful coming of her own,
And basely selling offices (ere then)
The due reward of best deserving men.
And being irrecouerably proud,
Held all things vile that suited not my vain,
Nothing might pass, my judgment not allowed,
A great opinion to my wit to gain,
Giving vile terms and nicke-names of disgrace,
To men that sat in honorable place.
By which brake out that execrable rage,
That long before had boiled in their blood,
And for revenge they boldly them engage,
When lastly for their liberty they stood;
And in this quarrel open Arms they take,
Or to mar all, or better it to make.
They durst affirm my mother was a witch,
And for the fact condemned burnt to be,
And I her son and rightly of her pitch,
She had bequeathed her damned Art to me;
Urging it an inseparable thing,
That I by Magic wrought upon the King.
And into France did charge me to convey,
A goodly table that was massy gold,
A relic kept at Windsor many a day,
^^at to King Arthur did belong of old;
Upon whose margent as they did surmise,
Merlin ingraued many prophecies.
And by appealing to the sea of Rome,
A Legate thence procuring to the land,
With malediction by the Churches doom,
On any one that on my part should stand:
The King suspending should he not consent,
And ratify the general intent.
Which they did but to prosperously effect,
Being so strong to counterprize his force,
The Clergy still being ready to direct
Them the best way in all their restless course,
That at the last they strongly him procure,
By solemn oath me ever to abjure.
Th'vncertaine biding of each earthly thing,
(Set out most lively in my starre-crost state)
That does remain in Fortunes mannaging,
Appearing in my variable fate
On me that frowned and flattered me so often,
Casting me down and setting me aloft.
To Flanders then my present course I cast,
Which being the near'st, lay fittest for my ease,
Safest the way the sooner it was past,
All not my friends that were abroad at seas;
Such means in France they daily do procure,
That there my self I doubted to secure.
And though I changed my habit and my name,
Because I meant to live unknown to any,
Yet swift report had so divulged my shame,
My hated life was published to too many;
In every street that as I pas'd along,
I was the talk of every common tongue.
And finding that which only I did crave,
A secret means to send unto the King,
To me certain intelligence that gave,
Of the state and course of every thing;
Who labored now (more ever then before)
Me into England safely to restore.
For which relying on my Sovereigns love,
(To whom my life had ever been so dear)
Which I then now had never more cause to prove,
Striving t'obtaine (if any mean there were;)
A dispensation for his former oath,
In their dispight that thereto seemed most loath.
Where casting many a sundry course at length,
(Being by marridge mightily alide,
And but too much presuming of my strength)
Resolved for England, come what could be^ide;
And in a ship that for the journey lay,
Thither my self did suddenly convey.
And safely landed on the wished shore.
Unto the Court me secretly betook,
Of which the King had notice long before,
And for my coming every day did look,
Wisely that plotted when I should arrive,
All helpefull means my safety to contriue.
Which soon being known whilst yet their blood was hot,
That to their strength now only were to trust,
For what before was done prevailed not,
And for my sake the King did prove unjust;
Bringing thereby whilst trifling they did stand,
Wrong to themselves, and danger to the land.
Now when the time did generally distaste,
Our lewd and inconsiderate neglect,
when those in Court that our high favors plac'de,
Give us just cause their dealings to suspect,
And they that viewed us with the pleasedst eye,
Yet at our actions sometimes look awric.
Wherefore the King enforced to provide,
A present Army trusting to his friends,
Rep^irde to York until he were supplide,
From whence for, aid he into Scotland sends,
To warlike Balioll and to Wales, from whence
He might get power to frustrate their pretense.
But they his purpose wholly intercept,
Not now to seek in any secret thing,
The marches that so vigilantly kept,
And yet renounce all malice to the King;
Only to chastise my abhorred sin,
Who had the cause of all this trouble bin.
Thus Like a ship dismembered of the sails,
Forced by the wind against the streamefull tide,
From place to place with every billow hales,
And as it haps from shore to shore does ride;
As that poor vessel rests my brittle stay,
Nearer the land, still nearest cast away.
Corsiue of kingdoms home-begotten hate,
which in no limits ever yet wast bounded,
when did thou seize even on the greatest state,
By thee that was not utterly confounded?
How many kingdom be there that do rue thee?
Happy the world was till too well it knew thee.
Thus of our succor instantly bereft,
Having but now some little force at sea,
Lastly to trust to, only us was left,
On which our hope infortunately lay;
Which he to hasten, speedily does make,
His former courses forced to forsake.
The present danger mannaging it so,
That did for aid importunately call,
Wherefore in York as farthest from the foe,
Leaves me unto the safeguard of the wall;
Till his return me further help might give,
Whom more and more he studied to relecue.
From Bedford now the Army setting on,
The Appointed randy whore they gathered head,
When they had notice that the King was gone,
Upon their way more hastily them sped,
(Me t'afflict as purposed they were,)
Whose presence else might force them to forbear.
To Skarborough immediately I post,
With the small force my fortune then did lend me,
A Fort best fitting standing on the coast,
And of all other likest to defend me,
And came the worst, resistlesse were their might.
The sea should safely privilege my flight.
But they the City lying round about,
Keep every passage with a watchful spy,
That gave them notice of my passing out,
With their light horse pursue me by and by;
Whereas upon me suddenly they came,
E're I had time to fortify the same.
Along the lands toward Oxford they convey me,
Wondering my sight as birds do at the Owl,
And by the way continually they bray me,
As hungry woolues at passengers do howl;
Each one exulting that I now was caught,
That in the land such mischief ever wrought.
And being brought to Dedington at last,
Where the Ea^le of Pombrooke wild me to be staid,
To understand (...) further that I past,
Things to my charge that secretly were laid;
And to the King he speedily had sent.
T'acquaint him with the general intent.
But the Earl of Warwick (lying but too near)
(The dog of Arden that I used to call,)
That deadly hatred still to me did bear,
And that I ever doubted most of all;
Thither repairing with a powerful band,
Ceazed upon me with a violent hand.
And unto Warwick carrying me along,
Where he had long desired me to get,
With friends and tenants absolutely strong,
Whom all the puissant Barronry abet;
Since now occasion offered them such hold,
Hasten my death by all the means they could.
North from the Town, a mile or very near,
An easy hill in public view does lie,
Blacklow then called of those that dwelled there,
Near to the ancient Hermitage of Guy;
Thither with armed bands strongly they me led,
Whereas I lastly forfa^ed my head.
My sundry passions having thus expressed,
In the sad tenor of my tragic Tale,
Let me return unto the fields of rest,
Thither transported by a prosperous gale;
I leave the world my destiny to view,
Bidding it thus for evermore a diew.
FINIS.
